# Mistake of Law: Definition and Case Law
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: mistake of law does not exculpate liability] [CONCEPTS: mistake of law, mistake of fact, legal advice, criminal liability, court decisions] [CITATION: hoc-b017-m001-mistake-of-law] [ACT: None] [CASE: Thomas v R, Strathfield Municipal Council v Elvy, Minear v Rudrum, Brooks v Roberts, Wroblewski v Starling, Ostrowski v Palmer]

Commonly known as the defence of ignorance and is not a ground for exculpation. It involves a misconception that occurs when a person with complete knowledge of the facts reaches an erroneous conclusion as to their legal effect; an incorrect opinion or inference, arising from a flawed evaluation of the facts.

A mistake of law, as distinct from a mistake of fact, does not generally exculpate the defendant's criminal liability.

Determining what is a mistake of fact and what is a mistake of law is not always straightforward. The process is further complicated when the mistake involves a combination of factual and legal elements. In *Thomas v R* (1937) 59 CLR 279, Dixon J (Latham CJ and Rich J agreeing) drawing on English authorities it concluded that such mixed mistakes should be considered mistakes of fact. However, more recently in *Strathfield Municipal Council v Elvy* (1992) NSWLR 745 at 751, Gleeson CJ held that mixed mistakes will not ordinarily constitute mistakes of fact.

In determining this issue the court may be assisted by *Minear v Rudrum* \[2001\] WASCA 10 where the Court of Appeal dismissed an appeal because the mistake was legal not factual.

In that case the appellant was the holder of a provisional driver licence and issued with a traffic infringement notice resulting in automatic disqualification. The appellant's mother managed the infringement notice on behalf of her son and made enquiries with the traffic infringement section. Here erroneous advice was received to the effect that disqualification would not take effect until the licence was collected.

The appellant relied on such advice and was charged and convicted of driving offences.

On appeal it was held that the cancellation of the licence arose automatically by operation of the law and therefore any mistake he made was one of law. It was accepted that the appellant was advised that his licence remained valid and the honest and reasonable limbs of his claim were (presumably) made out, but dismissed.

The advice he received was advice as to the law and provision of the law remained applicable irrespective of such advice.

*Brooks v Roberts*, unreported, SCt of WA, Library 970072, 17 February 1997 was followed. This involved circumstances where the court failed to impose the minimum statutory period of disqualification and a correction of sentence application was made. The application was not served on the defendant and resulted in the sentence being corrected ex parte. The defendant then drove contrary to the disqualification and it was held his belief as to the validity of his licence could have been based only on his ignorance of the law.

A similar case of *Wroblewski v Starling* \[1987\] WAR 233 was distinguished as that involved particular administrative action that had to be taken to effect the period of driver suspension.

It is submitted that in the present circumstances there was no further action required to be taken to effect the court sanctioned disqualification period. It took effect immediately and any subsequent erroneous action taken by the Registrar of Motor Vehicle, or the advantage the defendant took of that mistake, does not impact upon the validity of the sentence.

A leading case which emphasises the distinction between mistake of law and mistake of fact is the High Court decision of *Ostrowski v Palmer* \[2004\] HCA 30.

The appellant was a fisherman who had sought advice from the Western Australia Fisheries Department as to regulations applicable to rock lobster fishing. He was provided with insufficient information of which he relied upon and was found in breach of an area restriction.

The High Court found the mistake to be one of law and not fact. In particular, Hugh J at paragraph 53 held "it is irrelevant that Mr Palmer's mistake was induced by the conduct of an employee of Fisheries WA. That conduct cannot convert a mistake as to the applicable law into a mistake of fact. If a defendant knows all the relevant facts that constitute the offence and acts on erroneous advice as to the legal effect of those facts, the defendant, like the adviser, has been mistaken as to the law, not the facts".

---

# Honest and Reasonable Mistake of Fact: General Principles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: honest and reasonable mistake of fact test] [CONCEPTS: honest mistake, reasonable belief, evidentiary burden, criminal responsibility, cross-examination] [CITATION: hoc-b017-m002-honest-reasonable-mistake] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: Hindrum v Lane, Pearce v Stanton]

It is worth noting as a preliminary issue that in *Hindrum v Lane* \[2014\] Tennant J referred with approval to the comment of Rowland J in the Western Australian case of *Pearce v Stanton* \[1984\] WAR 358 (at 363) to the effect that a consideration of whether in the circumstances a belief is reasonable must also look at the policy behind the relevant statute. The section of the *Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970* which is the subject of the charge was introduced in 2005 by an amending Act. During the Second Reading Speech introducing the amending Bill, the Minister responsible noted 'the purpose of this Bill is to reform the Act to address the road safety issues associated with people who drive motor vehicles with illicit drugs in their blood, increasing the risk of road trauma on Tasmanian roads.' It is submitted this purpose should be kept in mind by a Court considering any question of honest and reasonable mistake in relation to the presence of amphetamine in a driver’s system.

The defence of honest and reasonable mistake is raised by the evidence adduced by the defendant. The defendant has no special burden when raising the defence of honest and reasonable mistake; they need only raise the evidentiary burden to satisfy its foundation. This can occur in the cross-examination of prosecution witnesses, though.

Notwithstanding common usage, it is strictly not a defence. Rather, if held, it means the prosecution has been unable to establish all of the essential ingredients of the charge.

Once raised by the defendant, the onus is on the prosecuting authority to dispel any doubt that may have been raised by the evidence that the acts performed were a result of honest and reasonable mistake. The standard is beyond reasonable doubt.

The general test is whether the defendant had an honest and reasonable mistaken belief in a certain state of things which, if that state of things existed, the defendant's actions would have been innocent.

The belief is based on both a subjective and objective test.

The court in *Hindrum v Lane* \[2014\] TASFC 5 made the point that the requirement that the belief be reasonable is a requirement that the belief honestly held by the accused person is a belief held on reasonable grounds, not that the belief itself must be objectively reasonable.

Honest (subjective)

Reasonable (mixed -- subjective attributes of the specific person in subjective context against an objective standard)

When raised, the prosecution must prove beyond reasonable doubt that the applicant did not act pursuant to an honest belief, held on reasonable grounds, in a state of facts which, if true, would render him not guilty of the offence.

The first question to be asked is whether Mr King’s assertions are sufficient to raise the issue of honest and reasonable mistake. It is submitted this is arguable, given Mr King simply made statements that could not be regarded as evidence, in the sense of admissible evidence, and those statements were untested by cross-examination and were unsupported by any other evidence. Furthermore, there was no opportunity for prosecution to test other aspects of Mr King's statements that might lead to questions about his credibility.

For example, at one stage in his statement, he referred to unknown persons at the birthday party calling Police. He also referred to his previous assistance to Police on an unidentified matter. Given there were no particulars given about either of those statements, prosecution has been unable to test their veracity.

As then Acting Chief Magistrate Daly said in the analogous case of *Barnes v Fowler* \[2016\] TASMC 8, referring to drinks being spiked, '...in order for the evidence on this issue to be sufficient there would need to be something more than the merest of possibilities of an opportunity for such an occurrence'.

---

It involves a misconception that occurs when a person with complete knowledge of the facts reaches an erroneous conclusion as to their legal effect; an incorrect opinion or inference, arising from a flawed evaluation of the facts. A mistake of law, as distinct from a mistake of fact, does not generally exculpate the defendant's criminal liability.

### Reasonableness of Belief
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Reasonableness of Belief in Defense Case Overview] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Considerations of "reasonable" belief in court decisions] [CONCEPTS: reasonable belief, subjectivity, objectivity, belief assessment, legal standards]

The word 'reasonable' in honest and reasonable doesn’t involve the hypothetical ordinary or reasonable person test; requires that the belief be that of the accused; requires that the accused's belief be objectively reasonable i.e. rational, based on reason, or capable of sustaining belief.

---

### Case Authority: Hindrum v Lane
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Hindrum v Lane Case Authority] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Insights on reasonable belief from court ruling] [CONCEPTS: Hindrum v Lane, reasonable belief, personal attributes, criminal responsibility, legal framework]

Court in *Hindrum v Lane* \[2014\] TASFC 5. The Full Court's consideration of the question of the evidentiary burden: All judgments in the Full Court made the point that **the requirement that the belief be reasonable is a requirement that the belief honestly held by the accused person is a belief held on reasonable grounds, not that the belief itself must be objectively reasonable.**

### Counters to "Reasonable" Belief
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Reasonable Beliefs Counters Overview] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal arguments challenging reasonable belief] [CONCEPTS: reasonable belief, inherent understanding, legal representation, court assertions, judicial commentary]

As to existing restrictions or sentences: Imposed in court where the defendant did appear. It was unequivocally made clear to the defendant that the assertion that the defendant did not understand the sentence is unreasonable as he was represented by legal counsel and it could be inferred if there was any doubt in his mind he would have sought clarification.

### Case Authority: Tasmania v Bell
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Tasmania v Bell Case Authority] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Mistake of fact concerning unlawful acts] [CONCEPTS: Tasmania v Bell, mistake of age, supply offence, legal ramifications, court decisions]

Def was charged with supplying to a minor. Tried to claim mistake of fact as to age. No alternative charge available to get conviction for supply generally, if pross failed to win supply to minor. BLOW CJ held that the defence was not open because even if there was a mistake as to age, the act still breached the supply offence under another section.

---

### Duress Under Criminal Code Act 1924
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924] [SECTION: 20] [CITATION: Duress Definition and Elements] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal framework for duress as a defense] [CONCEPTS: duress, threat, criminal code, immediate harm, defense criteria]

**Essential Elements:** (***Hurley and Murray \[1967\] VR 129)***

1) There must be a threat of immediate death or grievous bodily harm.
2) The accused must be subject TO the threats.
3) The person making the threat must be actually present at the commission of the offence.
4) The accused must believe that the threat will be executed.
5) The offence committed by the accused must not be an 'excepted offence'.
6) The defence will not be available to the accused if he is a party to any association or conspiracy that rendered him subject to compulsion.

---

### Common Law Regarding Duress
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Common Law Duress Overview] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Explanation of common law perspective on duress] [CONCEPTS: duress, common law, onus, criminal responsibility, threat]

Where there is evidence of duress the onus denying its presence or its influence should rest on the prosecution. That the conduct was not the product of duress then becomes one of the absence of duress in the appropriate sense and one of the "essential elements of criminal responsibility".

---

<!-- block_018 master -->

# Express and implied threats
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: threat must conform with effective duress requirements] [CONCEPTS: duress, implied threats, harm, criminal act, necessity] [CITATION: hoc-b018-m001-express-implied-threats] [ACT: None] [CASE: The King v Anna Rowan]

The necessity for the accused to be subject to a threat of infliction of the relevant form of harm if the accused fails to commit the acts that constitute the offence charged has been a consistent feature of the consideration of the common law of duress by intermediate courts of appeal in the common law jurisdictions in this country.

The nature of the requisite threat is illustrated by the facts of *R v Dawson* and *R v Lorenz*. In *Dawson*, the accused escaped from prison but led evidence that he did so because he feared for his life after receiving threats from other prisoners. The Full Court held that duress was not open because the threats were not made on the basis that, if he did not escape, he would be subject to violence. In *Lorenz*, the accused robbed a supermarket employee with a knife after her de facto partner threatened to kill her if she did not obtain enough money to re-register his car. The accused was pregnant at the time. After noting *Hurley* and *Dawson*, Crispin J held that the defence of duress was not available as the accused's de facto partner did not direct her to commit the robbery.

However, neither *Dawson* nor *Lorenz* suggests that a threat to inflict the relevant form of harm if the accused fails to commit the acts constituting the charge cannot be implied. In *Hurley*, Smith J postulated the scenario of armed men taking possession of a house at gunpoint and sending the accused to commit a crime on their behalf while retaining a hostage such that "the threat to the hostage's life, *whether it is formulated in words or left unsaid*, may be held to be, during the execution of the commission, sufficiently present and continuing, imminent and impending to found the defence of duress" (emphasis added).

The references in *Hurley* to the threat being "present and continuing, imminent and impending" do not require that all the conduct relied upon as giving rise to the threat be engaged in immediately prior to the crime being committed. Instead, those statements should be taken as conveying a requirement that the threat conform with the description of the equivalent aspect of duress noted in other cases, namely that the threat be "operative" or "effective" at the time the accused committed the acts that constitute the offence charged. The relevant threat does not have to immediately precede the accused's criminal act if there was no reasonable opportunity to avoid committing the offence in the meantime (that being the issue raised by element (viii) in *Hurley*).

# Duty to declare basis of alleged criminal liability
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: obligation of Crown to declare basis of liability] [CONCEPTS: criminal liability, Crown Prosecutor, principal, accessory, evidence] [CITATION: hoc-b018-m002-duty-to-declare-liability] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [CASE: R v Tangye]

The obligation of the Crown Prosecutor in opening the Crown case is not merely to outline the facts which the Crown proposes to establish in evidence. It is also to indicate, in conceptual terms, the nature of the Crown case. This is to assist both the trial judge and counsel for the accused, more so than the jury. It is essential that any doubt about the nature of the Crown case, conceptually, be removed at that early stage. If it is not done at that stage, or if there had been some change in its nature since the case was opened, it is vital that it be identified with some precision, in the absence of the jury, before counsel commence their final addresses. It becomes very difficult for the judge sensibly to make alterations to directions already given once it is learnt that the issues are different to those which had been assumed to exist.

A person who contributes to the commission of a crime/offence may in certain circumstances be charged as a principal to that crime/offence - if it can be shown that the contribution was done or made with knowledge, willingness and a desire to assist. If the offence constitutes a crime then such a person is charged pursuant to section **3 of the Criminal Code, 1924**:

**3. Which parties to crimes to be deemed principals in the first degree**

(1) Where a crime is committed, each of the following persons is deemed to be a party to, and to be guilty of, the crime, and may be charged with actually committing it:

(a) every person who actually commits the crime;

(b) every person who does any act or makes any omission for the purpose of enabling or aiding another person to commit the crime;

(c) every person who abets another person in committing the crime;

(d) every person who instigates any other person to commit the crime.

(2) Any person who instigates another to do any act or make any omission of such a nature that, if he had himself done the act or made the omission, the act or omission would have constituted a crime on his part, is guilty of the same crime as if he had himself done the act or made the omission; and may be charged with himself committing that crime.

If the offence involves a simple offence then such person is charged pursuant to section **73 of the Justices Act, 1959**:

**73. Accessories**

(1) Subject to any contrary intention in the Act creating the offence, where a simple offence is committed, each of the following persons is deemed to be a party to, and to be guilty of, the offence, and may be charged with actually committing it, namely:

(a) a person who actually commits the offence;

(b) a person who does any act or makes any omission for the purpose of enabling or aiding another person to commit the offence;

(c) a person who abets another person in committing the offence;

(d) a person who instigates another person to commit the offence.

(2) A person who instigates another person to do an act or make an omission of such a nature that, if he had himself done the act or made the omission, the act or omission would have constituted a simple offence on his part, is guilty of the same offence as if he had himself done the act or made the omission, and may be charged with himself committing the offence.

(3) A person who is alleged to have instigated, aided, or abetted the commission of a simple offence may be convicted upon a complaint charging him with having committed the offence, or upon a complaint charging him with having instigated, aided, or abetted, as the case may be, the commission thereof.

# Aiding and abetting in criminal law
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: legal distinctions between aiding and abetting] [CONCEPTS: aiding, abetting, mens rea, encouragement, intention] [CITATION: hoc-b018-m003-aid-abet-distinction] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [CASE: Giorgianni]

'Aid' is different to 'abet', but they tend to be used interchangeably in many conversations. To 'aid' in the commission of a crime involves a conscious positive decision to act in a way which is designed, and which will in fact enable another to commit the crime or offence. Abet still requires knowledge of what is to transpire, but describes an equally conscious positive decision to be present and supportive in a manner that encourages the other to proceed with that which is proposed.

It is a well-established fact that "merely standing by while a crime is committed will not, of itself, establish guilt" - Gray (1917) 12 Cr App R 244 as cited by Nettlefold J at 4 in *R v Smith* unreported No 4/1979. This case, which dealt with a common law situation of a supervisor directing a driver to drive a defective vehicle leading to a crash, makes clear that parties who are said to aid, abet or instigate an offence must have full "mens rea" (knowledge of what constitutes the offence) and intend by that contribution, support or urging to enable or facilitate the commission of the offence by the principal.

Requirements for liability include:

(i) The commission of the offence by the principal offender;

(ii) That the defendant was present at the time when the offence was committed;

(iii) The defendant knew all the essential facts or circumstances that must be established by the prosecution in order to show the offence was committed by the principal offender;

(iv) That, with that knowledge, he intentionally assisted or encouraged the principal to commit that offence. His intention to assist must be based on that knowledge.

# Criminal liability for common purpose and its implications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: common purpose in prosecution of offences] [CONCEPTS: common purpose, liability, subjective test, offences, mens rea] [CITATION: hoc-b018-m004-common-purpose-liability] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [CASE: Shaw v Tasmania]

Where two or more persons form a common intention to prosecute an unlawful purpose in conjunction with one another, and in the prosecution of such purpose a crime is committed of such a nature that its commission was a probable consequence of the prosecution of such purpose, each of them is deemed to have committed the crime. We have examined what occurs when persons are principal offenders, or are deemed to be principal offenders because they have aided, abetted or instigated an offence, but what happens when one offender out of a group commits a crime that wasn't planned? For example, if two people set out to commit an armed robbery, and in the course of that robbery one offender kills someone, is the other offender also guilty of murder? What if they didn't want the victim killed?

Section 4 of the Criminal Code deals with crimes committed in prosecution of a common purpose. This section essentially states that where two people agree to commit a crime, and in the course of committing that crime, another crime is committed by one, then the other is also guilty of that second crime if it was a probable consequence of committing the original crime. Whether the second crime is a probable consequence of the first is assessed objectively, and it is not necessary that the second offender believed that it was probable.

### Recent High Court authority on duress
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on duress] [CITATION: The King v Anna Rowan - Recent HCA Authority] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: High Court authority on the concept of duress] [CONCEPTS: duress, criminal law, High Court, threats, harm]  
The necessity for the accused to be subject to a threat of infliction of the relevant form of harm if the accused fails to commit the acts that constitute the offence charged has been a consistent feature of the consideration of the common law of duress by intermediate courts of appeal in the common law jurisdictions in this country.

### Nature of requisite threat in *R v Dawson* and *R v Lorenz*
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on duress] [CITATION: R v Dawson and R v Lorenz - Nature of Threat] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Analysis of threat requirements in duress cases] [CONCEPTS: duress, threats, *R v Dawson*, *R v Lorenz*, legal analysis]  
The nature of the requisite threat is illustrated by the facts of *R v Dawson* and *R v Lorenz*. In *Dawson*, the accused escaped from prison but led evidence that he did so because he feared for his life after receiving threats from other prisoners. The Full Court held that duress was not open because the threats were not made on the basis that, if he did not escape, he would be subject to violence. In *Lorenz*, the accused robbed a supermarket employee with a knife after her de facto partner threatened to kill her if she did not obtain enough money to re-register his car. The accused was pregnant at the time. After noting *Hurley* and *Dawson*, Crispin J held that the defence of duress was not available as the accused's de facto partner did not direct her to commit the robbery.

### Implied threats in the context of duress
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on duress] [CITATION: Implied Threats in Duress Context] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Discussion on implied threats and duress] [CONCEPTS: implied threats, duress, legal interpretation, *Hurley* case]  
Neither *Dawson* nor *Lorenz* suggests that a threat to inflict the relevant form of harm if the accused fails to commit the acts constituting the charge cannot be implied. In *Hurley*, Smith J postulated the scenario of armed men taking possession of a house at gunpoint and sending the accused to commit a crime on their behalf while retaining a hostage such that "the threat to the hostage's life, whether it is formulated in words or left unsaid, may be held to be, during the execution of the commission, sufficiently present and continuing, imminent and impending to found the defence of duress".

### Continuity of threats in duress
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on duress] [CITATION: Continuity of Threats for Duress] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Analysis of continuous threats in duress claims] [CONCEPTS: duress, continuous threats, criminal liability, legal interpretation]  
The references in *Hurley* to the threat being "present and continuing, imminent and impending" do not require that all the conduct relied upon as giving rise to the threat be engaged in immediately prior to the crime being committed. Instead, those statements should be taken as conveying a requirement that the threat conform with the description of the equivalent aspect of duress noted in other cases, namely that the threat be "operative" or "effective" at the time the accused committed the acts that constitute the offence charged. The relevant threat does not have to immediately precede the accused's criminal act if there was no reasonable opportunity to avoid committing the offence in the meantime.

### Crown Prosecutor's obligations in *Tangye*
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on Prosecutor's obligations] [CITATION: Tangye (1997) - Crown Prosecutor's Obligations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Obligations of the Crown Prosecutor in cases] [CONCEPTS: Crown Prosecutor, obligations, case law, prosecution duties]  
The obligation of the Crown Prosecutor in opening the Crown case is not merely to outline the facts which the Crown proposes to establish in evidence. It is also to indicate, in conceptual terms, the nature of the Crown case. This is to assist both the trial judge and counsel for the accused, more so than the jury. It is essential that any doubt about the nature of the Crown case be removed at that early stage.

### Forms of criminal liability under the Criminal Code
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section 3] [CITATION: Forms of Criminal Liability in Criminal Code] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Criminal liability and forms under the law] [CONCEPTS: criminal liability, Criminal Code, principal offender, accessories, legal doctrine]  
A person who contributes to the commission of a crime/offence may in certain circumstances be charged as a principal to that crime/offence - if it can be shown that the contribution was done or made with knowledge, willingness and a desire to assist. If the offence constitutes a crime then such a person is charged pursuant to section 3 of the *Criminal Code, 1924*.

### Aid or abet explained with case law
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on aiding and abetting] [CITATION: Aid or Abet - Legal Explanation] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Explanation of aiding and abetting in criminal law] [CONCEPTS: aid, abet, criminal liability, legal analysis, case law]  
"Aid" is different to "abet", but they tend to be used interchangeably in many conversations. To "aid" in the commission of a crime involves a conscious positive decision to act in a way which is designed to enable another to commit the crime or offence. Abet still requires knowledge of what is to transpire, but describes an equally conscious positive decision to be present and supportive in a manner that encourages the other to proceed.

### Requirements for aiding a principal offender
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on aiding principle offender] [CITATION: Requirements for Aiding a Principal Offender] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Key requirements for establishing aiding liability] [CONCEPTS: aiding, requirements, criminal law, principal offender, liability]  
Requirements to establish aiding a principal offender:
1. The commission of the offence by the principal offender;
2. That the defendant was present at the time when the offence was committed;
3. The defendant knew all the essential facts or circumstances that must be established by the prosecution in order to show the offence was committed by the principal offender;
4. That, with that knowledge, he intentionally assisted or encouraged the principal to commit that offence.

### *Truman v Buxton* case notes on aiding
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on aiding and abetting] [CITATION: Truman v Buxton - Case Notes on Aiding] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Case analysis on aiding in crimes] [CONCEPTS: Truman v Buxton, aiding, abetting, case analysis, legal principles]  
The defendant was convicted of stealing as an aider by being in company with males who attended two bottleshops whilst they stole alcohol. The conviction was appealed by defence counsel. Tennet J stated that aiding requires an active step to enable purposes of crime and emphasized that mere presence may not constitute aiding unless further actions are clearly identified.

### Common purpose analysis in crimes
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code 1924] [SECTION: Section on common purpose] [CITATION: Common Purpose in Criminal Code] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Discussion on common purpose in criminal liability] [CONCEPTS: common purpose, criminal liability, Code vs Common Law, accomplice liability]  
Section 4 of the *Criminal Code* deals with crimes committed in prosecution of a common purpose. This section essentially states that where two people agree to commit a crime, and in the course of committing that crime another crime is committed by one, then the other is also guilty of that second crime if it was a probable consequence of committing the original crime.

<!-- block_019 master -->

# Joint Criminal Enterprise: Common Law Principle and Definitions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: joint criminal enterprise liability requires understanding to commit crime] [CONCEPTS: joint criminal enterprise, abets, criminal liability, common law, encouragement] [CITATION: hoc-b019-m001-joint-criminal-enterprise] [ACT: None] [CASE: Shaw v Tasmania]

Can find them guilty where satisfied beyond reasonable doubt that the parties had reached an understanding to commit that crime, and that it was committed in accordance with that understanding, in which case each was responsible for what the other one did.

Definition of abets:

*In our s 3(1)(c), "abets" means "encourages": It is possible for a person to abet the commission of an offence merely by being present at the place where it is committed: Encouraging the commission of an offence in that way has been regarded in Queensland as aiding the commission of the offence.*

A person who intentionally assists the offender who commits a crime would be guilty of that crime under s 3(1)(b) of the Code as a "person who does any act or makes any omission for the purpose of enabling or aiding another person to commit the crime." A person who intentionally encourages an offender to actually commit a crime can be guilty by virtue of s 3(1)(c) as a "person who abets another person in committing the crime."

A person who, at common law, would be regarded as a party to a joint criminal enterprise, but who does not actively take part in that enterprise, might incur criminal liability as an abettor pursuant to s 3(1)(c). A person who is merely present at the scene of a crime will incur criminal liability as an abettor if he or she intends, by mere presence, to encourage the offender and if the offender is in fact encouraged by that person's mere presence: *Hutt v The Queen* (above) at [51] per Underwood J (as he then was), with whom Cox and Crawford JJ (as they then were) agreed.

In our view it is clear that the words "every person who actually commits the crime" in s 3(1)(a) must be given their ordinary literal meaning, and must not be interpreted as including every person present when a crime is committed who has reached an understanding or arrangement with a person who actually commits the crime that it will be committed. In other words, s 3(1)(a) is not to be interpreted as somehow restating the common law as to the criminal liability of parties to joint criminal enterprises. The Crown's contentions as to the scope of s 3(1)(a) must be rejected. The learned trial judge directed the jury on an erroneous basis.

# Criminal Responsibility Under Section 4
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: criminal responsibility requires mutual embarkation on a crime] [CONCEPTS: mutual embarkation, criminal responsibility, objective test, common unlawful purpose, probable consequences] [CITATION: hoc-b019-m002-criminal-responsibility] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924] [CASE: Shaw v Tasmania]

Not so limited as to apply only to offence b when intent was to commit offence a. "a" can be said to be a probable consequence of intending to commit "a"!

The foundation of criminal responsibility under s 4 is the "mutual embarkation on a crime." The section establishes the ambit of criminal responsibility by participants while carrying out a common unlawful purpose. In contrast to the common law, the Code defines this ambit by requiring a connection of objective probability between the common purpose and the committed crime.

*Interpreted in this way, criminal responsibility will arise when:*

*(a) two or more persons form a common intention to prosecute an unlawful purpose in conjunction with one another, and*

*(b) a crime is committed in the prosecution of such unlawful purpose, and*

*(c) the crime committed is of such a nature that its commission is a probable consequence of the prosecution of the unlawful purpose.*

Provided the requisite connection is established between the unlawful purpose and the crime, there is nothing in the text of the provision which suggests that its operation should be limited by excluding crimes which are included within the intended scope of the unlawful purpose. Obviously, the provision extends the ambit of responsibility beyond this, by applying an objective test of probable consequence. However, this prescribes the outer reach of the provision. It does not expressly or impliedly exclude intended crimes. Self-evidently, a crime which is intended will meet the objective test of "probable consequence."

The criminal responsibility of the person should be determined by application of the test prescribed by s 4 according to its terms. Under that test, a person will be responsible for any crime committed in the prosecution of the common purpose, provided that the crime is of such a nature that its commission was a probable consequence of such prosecution, including those where the test is satisfied because the crime was expressly agreed or contemplated by the common purpose. The intention of the provision is to attribute criminal responsibility to all such crimes, on the basis of their connection to the prosecution of the unlawful purpose, irrespective of the contribution actually made by the person as defined in s 3. The section defines the limits of criminal responsibility arising from the prosecution of common purpose. There is no justification for excluding crimes which fall within that ambit from attribution of criminal responsibility.

As already noted, a consequence of the wider interpretation is the potential for considerable overlap between ss 3 and 4, because in cases in which the crime committed is that which is intended by the participants, criminal responsibility will almost certainly arise under both provisions. We see no difficulty either in principle or in practical application with this outcome. In fact, we would consider it to be the expected and appropriate effect of provisions which attribute criminal responsibility to those complicit in a crime on more than one basis. Certainly, under s 3 it is common for criminal responsibility to arise under more than one provision. We accept that there is a practical danger that relying on both provisions in every case to which both may apply on the facts could lead to unnecessarily complicated directions, and thereby create confusion for juries. Any such problem is capable of amelioration by the exercise of appropriate discretion on the part of the prosecution. As a matter of practice, resort should only be had by prosecutors to s 4 in cases where, on the facts, there may realistically be a need to rely on the extended ambit of the provision in order to attribute criminal responsibility, or where there is clear evidence of a preformed common unlawful purpose, which is additional to inferences drawn from the conduct of the parties in the commission of the crime. In any event, practical issues concerning the potential for overlap of the provisions will not "deny their separate utility."

### Definition of abets
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924] [SECTION: 3] [CITATION: Definition of Abets] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Explanation of "abets" under Tasmanian law] [CONCEPTS: abet, encourage, presence, crime, knowledge]

In our s 3(1)(c), "abets" means "encourages": It is possible for a person to abet the commission of an offence merely by being present at the place where it is committed: Encouraging the commission of an offence in that way has been regarded in Queensland as aiding the commission of the offence.

A person who intentionally assists the offender who commits a crime would be guilty of that crime under s 3(1)(b) of the Code as a "person who does any act or makes any omission for the purpose of enabling or aiding another person to commit the crime." ### Discussion on common intention under s 4
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924] [SECTION: 4] [CITATION: Common Intention Discussion] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Commentary on common intention as a basis for criminal liability] [CONCEPTS: common intention, unlawful purpose, criminal responsibility, probable consequence, objective test]

The foundation of criminal responsibility under s 4 is the "mutual embarkation on a crime". The section establishes the ambit of criminal responsibility by participants while carrying out a common unlawful purpose. In contrast to the common law, the Code defines this ambit by requiring a connection of objective probability between the common purpose and the committed crime.

Interpreted in this way, criminal responsibility will arise when:
(a) two or more persons form a common intention to prosecute an unlawful purpose in conjunction with one another, and
(b) a crime is committed in the prosecution of such unlawful purpose, and
(c) the crime committed is of such a nature that its commission is a probable consequence of the prosecution of the unlawful purpose.

### Section 50. Domestic discipline
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924] [SECTION: 50] [CITATION: Domestic Discipline Requirements] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal principles governing parental disciplinary actions] [CONCEPTS: domestic discipline, parental rights, reasonable force, child care, correction]

It is lawful for a parent or a person in the place of a parent to use, by way of correction, any force towards a child in his or her care that is reasonable in the circumstances.

Three principles (from Lane v James [2010] TASMC 19):
- Punishment must be moderate and reasonable.
- Must have a proper relationship to the age, physique and mentality of the child.
- The punishment must be carried out with a reasonable means or instrument.

### Attempts
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Acts Interpretation Act 1931] [SECTION: 34] [CITATION: Attempts Definition] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal definition of attempts to commit an offence] [CONCEPTS: attempts, offence, culpability, intention, crime]

**ACTS INTERPRETATION ACT 1931 - SECT 34**
Where by any Act an offence is constituted, any attempt to commit such offence shall be an offence and be punishable in the same manner as the offence constituted by such Act.

**CRIMINAL CODE ACT 1924 -- SECT 299**
Any person who attempts to commit a crime is guilty of a crime.

Charge: Attempting to commit [*specify particular crime*].

### Considerations when opposing bail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Bail Opposition Considerations] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Key considerations when opposing bail] [CONCEPTS: bail, opposition, public interest, prior criminal history, court attendance]

Save for s. 12, principle question is: ***whether there is a reasonable likelihood that the accused will be present at the hearing***. Bail is not to restrict lifestyle pending hearing or to impose punishments, but serves the purpose of non-interference with witnesses and the ***securing of attendance at trial***.
- Public interest
- Family ties
- Employment
- Property
- Prior Criminal record
- History of turning up or not turning up to court
- The probability of conviction or strength of the evidence
- Strength of the prosecution case
- Severity of the penalty that may be imposed
- Likelihood of D interfering with witness
- Whether remaining in custody will prejudice their case

# Public Interest Considerations in Bail Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: "public interest" and likelihood of re-offending] [CONCEPTS: public interest, bail, prior convictions, offences on bail, character assessment] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m001-public-interest-bail] [ACT: None] [CASE: Burton; R v Appleby & McMahon]

Where def breach bail on serious matters by committing other serious matters. The public interest is in preventing the def's very high risk (need to demonstrate there is a basis for this) from committing further serious offending. *Burton* notes that this factor shouldn't be considered with too much weight. Whether the accused has committed offences while on bail before see *R v Appleby & McMahon* (1966) 1 NSWR 35. The second consideration is the safety of the public and the security of its property "public interest". Subsidiary to this are the character and antecedents of the accused, including any record, particularly any record of offences while on bail. If the offence presently charged had been committed while on bail the strength of the case becomes a factor in this assessment.

**Offences on bail**
- Was the defendant on bail at the time of offending making it suggestive that he will continue?
- Does he have convictions for similar offending?
- Are they violent or abhorrent offences?
- Offences included breaking into people's homes at night?
- Extent of criminal history

*Victorian R v Gray* \[1977\] VR 225 at 230 has been stated that "the real point is that the commission of offences whilst on bail indicates contempt for or disregard of the system of law under which bail was granted to the offender suggesting the offender has small regard for the law and little intention of obeying its commands." 

* **Sowerby v State of Tasmania \[2024\] TASFC 7** : Magistrate messed up, applying a made-up test and determining: "On balance, I'm not satisfied that there are compelling circumstances or a carefully structured plan to secure the safety, wellbeing and interest of the affected person, so bail is refused." But SC also held that bail was appropriate. They did note, that the fact a defendant has previously been granted bail is not the point. It is a fresh consideration each time s. 12 arises.

\[15\] The appellant intends to defend the indictable assault charge. He is entitled to the presumption of innocence although in assessing the risk he may pose to Ms Williams-Jacques it was proper to take into account the possibility that the allegation may be true. There is likely to be a very substantial delay before the charge is brought to trial. Every instance of violence committed within a relationship or former relationship is to be regarded seriously. However, on the limited information available to this Court, the objective gravity of the crime alleged against the appellant and the absence of a serious record for violent offending meant that there was a real risk of injustice if he were remanded in custody, even if found guilty. Assuming conviction, it is reasonably possible that without bail he would remain in custody awaiting trial for a period well beyond any sentence of imprisonment reasonably proportionate to his crimes. Sometimes that is not an answer to the matters which s 12 requires be addressed. The provision, and the presumption against bail it contains, must be given effect according to its terms. There are very frequently occasions where protection of the safety, wellbeing and interests of an affected person or affected child requires remand of an accused person in custody for a period, sometimes a very lengthy period, even at the risk of injustice. However, in our view, this was not such a case.

\[16\] We concluded that, with the imposition of appropriate bail conditions, release of the appellant on bail would not be likely to adversely affect the safety, wellbeing and interests of Ms Williams-Jacques. Whilst on bail between 27 March 2024 and 24 September 2024 the appellant did not breach the terms of his bail or the police family violence order. Nor had he done any other act which suggested a substantial risk to the complainant. The appellant spent six days in custody following his arrest on 21 March 2024 and has been in custody since his arrest on 24 September 2024. Having spent more than seven weeks in custody he must be acutely aware of the likely consequences of future breach and has a strong incentive to comply. Mr Churchill impressed us as a person who will, as a surety, act conscientiously. He has the means to meet the recognizance if called upon and will be in a good position to monitor and supervise the appellant. The appellant will have an additional incentive to comply by reason of his awareness that any breach may mean that Mr Churchill, through no fault of his own, may forfeit a substantial sum.

---

# Prior Convictions and Their Impact on Bail Decisions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: relevance of prior convictions to bail decisions] [CONCEPTS: prior convictions, prosecution case, bail, severity of penalty, criminal history] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m002-prior-convictions-bail] [ACT: None] [CASE: None]

An accused's prior convictions are relevant in two ways. Prior convictions for similar offences may show that the accused has a propensity to commit crime, and prior convictions for completely different offences may be argued to be relevant because it shows an accused's lack of respect of the law and authority. Subsequent offending (committed after the principal offence but prior to sentencing for it) is still relevant to the sentencing process. It can mitigate leniency being shown for what would otherwise seem an aberration of behaviour.

**History of turning up or not turning up to court**

**Nature and seriousness of charge**

**Strength of the prosecution case**

**Severity of the penalty that may be imposed.** This links into "he is unlikely to engage with the criminal justice system -- has little incentive to do so." 

- Is he going to jail?
- Is he likely to get a significant penalty or suffer hardship?
- What incentive does he have not to appear?? 

Sometimes an escalating course of conduct comprising of many similar offences will be enough to refuse bail. If an accused is facing a likely conviction and a long sentence, they have a strong incentive to not turn up at trial, so courts may tend to refuse bail in such cases. 

**The probability of tampering with prosecution witnesses**: If they are family, or in a significant relationship, is there a possibility the defendant will try to exert some emotional control? Probs need something tangible to point to in order to support such a contention (i.e., previous threats, or emotional abuse charges -- evidence of victim's making withdrawal statements).

---

# Personal Circumstances and Conditions Impacting Bail Decisions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: personal circumstances of the defendant in bail applications] [CONCEPTS: personal circumstances, bail conditions, suitable accommodation, surety, community ties] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m003-personal-circumstances-bail] [ACT: None] [CASE: Fisher; JCN]

Consider the *Fisher* principles. Discuss def's personal circumstances (ties to the state) -- likelihood of fleeing or avoiding jurisdiction.

- Where does he live?
- Where does he work?
- Where does his family live?
- Does he have somewhere he can reside where police can monitor him?
- Does he have appropriate accommodation?

The fewer ties he has to the area, the less likely he is to appear. 

- Social ties.
- Financial position.

---

# Relevant Rules of Evidence for Bail Hearings
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: rules of evidence applicable to bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: evidence act, hearsay, burden of proof, tendency evidence, bail hearings] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m004-evidence-rules-bail] [ACT: Evidence Act; Justices Act] [CASE: Richardson, Roger v Chatwin; Re Moles; Levy]

Only the Magistrates Court can deal with breaches. Including breaches of supreme court bail orders *Richardson, Roger v Chatwin, Damien John \[1998\] TASSC 118 (29 September 1998)*. The Evidence Act applies to bail hearings per s 4 of the Evidence Act.

**Hearsay**: EVIDENCE ACT 2001 - SECT 75

**75. Exception: interlocutory proceedings**: In an interlocutory proceeding, the hearsay rule does not apply to evidence if the party who adduces it also adduces evidence of its source. Hearsay evidence is relevant and admissible for bail purposes (*Re Moles*). The question of hearsay evidence will be one of **weight**: e.g. consider the value of an anonymous tip-off versus a witness's account of prior threats.

**Burden of proof**: Bail application is not a formal trial, therefore, evidence and burden of proof rules are not set to as high a standard (*Levy*).

**Tendency evidence**: Tendency rules do not apply to bail hearings: Per section 94 of the Evidence Act.

---

# Seeking Bail Conditions and Restrictions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: conditions for bail and the Crown’s onus] [CONCEPTS: bail conditions, crown's burden, justice interests, restrictions, surety] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m005-bail-conditions] [ACT: Justices Act] [CASE: Levy; Tasmania v Wooders]

Courts can also grant conditional bail (*Justices Act*). The onus is on the Crown to affirmatively persuade the court to exercise its discretion. The judge must be satisfied of the necessity to impose conditions and cannot impose general conditions; regard must be had to the circumstances of the case, and the imposition of any conditions must be linked to one of the primary considerations in bail applications (*Levy v Strickland*). 

A court cannot simply impose standard conditions by virtue of the offence (as in *Brown* -- the judge initially imposed "the usual condition" in relation to not driving with alcohol in his body, for defendant charged with drink driving). Rather, the court must consider the individual circumstances of the case. However, an order for bail may be made subject to such other conditions as the judicial officer thinks desirable in the interests of justice: [s 7(4)](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ba199441/s7.html). Conditions may serve a number of purposes. They may be directed not only to reducing the risk that the person admitted to bail will abscond. They may also be directed to other matters relevant to the decision to grant or refuse bail, including the risk that a person will use the opportunity of bail to continue to offend, may present a risk to a person or persons, or influence potential witnesses (*Tasmania v Wooders* \[2022\] TASSC 15).

---

# Common Conditions Imposed in Bail Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: common bail conditions imposed by the courts] [CONCEPTS: bail conditions, monitoring, compliance, surety, prohibitions] [CITATION: hoc-b020-m006-common-bail-conditions] [ACT: None] [CASE: None]

- A surety.
- Curfew.
- Requirement to report to police.
- An order to stay away from particular people (e.g. Crown witnesses, family in cases of domestic violence) or places.
- An order to live elsewhere.
- Prohibitions on driving and alcohol with tests to enforce these or a condition to hand in their driver's license.

### History of Attendance at Court
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: History of Court Attendance] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Importance of history in court attendance] [CONCEPTS: court attendance, bail compliance, history, criminal justice, defendant behavior]

### Nature and Seriousness of Charge

### Strength of the Prosecution Case
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Prosecution Case Strength in Bail Hearings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Assessing the strength of prosecution cases in bail] [CONCEPTS: prosecution strength, bail decisions, evidence weight, criminal charges, legal argument]

### Severity of Penalty Imposed
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Severity of Penalty Considerations for Bail] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Penalty severity factors in bail considerations] [CONCEPTS: penalty severity, bail implications, sentencing risk, trial attendance, judiciary considerations]

This links into "he is unlikely to engage with the criminal justice system -- has little incentive to do so"  
- Is he going to jail?  
- Is he likely to get a significant penalty or suffer hardship?  
- What incentive does he have not appear???  

Sometimes an escalating course of conduct comprising of many similar offences will be enough to refuse bail. If an accused is facing a likely conviction and a long sentence, they have a strong incentive to not turn up at trial, so courts may tend to refuse bail in such cases.

---

### Probability of Tampering with Prosecution Witnesses
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Probability of Witness Tampering Considerations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Risk of witness tampering in bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: witness tampering, emotional control, defendant behavior, family violence cases, bail implications]

If they are family, or in a significant relationship, is there a possibility the defendant will try to exert some emotional control? Probs need something tangible to point to in order to support such a contention (i.e., previous threats, or emotional abuse charges -- evidence of victim's making withdrawal statements).

### Personal Circumstances of the Defendant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Personal Circumstances Assessment in Bail Applications] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Evaluating personal circumstances for bail] [CONCEPTS: personal circumstances, ties to jurisdiction, flight risk, stability factors, bail assessments]

Consider the *Fisher* principles  

**Discuss def's personal circumstances (ties to the state) -- likelihood of fleeing or avoiding jurisdiction**  
- Where does he live?  
- Where does he work?  
- Where does his family live?  
- Does he have somewhere he can reside where police can monitor him?  
- Does he have appropriate accommodation?  

The fewer ties he has to the area, the less likely he is to appear - Social ties - Financial position

### Relevant Rules of Evidence for Bail Hearings
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001] [SECTION: s. 4] [CITATION: Evidence Rules Applicable to Bail Hearings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Overview of evidence rules in bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: evidence rules, bail hearings, magistrates court, hearsay rules, legal standards]

Only the Magistrates Court can deal with breaches. Including breaches of supreme court bail orders *Richardson, Roger v Chatwin, Damien John [1998] TASSC 118 (29 September 1998)*. The *Evidence Act* applies to bail hearings per ***s 4 of the Evidence Act***. 

---

### Hearsay Rules for Bail Proceedings
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001] [SECTION: s. 75] [CITATION: Hearsay Rule Exception for Bail] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Hearsay rules relevant to bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: hearsay, evidence admission, bail hearings, rule exceptions, legal principles]

***EVIDENCE ACT 2001 - SECT 75***

**75. Exception: interlocutory proceedings**

In an interlocutory proceeding, the hearsay rule does not apply to evidence if the party who adduces it also adduces evidence of its source. Hearsay evidence is relevant and admissible for bail purposes (*Re Moles*). The question of hearsay evidence will be one of **weight**: e.g. consider value of an anonymous tip off v witness's account of prior threats.

---

### Burden of Proof in Bail Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Burden of Proof in Bail Proceedings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Standards of proof in bail applications] [CONCEPTS: burden of proof, bail applications, legal proceedings, trial standards, judicial considerations]

Bail application is not a formal trial, therefore, evidence and burden of proof rules are not set to as high a standard (*Levy*).

---

### Tendency Evidence for Bail Hearings
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001] [SECTION: s. 94] [CITATION: Tendency Evidence Exclusion in Bail Hearings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Tendency evidence rules for bail applications] [CONCEPTS: tendency evidence, bail hearings, legal standards, evidence exclusion, judicial rules]

Tendency rules do not apply to bail hearings: Per section 94 of the *Evidence Act*.

---

### Seeking Bail Conditions/Restrictions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Imposing Bail Conditions] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Process for seeking bail conditions] [CONCEPTS: bail conditions, restrictions, court discretion, case circumstances, legal process]

Courts can also grant conditional bail (*Justices Act*). The *onus is* on the Crown to affirmatively persuade the court to exercise its discretion. The judge must be satisfied of the necessity to impose conditions and cannot impose general conditions; regard must be had to the circumstances of the case, and the imposition of any conditions must be linked to one of the primary considerations in bail applications (*Levy v Strickland*). 

---

### Common Conditions for Bail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Common Bail Conditions] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Common conditions applied to bail] [CONCEPTS: bail conditions, surety, reporting requirements, conduct restrictions, alcohol prohibitions]

- a surety  
- curfew  
- requirement to report to police  
- an order to stay away from particular people (e.g. Crown witnesses, family in cases of domestic violence) or places  
- an order to live elsewhere  
- prohibitions on driving and alcohol with tests to enforce these or a condition to hand in their driver's license  

---

### Section 12 Basic Principles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Basic Principles of Bail Under s. 12] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Bail principles in family violence cases] [CONCEPTS: bail principles, family violence, presumption against bail, court considerations, judicial authority]

The family violence creates a presumption against bail: Re S [2005] TASSC 89; (2005) 157 A Crim R 451. The onus is on the respondent to displace the presumption: *Olsen v State of Tasmania* [2005] TASSC 40.

### Wishes of the Complainant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Complainant's Wishes in Bail Hearings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Relevance of complainant's wishes in bail applications] [CONCEPTS: complainant wishes, bail decisions, domestic violence, judicial independence, court obligations]

Irrelevant basically.  
***DPP v JCN [2015] TASFC 13***  
**\[Para 20\]**  
*"There are a range of possible reasons for the behaviour of a complainant acting in support of a defendant. A court has a duty to consider the interest of persons who may be affected by family violence and if necessary protect them. By doing so it may sometimes act contrary to the wishes of the affected person."*

### Demeanor of the Defendant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Defendant Demeanor Considerations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Examining the demeanor of the defendant in bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: demeanor, behavior assessment, bail evaluations, emotional stability, courtroom behavior]

**12(2)(b) Demeanor**  
Outward behavior or bearing.  
- Have they resisted arrest?  
- Did they yell?  
- Did they flee?  
- Has the defendant subsequently made contact with the victim whilst avoiding police?  
- What were the circumstances surrounding arrest?

Example where it was a principal consideration:  
It is true, that the defendant himself turned himself in following the incident. But whilst in police custody, the defendant made serious threats to the former partner of Ms. Collins. Whist in the station, in the presence of police, the defendant stated and I quote "I will bash the shit out of him" stating that he would face the consequences once he had done so.  

While not directed at the victim, they are indicative of the defendant's demeanor. This man was prepared to make open threats to affect physical violence, even whilst in custody. The defendant himself stated to police that he believes that he suffers from mental health issues, which cause him to snap and become violent.

---

### Presence of Children During Incidents
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Presence of Children as an Aggravating Factor] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Children's presence and its impact on bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: children, family violence, aggravating factors, bail considerations, courtroom impacts]

**Consider child/ren at scene:**  
- Was there a child?  
- Were they present?  
- Could they hear?  
- Were they assaulted?  
- Were they subject to the consequences of the offending (i.e. seeing the parents in a state of distress?)  

Master, x was born on the 8th of November 2011  
- He is said to have been present at both family violence incidents  
- On both occasions witnesses have reported that he was screaming and crying  

Under section 13 sub-s (a) the court may consider the offenses to be aggravated.  
***Allen v Kerr [2009] TASSC 10, 19 Tas R 132 at [13]: Porter AJ***  
*"The reasons that the presence of children is an aggravating factor of some significance are largely self-evident. Violence witnessed by children in the domestic environment not only is distressing (usually the victim is a parent or someone in the place of a parent), but it also serves to desensitize impressionable minds to violence and to encourage the notion that resort to violence is acceptable."*

---

### Availability of Suitable Accommodation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Availability of Suitable Accommodation for Bail] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Assessing accommodation suitability in bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: accommodation suitability, bail considerations, defendant’s residence, domestic violence situations, court assessments]

A mandatory consideration of the court under s. 12(d).  
Has there been previous residential conditions on the defendant that have failed? Submit the presence at the location has not prevented breaches of the respective orders.  
Is the proposed address so close to that of the protected party as to be questionable in its utility of satisfying the court, or alleviating the mental distress of the complainant?

---

### Duties of a Surety
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Responsibilities of a Surety in Bail Proceedings] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Surety requirements in bail hearings] [CONCEPTS: surety duties, bail compliance, judicial expectations, legal obligations, risk management]

**There is a positive duty on a surety:**  
**\[14\]...** *The surety is required to take positive steps. It is not enough that the surety does nothing and states that he or she believed the accused would attend trial or abide by conditions: Mokbel v DPP [2006] VSC 487; (2006) 14 VR 405 at [42] and [54]. He or she must take all reasonable steps to ensure compliance: Re Condon [1973] VicRp 40; [1973] VR 427 at 431. Evidence that reasonable steps were taken will be a factor, although not determinative, in an application for forfeiture. It is generally not enough for a surety to say that there was no complicity or connivance in, or culpability for, the breach: Re Nevil Martin Schoenmakers v Director of Public Prosecutions (above) at 284.* **Tasmania v Wooders [2022] TASSC 15 (28 February 2022)**

---

### After-Hours Sureties
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: After-Hours Sureties Process] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Processes for after-hours surety applications] [CONCEPTS: after-hours sureties, bail processes, police coordination, defendant release, judicial requirements]

The Bench Justices are able to make orders for a recognisance but they would need to be present to agree to the order and conditions, and sign the paperwork.  

The Bench Justices are also able to make an order for a paid surety and there is an agreement in place with Tasmania Police for this payment to be facilitated. Tasmania Police release the defendant once the deposit has been made at the police station and the bail document has been signed.  

Similarly, there is an arrangement with Tasmania Police to accept surety payments after hours for orders made during business hours that are not able to be complied with until after hours and Launceston Reception Prison is notified once the payment has been made to enable the release of the defendant.

---

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [SECTION: s. 12] [CITATION: Counter Arguments Concerning Surety Appropriateness] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Evaluating the appropriateness of proposed sureties] [CONCEPTS: appropriate surety, family dynamics, bail compliance risks, judicial considerations, defendant oversight]

Family members not likely to arrest their own family to comply with bail so can argue not appropriate. 

Surety Promissory or actual  
Promissory not as much weight.  
Consider whether appropriate or not: Are they likely to arrest in case of breach or cooperate with police? Can they supervise or monitor the defendant?
