# Contra Section 12 Surety Arguments
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers the capacity of surety and supervision requirements] [CONCEPTS: surety, breach, bail, supervision, safety] [CITATION: hoc-b021-m001-contra-section-12-surety] [ACT: Family Violence Act] [CASE: R v Barrett; Queen v Badger; Tasmania v Wooders]

R v Barrett is a 1985 SA decision referring to the 1843 UK judgment of the Queen v Badger. It is a common law principle applied in the context of a presumption in favour of bail. Neither contemplated or considered the introduction of the Family Violence Act. However violent a departure from common law principles it may be, Section 12 is binding. The court must be satisfied as to the safety and well-being of the complainant. The surety's capacity to pay, alone, should the defendant act contrary to that requirement, is inherently insufficient to discharge that requirement. It is the "supervision" of a defendant by proposed surety that can satisfy the court's, and in that question, the character and antecedents of the proposed surety are not only relevant, but necessary considerations. The court is effectively placing its trust in the surety; it's entitled to consider if that trust is appropriately placed.

At para 22 of JCN, Pearce J said: *"His own home is quite close to the complainant's residence. If he is there, monitoring his movements and conduct would be very difficult. It would achieve little to reduce the risk. His mother's home is in Hobart and would increase the physical separation from the complainant. However, his presence at that home has not, in the past, prevented breach of the order. Nor does his mother's presence seem to prevent breaches. He was at his mother's house with the complainant when he was arrested. The circumstances to which I have referred also reduce my confidence in his mother's capacity as surety to monitor the respondent's behavior and deter any breach."*

At para 14 of *Tasmania v Wooders [2022] TASSC 15*, *"A person who provides a recognizance has an important role, not to be entered into lightly. He or she is, unlike the Court, in a position to supervise obedience to the order and the conditions. A surety undertakes a real and substantial obligation to ensure that the person admitted to bail appears when required and complies with the conditions and must understand the obligations of the accused and the consequences of breach."*

*Secondly, the recognizance also serves to motivate the person released on bail to comply by the assurance that, if they fail to appear or breach a condition, the person who agreed to provide the recognizance will suffer the forfeiture. The accused must know that, if he or she breaks the trust that has been placed in them by a surety, often a family member or friend, the surety will suffer: Mokbel v DPP. The object is to dissuade an accused from breach because of the adverse consequences which might otherwise flow to a surety.*

# Estreatment Applications Against Sureties
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: review of section 20 of the Bail Act] [CONCEPTS: surety, doit, application, forfeiture, conditions] [CITATION: hoc-b021-m002-estreatment-applications-against-sureties] [ACT: Bail Act] [CASE: Tasmania v Wooders]

Applications to be made under section 20 of the *Bail Act*. (Section 19 applies to defendants who deposit funds on their own behalf.) The terms of [s 20](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ba199441/s20.html) are to be distinguished from [s 19](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ba199441/s19.html) which, as I interpret it, applies to a sum deposited by a person admitted to bail with a condition imposed under [s 7(5)(a)](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ba199441/s7.html) to secure compliance with the terms of his or her own bail. In such a case, on conviction for breach, a court "must" order forfeiture of the full sum deposited unless the convicted person demonstrates "excessive hardship to that person or the dependants of that person" not relieved by allowing payment by instalments or satisfies the court that it is otherwise just to order forfeiture of part only.

#### Unfairness is a Part-and-parcel of Being a Surety
[Cited in **Tasmania v Wooders [2022] TASSC**]

In R v Crown Court at Maidstone; ex parte Lever [1995] 2 All ER 35, Hoffman LJ said at 41: *"It follows that in one sense the system has unfairness built into it. It may result in persons entirely innocent having to suffer on account of the wrongdoing of another. The courts rely upon the moral pressure which this prospect should apply to the mind of the accused. But the pressure would evaporate if judges were not willing, as a general rule, to harden their hearts against a plea of lack of culpability when it turns out that the surety's trust in the accused was misplaced."*

*As a result, even if a surety does as much as reasonably can be expected, the deterrent effect of a recognizance as it applies to the person released on bail will only work if sureties lose a substantial amount of the sum deposited or promised in the event of breach: R v Agius.*

*In general terms however, a court should take care in attributing significant weight to considerations of financial hardship. To do so would discourage proper consideration of provision of the surety in the first place, would undermine the importance of understanding the genuineness of the surety and would reduce the incentive to discourage breach.*

*The overriding consideration is maintenance of the integrity of the bail system by emphasis on the purposes to be served by provision of surety, the importance of the obligation it imposes and the consequence of breach. For that reason, the greater part of the recognizance should be forfeited.*

***R v AGIUS [2012] SASC 136 (16 July 2012)***

*Held: Alerting authorities is not in itself sufficient to deny forfeiture -- deterrence of person released on bail is an important consideration -- hardship is not ordinarily a sufficient reason to reduce forfeiture but there were special reasons in this case -- application allowed -- monetary sums estreated but amounts of forfeiture reduced.*

# Applications to Revoke Bail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: grounds for revoking bail] [CONCEPTS: revocation, application, bail, court, conditions] [CITATION: hoc-b021-m003-applications-to-revoke-bail] [ACT: Bail Act] [CASE: DPP v JCN; Maingay v Seabourne]

Court can revoke bail on own motion pursuant to section 25 of the Bail Act if there is a PG or found guilty. Arguably can do it on own motion whenever a defendant appears on an order for adjournment under 25 too, but it’s arguable. Otherwise, application to be made under s 24. Section 24 requires it to be in the prescribed manner. The bail regulations (under regulation 10) require that to be in a specific form and supported by an affidavit.

NOTE: the wording of s. 25 says they need to be present in order to make the order. There is no specific or general power under the Bail Act to issue a warrant on a respondent defendant if they don't answer to the notice of the application however.

The answer Julia came up with is recourse to r. 62 of the *Justices Rules*, arguing that it would be a necessary modification of Part 4 that "*summons*" should be read interchangeably with "*application*."

Sub 3 of rule 34 is what convinced me she was on a winner. On what other basis would a Magi have power to dismiss an application if no one turned up?

<!-- block_021 procedure -->

### Surety Arguments: R v Barrett and Queen v Badger
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: 12] [CITATION: Surety Arguments - R v Barrett] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Overview of surety arguments in relation to bail obligations] [CONCEPTS: surety arguments, R v Barrett, Queen v Badger, bail, family violence]

In discussing surety arguments, R v Barrett refers to the 1985 SA decision, which cites the 1843 UK judgment of Queen v Badger. This common law principle applies in the context of a presumption in favour of bail, though neither case referenced the introduction of the Family Violence Act. However violent a departure from common law principles it may be, Section 12 is binding. The court must be satisfied as to the safety and wellbeing of the complainant. The surety's capacity to pay alone, should the defendant act contrary to that requirement, is inherently insufficient. It is the supervision of a defendant by a proposed surety that can satisfy the court's concerns. Additionally, the character and antecedents of the proposed surety are relevant and necessary considerations, indicating the court's need to place adequate trust in the surety.

### Judicial Perspective on Monitoring by Sureties
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Judicial Perspective on Monitoring - JCN] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Judicial opinion regarding the monitoring capacity of sureties] [CONCEPTS: surety monitoring, JCN, judicial perspective, bail considerations]

At para 22 of JCN, Pearce J commented, "His own home is quite close to the complainant's residence. If he is there, monitoring his movements and conduct would be very difficult. It would achieve little to reduce the risk. His mother's home is in Hobart and would increase the physical separation from the complainant. However, his presence at that home has not, in the past, prevented breach of the order. Nor does his mother's presence seem to prevent breaches. He was at his mother's house with the complainant when he was arrested. The circumstances to which I have referred also reduce my confidence in his mother's capacity as surety to monitor the respondent's behavior and deter any breach."

### Judicial Perspective on the Role of a Surety
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Judicial Perspective on Role of Surety - Tasmania v Wooders] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Judicial opinion on the obligations of a surety] [CONCEPTS: surety obligations, Tasmania v Wooders, judicial opinion, bail compliance]

In Tasmania v Wooders [2022] TASSC 15, the court outlined the important role of a person providing a recognizance. Pearce J noted, "A surety undertakes a real and substantial obligation to ensure that the person admitted to bail appears when required and complies with the conditions, and must understand the obligations of the accused and the consequences of breach."

### Discussion of Estreatment Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: 20] [CITATION: Estreatment Applications under Section 20] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Application process under the Bail Act for estreatment] [CONCEPTS: estreatment applications, Bail Act, Section 20, surety obligations]

Applications regarding estreatment must be made under Section 20 of the Bail Act. It is important to distinguish this from Section 19, which applies when a defendant deposits funds on their own behalf. Under Section 20, on conviction for breach, a court "must" order forfeiture of the full sum deposited unless the convicted person demonstrates "excessive hardship" or that it is otherwise just to order a partial forfeiture.

### Commentary on Unfairness of Being a Surety
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Unfairness of Surety - R v Crown Court at Maidenstone] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Inherent unfairness in surety systems] [CONCEPTS: unfairness, surety obligations, R v Crown Court, judicial perspective]

As cited in Tasmania v Wooders [2022], R v Crown Court at Maidenstone [1995] stated that "it follows that in one sense the system has unfairness built into it. It may result in persons entirely innocent having to suffer on account of the wrongdoing of another. The courts rely upon the moral pressure which this prospect should apply to the mind of the accused." Furthermore, in R v Agius, the court noted that "the deterrent effect of a recognizance will only work if sureties lose a substantial amount of the sum deposited in the event of breach."

### Summary of Mitigating Factors in Estreatment Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Mitigating Factors in Estreatment] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Overview of mitigating factors in breach situations] [CONCEPTS: mitigating factors, estreatment, breach consequences, bail conditions]

Mitigating factors are considered in cases where the primary duty to attend court is complied with, but only some secondary condition is breached. In such cases, it is arguable that only a portion of the amount should be forfeited (see para 22).

### Judicial Discretion on Forfeiture of Recognizance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: 20] [CITATION: Discretion on Forfeiture - Tasmania v Wooders] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Discretion available to judges in forfeiture of bail amounts] [CONCEPTS: judicial discretion, recognizance forfeiture, bail act, Tasmania v Wooders]

The Bail Act does not expressly limit the discretion of Magistrates regarding forfeiture of the amount of a recognizance. A judge may order forfeiture of the whole amount, or such part as deemed "appropriate" (as referenced in Tasmania v Wooders [2022]).

### Applications to Revoke Bail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: 25] [CITATION: Applications to Revoke Bail] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Procedure to revoke bail under Section 25] [CONCEPTS: revoke bail, bail act, Section 25, courts]

The court can revoke bail on its own motion under Section 25 of the Bail Act if there is a finding of guilt. It is arguable that this may also occur on the court's motion whenever a defendant appears on an order for adjournment under the same section. Otherwise, an application to revoke bail must be made under Section 24, which has specific regulations for the prescribed manner and form.

### Applications to Vary Bail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: 23] [CITATION: Applications to Vary Bail] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Procedure for varying bail under Section 23] [CONCEPTS: vary bail, bail act, Section 23, applications]

An application to vary bail must be accompanied by an affidavit according to the CMagi Practice Direction 6 of 2020. The application must be made in the prescribed form and specify the conditions being varied.

### Consequences of Failure to Answer Call
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Consequences of Failure to Appear - Gandini v Tasmania Police] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Consequences of a defendant not answering a court call] [CONCEPTS: failure to appear, warrants, Gandini v Tasmania Police, court procedures]

If a defendant is called in court and refuses to accept their name, as in the case of Soc Citz, the court can issue a warrant for failure to appear (as established in Gandini v Tasmania Police [2021]).

### Court Duties in Cases of Family Violence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Bail Act 1994 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Court Duties in Family Violence Cases - DPP v JCN] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal considerations in family violence cases] [CONCEPTS: family violence, court duties, DPP v JCN, bail considerations]

A court must consider the interests of those affected by family violence, even if such actions might go against the wishes of the affected person (DPP v JCN [2015]).

### Definition of "Criminal Proceeding"
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Various] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Definition of Criminal Proceeding] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal definition under Tasmanian law] [CONCEPTS: criminal proceeding, legal definition, Bail Act, prosecutorial context]

A "criminal proceeding" means a prosecution for an offence and includes proceedings regarding bail and committal.

### Application of Part 6: Tendency and Coincidence Evidence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: 94-101] [CITATION: Tendency and Coincidence Evidence] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Rules regarding evidence of tendency and coincidence] [CONCEPTS: tendency evidence, coincidence evidence, rules, evidentiary standards]

Sections 94-101 of the Evidence Act outline the application of tendency and coincidence evidence in relation to credibility and admissibility issues.

### Mental Health Treatment Order
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Mental Health Act 2013 (Tas)] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Mental Health Treatment Order Process] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Overview of mental health treatment orders] [CONCEPTS: mental health orders, treatment order, protective custody, assessment order]

There are two types of mental health treatment orders: those arising from court processes and those for civilians. Civilians can be taken into protective custody by law enforcement for an assessment per the Mental Health Act and can undergo treatment orders subsequent to assessments.

# Mental Health Protective Custody Authority
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legislation] [TYPE: legislation] [TOPIC: police power for protective custody] [CONCEPTS: mental illness, protective custody, assessment criteria, reasonable force, entry provisions] [CITATION: hoc-b022-m001-mental-health-protective-custody] [ACT: Mental Health Act 2013] [CASE: None]

Section 17: power for police officer to take a person into protective custody if the police officer reasonably believes that --

(a) the person has a mental illness; and

(b) the person should be examined to see if he or she needs to be assessed against the assessment criteria or the treatment criteria. 

**SCHEDULE 2 - Custody and escort provisions**

**1. Interpretation**

In this Schedule --

**custodian** or **escort**, of a patient, means a police officer, MHO or authorised person who, by or under this Act, is empowered or authorised to take the patient into protective custody or under escort;

**1. General powers, &c., of custodians and escorts**

To take a patient into protective custody or under escort --

(a) the custodian or escort may enlist the assistance of any person, including, if necessary, a police officer; and

(b) the custodian or escort (and any assistant) may use reasonable force against the patient if he or she resists being taken into protective custody or under escort; and

(c) the custodian or escort (and any assistant) may use reasonable force against anyone who may try to prevent the patient from being taken into protective custody or under escort; and

(d) the custodian or escort (if necessary with any assistant) may, without warrant and doing as little damage as possible, enter any premises if the custodian or escort reasonably believes that the patient may be found on those premises; and

(e) the custodian or escort (or any assistant) may take possession of and safeguard any medication, physical aid or other thing that the custodian or escort reasonably believes is or may be necessary to the patient's health, safety or welfare; and

(f) the custodian or escort (or any assistant) may take possession of and safeguard any medication, prescription or other thing that the custodian or escort reasonably believes is or may be relevant to the patient's examination, assessment, treatment or care; and

(g) the custodian or escort may, as circumstances require, transfer physical control of the patient to another custodian or escort or to another police officer, MHO or authorised person (who then has, and may exercise, any powers of the transferring custodian or escort under this clause); and

(h) the custodian or escort is not required to be in close physical proximity to the patient during any examination or assessment.

# Contempt of Court and Case Authority Context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: contempt in face of the court] [CONCEPTS: contempt of court, judicial authority, evidentiary material, Justices Act, ex parte hearing] [CITATION: hoc-b022-m002-contempt-of-court-authority] [ACT: Justices Act] [CASE: Mansell v Hughes]

In the case of *Mansell v Hughes [2024] TASSC 28 (28 May 2024)*, the court reiterated the principle outlined in Section 25 of the Justices Act, highlighting clear authority for the proposition that something heard but not seen by a judicial officer can constitute contempt in the face of the court. This was supported by precedents such as *Registrar, Court of Appeal v Collins* \[1982\] 1 NSWLR 682 and *Fraser v The Queen* \[1984\] 3 NSWLR 212.

The procedural aspects of hearings further illuminate how courts approach cases involving the defendant's absence. For instance, it is noted that there is no strict requirement for the accused to give evidence before calling other witnesses, although this is a general practice: *RPS v The Queen* (2000) 199 CLR 620 at [8]-[9]. It was highlighted that a trial judge may insist that the defendant must be called first, which raises procedural implications regarding the accused's absence.

If a defendant does not turn up, there are options such as proceeding with an ex parte hearing under rule 31 of the Justices Rules or seeking personal service or a warrant under Rule 32. However, caution is advised when considering ex parte hearings, as the case *Woodward v Moore [2017] TASSC 63* demonstrates that ex parte hearings are not always appropriate.

The Justices Act allows for certain statutory presumptions under Rule 72D, which permits the court to treat matters as prima facie evidence without the accused present. This provides a framework that requires careful scrutiny of the case context and the nature of the complaints being presented. The section presupposes that the complaint will be in proper form, and the legal consequences are based on the content of well-structured complaints rather than unsubstantiated allegations, as discussed in *R v Hush; Ex parte Devanny* (1932) 48 CLR 487.

# Road Safety and Licensing Regulations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legislation] [TYPE: legislation] [TOPIC: restricted licences and public interest] [CONCEPTS: restricted licence, public interest, severe hardship, evidentiary provisions, alcohol dependency] [CITATION: hoc-b022-m003-road-safety-licensing-regulations] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970; Vehicle and Traffic Act 1999] [CASE: R v Stockman; Davies v Deverell; Morton v Scott]

The Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 and the Vehicle and Traffic Act 1999 outline the provisions governing restricted licences. Under Section 18(5) of the Vehicle and Traffic Act, punitive measures can accompany the granting of a restricted licence where punishment was imposed. There are statutory bars under Section 19(1A) of the Road Safety Act, which indicate that any offence committed during disqualification periods can disallow applications for returning driving privileges. 

When assessing applications for a restricted licence, the courts consider several factors, including the need for an applicant to establish "severe hardship," which must be convincingly articulated. For example, details about personal circumstances and the unavailability of alternative transport options are vital.

With regard to prior offences, the circumstances surrounding the offending behaviour significantly shape the evaluation of whether granting restricted licences aligns with public safety considerations. There is a public interest in enforcing disqualification laws, which places a significant burden on applicants to demonstrate that their request for driving rights does not undermine the community’s safety or legal integrity.

Cases such as *R v Stockman* underscore that magistrates must carefully weigh the implications of risk to public safety. Similarly, in *Davies v Deverell (1992) 1 Tas R 214*, the court reiterated that evidence presented in support of applications for restricted licences should be properly tested in cross-examination to ensure that the public interest standard governs these legal proceedings.

# Mr Black's Alternative Employment Evidence in Lane v Black
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Mr Black's failure to establish employment connection] [CONCEPTS: evidence, alternative employment, financial position, assessment, inference] [CITATION: hoc-b023-m001-mr-black-alternative-employment] [ACT: None] [CASE: Lane v Black [2013] TASMC 3]

Mr Black said that *"\... If I am unable to drive I will not be able to obtain alternative employment."* While this may be so, Mr Black did not give any evidence upon which that conclusion or opinion was based. Mr Black did not say that he had consulted with any job agency to assess the labour market for someone with his skills. There was no evidence that Mr Black had made any enquiries whatsoever in relation to alternative employment. Nor was Mr Black asked inchief or under cross-examination, any questions about this assertion. Much of the picture in relation to alternative employment and the applicant's financial position was left to be inferred instead of being the subject of precise evidence. **Tasmanian RL may not be valid interstate.**

# Misuse of Drugs Act - Power to Search
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Power to search a person or conveyance] [CONCEPTS: power to search, prescribed belief, lawful entry, detention, privacy] [CITATION: hoc-b023-m002-power-to-search] [ACT: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001] [CASE: None]

Power to search a person where there is a prescribed belief without warrant. S. 29(5) and 30 are pretty broad powers up to and including a strip search (but they must be afforded privacy).

**29(1)**

***"Prescribed belief"*** means a reasonable belief that a controlled substance or other thing in relation to which an offence under this Act has been committed is --

(a) in the possession of a person in any place; or

(b) on or in a conveyance in any place; or

(c) on an animal in any place.

Where such belief police can search and detain person or conveyance. Cannot enter private premises to search UNLESS they form the belief after having lawfully entered the premises per s 29(2A).

# Prescribed Belief Standard
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Standard of Prescribed Belief] [CONCEPTS: prescribed belief, reasonable grounds, objective test, evidence, police database] [CITATION: hoc-b023-m003-prescribed-belief-standard] [ACT: None] [CASE: Tasmania v Hodges [2022] TASSC 45]

\[28\] *"A belief is something more than mere suspicion, but less than actual knowledge based on proof. There must be grounds for the belief, but certainty and proof are not required."*

This point was made by the High Court in *George v Rockett* \[[1990\] HCA 26](http://www6.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1990/26.html); [(1990) 170 CLR 104](http://www6.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281990%29%20170%20CLR%20104) at 116.

> *\"The objective circumstances sufficient to show a reason to believe something need to point more clearly to the subject matter of the belief, but that is not to say that the objective circumstances must establish on the balance of probabilities that the subject matter in fact occurred or exists: the assent of belief is given on more slender evidence than proof. Belief is an inclination of the mind towards assenting to, rather than rejecting, a proposition and the grounds which can reasonably induce that inclination of the mind may, depending on the circumstances, leave something to surmise or conjecture.\"*

\[29\] *Further, the requirement that the belief be reasonable means that it must be based on reasonable grounds. This question is to be judged by the Court by applying an objective test: Samoukovic v Brown \[[1993\] TASSC 78](http://www6.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1993/78.html), per Zeeman J.*

# Police Belief Basis in Hodges
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Basis for Belief in Hodges Case] [CONCEPTS: basis of belief, officer's observations, grounds for belief, reasonable grounds, search powers] [CITATION: hoc-b023-m004-belief-basis-hodges] [ACT: None] [CASE: Hodges [2022] TASSC 45]

In ***Hodges***, the basis of the officer's belief was:

- **Intel search showing defendant had drug history.**
- **Demeanour**
- **Physical presentation of the defendant -- fidgety, dilated pupils, dry mouth**

Great para at \[35\]: *"Further, I am satisfied that the basis of the belief as articulated by Constable Whelan, which include her initial suspicions, her observations of the physical indicia displayed by the accused, and the information obtained from the police database during the computer search, provided objectively reasonable grounds for her belief. In applying *[s 29](http://www6.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/moda2001184/s29.html)*, it should be borne in mind that the provision is intended to provide police with powers, including with respect to the search of vehicles, in operational circumstances in which there may be insufficient time or opportunity to obtain a warrant. The constraint on the exercise of those powers is the possession of a prescribed belief. Because it is a power which it is intended to be used in operational circumstances, the formation of the prescribed belief is likely to be in the type of circumstances described by Constable Whelan, that is, a belief which derives from the incremental accumulation of a number of discrete pieces of information, gathered and mentally processed quickly, eventually coalescing in the relevant belief. Provided the belief is actually held, a further constraint on the exercise of the powers is that it must be held on objectively reasonable grounds, but an assessment of objective reasonableness must take account of the circumstances in which the belief is formed. Having regard to all of the circumstances, I am satisfied that Constable Whelan believed that there was a controlled substance in the vehicle, and this belief was based on objectively reasonable grounds."*

# Ice Pipe Definition in Poisons Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legislation] [TYPE: legislation] [TOPIC: Definition of Ice Pipe] [CONCEPTS: ice pipe, controlled drug, definition, administration, intended use] [CITATION: hoc-b023-m005-ice-pipe-definition] [ACT: Poisons Act] [CASE: None]

***ice pipe*** means a device that --

\(a\) is capable of being used, or is intended to be used, for the purpose of administering a controlled drug into the body of a person by the drawing or inhaling of smoke, or vapour or fumes, resulting from the heating or burning of the controlled drug in a crystal or powder form; or

\(b\) is intended to be used as such a device but requires adjustment, modification or addition before it is so used; or

\(c\) is of a prescribed class, type or description;

***"Prescribed belief"*** means a reasonable belief that a controlled substance or other thing in relation to which an offence under this Act has been committed is --

(a) in the possession of a person in any place; or

(b) on or in a conveyance in any place; or

(c) on an animal in any place.

### Orr v Johnston [2014] TASMC 5 - Admission as Evidence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: The admissibility of admissions regarding drugs] [CONCEPTS: Orr v Johnston, admission evidence, drug possession, legal proceedings, Tasmanian justice]

### Evidentiary Certificate Provision Under Evidence Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001] [SECTION: 177A] [CITATION: Evidence Act s 177A - Evidentiary Certificate Provision] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Evidentiary certificate admissibility] [CONCEPTS: evidentiary certificates, admissible evidence, legal procedure, drug analysis, trial preparation]
Analysis certificate of drug test admissible under 177A of Evidence Act -- prima facie evidence. However, must be served on defence 14 days prior to a hearing. Does not extend to RSAD Act as that specifically excludes its operation (S. 29A).

---

### Detection Times for Oral Samples
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Detection times related to drugs in oral fluids] [CONCEPTS: detection times, oral samples, drug testing, legal implications, law enforcement]
Detection time of drugs in oral fluid is less than that in blood, so that it is more reflective of recent use. The following are general guidelines, and there may be some small exceptions to the rule.
- Cannabis -- 24 hours
- Methylamphetamine -- 3 days
- Amphetamine -- 72 hours
- MDMA -- 3 days
- Morphine -- 7 days
- Heroin -- 2 days

---

### Ice Pipe Definition Under Poisons Act s 86C
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Poisons Act 1971] [SECTION: 86C] [CITATION: Poisons Act s 86C - Ice Pipe Definition] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definition of Ice Pipe for legal purposes] [CONCEPTS: ice pipe, definition, controlled substances, legal terminology, Poisons Act]
***ice pipe*** means a device that --
(a) is capable of being used, or is intended to be used, for the purpose of administering a controlled drug into the body of a person by the drawing or inhaling of smoke, or vapour or fumes, resulting from the heating or burning of the controlled drug in a crystal or powder form; or
(b) is intended to be used as such a device but requires adjustment, modification or addition before it is so used; or
(c) is of a prescribed class, type or description;

### Establishing Items Used for Drug Administration
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Admissibility of evidence relating to drug use] [CONCEPTS: evidence collection, drug administration, legal standards, prosecution strategies, Tasmanian law]
Can get evidence directly from cops that they are used that way... BUT (see below). 

---

### Thomas v Frankcombe, Turton, Medwin and Pears [2010] TASMC 6 - Commentary on Police Observations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Admissibility of police observations as evidence] [CONCEPTS: Thomas v Frankcombe, police evidence, court admissibility, drug-related cases, observation evidence]
Evidence was given by Sergeant Flude, a police officer involved in seizing the relevant material. His evidence included a description of how the smoking devices, otherwise known by the colloquial name "bong", could be used for the purposes of smoking cannabis. His evidence was to the effect that bongs were regularly used by the users of cannabis for that purpose. His evidence also related to how the other items tendered in evidence were also regularly used by cannabis smokers for the smoking administration or taking of cannabis.

---

### Poisons Act - Definition of 'Restrained Substance' and 'Narcotic Substance'
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Poisons Act 1971] [SECTION: 3] [CITATION: Poisons Act s 3 - Substance Definitions] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of restricted and narcotic substances] [CONCEPTS: restricted substance, narcotic substance, legal definitions, substance schedules, Poisons Act]
***Restricted substance*** means a substance that is, for the time being, specified in Schedule 4 to the Poisons List; and ***Narcotic substance*** means a substance that is, for the time being, specified in Schedule 8 to the Poisons List;

---

Psychosocial immaturity and the prefrontal cortex is underdeveloped. That part that leads to impulse control and planning and decision making. Specifically, that those regions associated with emotions and rewards become increasingly active at a time when the brain's control centre, the prefrontal cortex, remains unable to deploy the skills required for weighing complex decisions and regulating behavioural and emotional responses ...

### Youth Offending Outcome Statistics in Tasmania

- Formal Caution: 65 (17%)

- Court: 167 (44%)

### Restorative Justice Overview
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Overview of restorative justice principles] [CONCEPTS: restorative justice, offender responsibility, victim impact, legal frameworks, diversion strategies]
Restorative justice is a facilitated process for people harmed by crime and wrongdoing to talk about the impact of the harm, and for offenders to take responsibility and help repair the damage, with a focus on healing and prevention rather than just punishment. Legislative preference is to divert where possible. Bars to that are a refusal to accept guilt, or the nature or recurrence of offending making it inappropriate.

---

### Limitations on Joint Charges in Youth Justice Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Limitations on joint charging of youth and adults] [CONCEPTS: joint charges, youth justice, legal restrictions, court processes, Tasmanian law]
**27.**   **Limitation on joint charge**: A youth who is under 15 years of age may not be jointly charged with an adult.

**28.**   **Limitation on power of Court to hear joint charge**
(1)  The Court is **not** entitled to hear and determine a charge against a youth if --
(a) the youth is 15 or more years old; and
(b) the youth is charged jointly with an adult; and
(c) the youth and the adult **[both plead not guilty]** to the charge.

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of responsible adult in legal context] [CONCEPTS: responsible adult, youth justice, legal definitions, nominated adult, Tasmanian law]
***Responsible adult***, in relation to a youth, means a person who --
(a) is a ***nominated adult*** in relation to the youth; or
(b) if there is no nominated adult in relation to the youth, a justice of the peace or a person, or a member of a class of persons, approved by the Secretary;

<!-- block_024 master -->

# Youth Sentencing Overview Under the Youth Justice Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: youth sentencing options under the Youth Justice Act] [CONCEPTS: sentencing, rehabilitation, youth offenders, general deterrence, court discretion] [CITATION: hoc-b024-m001-youth-sentencing-options] [ACT: Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas)] [CASE: DPP v NOP; Director of Public Prosecutions v JSP]

Dismissal is the least severe sentencing option for young offenders sentenced under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas). Dismissal means that a youth is found guilty, but the charge is dismissed and no other sentence is ordered. A conviction cannot be recorded.

A reprimand means that a youth is found guilty and the charge is dismissed and the youth is reprimanded. This is a formal warning by the court. A conviction cannot be recorded.

A dismissal with an undertaking to be of good behaviour is a form of conditional unsupervised release. An undertaking to be of good behaviour is an agreement by a youth to be of good behaviour, which generally means that the youth agrees to adhere to specific conditions set by the court.

Under the sanction of release and adjournment with conditions, the youth is released, and sentencing is postponed for a nominated period (no more than 12 months) on conditions set out by the court. A conviction cannot be recorded. At the conclusion of the adjournment with conditions, if the youth does everything required by the conditions, the youth is discharged from the order. If the youth does not meet the conditions, they may need to return to court, and the court may impose a new order.

For further sentencing measures, probation orders require young people to report to youth justice workers as required. The court can attach special conditions, such as attending school or other educational programs, counselling, or treatment. The youth may also be required to abstain from drinking alcohol or using controlled substances, submit to testing for controlled substances or alcohol, live at a specified address, and/or comply with a curfew.

A community service order can also be an option for young offenders. Detention is described as a last resort; however, it is acknowledged that general deterrence is permissible for serious offending. The Supreme Court has recognized that there are tensions in the aims and purposes of sentencing under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas) that can be difficult to reconcile, particularly regarding serious offences. It has been consistently accepted that while rehabilitation is often a predominant factor, both general and personal deterrence can play a role, especially in serious cases.

For example, in DPP v NOP, a case involving a conviction for rape, Evan J articulated that "whilst rehabilitation is ordinarily the primary consideration for youthful offenders, this does not mean that the Court must disregard the needs of general deterrence and denunciation." Most recently, in Director of Public Prosecutions v JSP, as quoted by Estcourt J in the Court of Criminal Appeal, the established principles concerning juvenile sentencing are reiterated. The principles dictate that the rehabilitation of the offender is always an important, if not the dominant, consideration, and that any sentence should be tailored with greater emphasis on the welfare of the youth; this emphasis on rehabilitation aligns with the broader goal of community protection.

The justification for these sentencing principles is that youthful offenders may not fully appreciate the nature and extent of their criminality and are likely to make ill-considered and immature decisions. Nonetheless, the importance of rehabilitation of a youthful offender is typically far more significant than general deterrence, although instances may arise where just punishment and general deterrence become equally important.

<!-- block_024 procedure -->

### Youth Sentencing Options
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: multiple] [CITATION: Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas) - Youth Sentencing Options] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Overview of youth sentencing options] [CONCEPTS: youth sentencing, dismissal, reprimand, good behaviour, probation, community service, court options]

This chunk covers the various sentencing options available for young offenders under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas).

1. **Dismissal**: Dismissal is the least severe sentencing option for young offenders sentenced under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas). Dismissal means that a youth is found guilty, but the charge is dismissed and no other sentence is ordered. A conviction cannot be recorded.

2. **Reprimand**: A reprimand means that a youth is found guilty and the charge is dismissed and the youth is reprimanded. This is a formal warning by the court. A conviction cannot be recorded.

3. **Dismissal with undertaking to be of good behaviour**: A dismissal with an undertaking to be of good behaviour is a form of conditional unsupervised release. An undertaking to be of good behaviour is an agreement by a youth.

4. **Release and adjournment with conditions**: Under this sanction, the youth is released and sentencing is postponed for a nominated period (no more than 12 months) on conditions set out by the court. A conviction cannot be recorded. At the conclusion of the adjournment with conditions, if the youth does everything required by the conditions, the youth is discharged from the order. If the youth does not do everything required under the conditions, the youth may have to return to court and the court may impose a new order.

5. **Fine**: Details not provided.

6. **Probation order**: Young people on probation orders must report to youth justice workers as required. The court can attach special conditions, such as attending school or other educational programs, counselling or treatment. The youth may also be required to abstain from drinking alcohol or using controlled substances, submitting to testing for controlled substances or alcohol, to live at a specified address, and/or comply with a curfew.

7. **Community service order**: Details not provided.

8. **Detention as a LAST resort**: General deterrence is appropriate for serious offending.

### Commentary on Youth Sentencing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: multiple] [CITATION: Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas) - Sentencing Commentary] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Discussion on rehabilitation and deterrence in youth sentencing] [CONCEPTS: youth sentencing, deterrence, rehabilitation, serious offending, Supreme Court]

This chunk provides commentary on the tensions in the aims and purposes of sentencing under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas), particularly for serious offending.

The Supreme Court has recognised that there are tensions in the aims and purposes of sentencing under the Youth Justice Act 1997 (Tas) that can be difficult to reconcile, particularly in relation to serious offending. It has been consistently accepted that while rehabilitation is often a predominant factor, both general and personal deterrence can have a role to play, particularly in relation to serious offending. For example, in DPP v NOP, a case involving a conviction for rape, Evan J wrote that "whilst rehabilitation is ordinarily the primary consideration for youthful offenders, this does not mean that the Court must disregard the needs of general deterrence and denunciation."

Most recently, in Director of Public Prosecutions v JSP, Estcourt J quoted the comments: "There is no need to set out those provisions in the Youth Justice Act. Those relevant to sentencing restate the relevant principles concerning the sentencing of juvenile offenders which have long been established by the courts. The principles are that rehabilitation of the offender is always an important, if not the dominant consideration, and that any sentence should be tailored with greater emphasis on the welfare of the youth."

### Strangulation Legal Definition and Elements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: 170B] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 170B - Strangulation Definition] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal definition and elements of strangulation] [CONCEPTS: strangulation, assault, intent, injury, legislative definition]

This chunk outlines the legal definition and required elements for strangulation under Tasmanian law.

Elements:
"The crime is also one of specific intent, meaning that the Crown must show that the accused intended to cause the act of choking, suffocation, or strangulation. Those acts are not defined in the legislation so that the words will have their ordinary meanings that change and evolve over time in line with community expectations."

Intentionally unlawfully causing harm to another person, consider also - S 168 - Disabling - rendering someone unable to flee. 

Suffocation does not require blunt force; it can just involve pressure to the chest or laying on their chest. 

Strangulation can occur by applying any external pressure from any source to the neck. Must be intentional. Evidence of injury is rare.

### Special Witness Applications Procedural Guidelines for Medical Practitioners
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001 - Special Witness Applications: Medical Practitioners] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Guidelines for medical practitioners on special witness applications] [CONCEPTS: special witness applications, medical practitioner guidelines, vulnerable witnesses, court evidence, psychological assessment]

This chunk outlines the requirements for medical practitioners in support of special witness applications.

Normally when witnesses give evidence in court it is in the presence of the defendant. The witness must retell their evidence of the offence in the witness box, within 2 to 3 metres of the defendant and in their full view. 

To satisfy the court of these matters we have requested that you prepare a report covering:
1. Qualifications and experience
2. The subject matter of the witness's evidence
3. Details of any disability/disorder (including stress disorders) the witness suffers and any treatment
4. Opinion on whether they could give evidence without suffering severe emotional trauma or coherently without distress.

### Special Witness Applications Procedural Guidelines for Prosecutors
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001 - Special Witness Applications: Prosecutors] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Guidelines for prosecutors on special witness applications] [CONCEPTS: special witness applications, prosecutor guidelines, vulnerable witnesses, court procedures, hearsay evidence]

This chunk focuses on the obligations of prosecutors regarding special witness applications.

What needs to be established?
(a) By reason of intellectual, mental or physical disability, the person is, or is likely to be, unable to give evidence satisfactorily in the ordinary manner; or
(b) By reason of age, cultural background, relationship to any party to the proceeding, the nature of the subject matter of the evidence, or any other factor the court considers relevant, the person is likely to suffer severe emotional trauma or to be so intimidated or distressed as to be unable to give evidence or to give evidence satisfactorily.

For supporting evidence, consider calling recent complaint evidence from Police Officers who took the victim's statements to establish distress and intimidation. 

### Key Definitions - Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001 - Key Definitions] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Key definitions under the Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act] [CONCEPTS: key definitions, affected child, prescribed witness, evidence, court definitions]

This chunk captures key definitions relevant to the Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act.

**Affected child**: Under 18, giving evidence in FV matters or child sex offences.

**Affected person**: Is 18 but under 18 when a victim of child sex offences.

**Prescribed proceedings/offences**: FV offences, Applications concerning PFVO and FVO's, 8(1) or 35(3) POA, child sex offences.

**Prescribed witness**: Witness declared as such under s 7J.

### Relevant Sections and Orders of Events for Witness Intermediaries
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001 - Orders of Events] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Orders of events for court treatment of witness intermediaries] [CONCEPTS: witness intermediaries, court procedures, legislation, orders, assessment reports]

This chunk outlines the necessary orders of events concerning the use of witness intermediaries in court.

**At earliest opportunity**: Petition the court to consider seeking an assessment report under Section 7I and adjourn for that purpose to a later "ground rules hearing" under Section 7K.

Once the report is obtained:
- Petition the court to formally order that a witness intermediary be used in respect of a witness in a specified proceeding.
- Identify and advise the court of any statutory presumption that arises by virtue of the witness becoming a prescribed witness.

# Application Before the Magistrate Regarding Child Witnesses
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: principles in s3 of the Act relating to child witnesses] [CONCEPTS: child witnesses, video recorded statements, distress, trauma, evidence] [CITATION: hoc-b025-m001-application-child-witnesses] [ACT: Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001] [CASE: None]

- File an application seeking the particular support/s.

- You can get instructions directly from the witness/parents and make representations to the Magistrate.

- Support person; it cannot be a witness in the proceeding. WAS officers are trained in what they can and can't do. WAS officers are available for certain matters.

- Admission of prior statement; submissions to the effect that; (e.g.)

The Principles in s3 of the Act relating to child witnesses are intended to reduce the child's distress/trauma when giving evidence. That video recorded statements are taken soon after the event and are made when they are fresh in the memory of the witness; they are therefore more likely to be an accurate and reliable account of what occurred. It is an irrefutable record of what was said, how the questions were asked/answers elicited. It captures gestures when the child attempts to explain an action that has occurred. It also enables the fact finder to observe their age and demeanour at the time that they are recounting what occurred, especially important when there has been a delay in listing for hearing.

For a special hearing; this may be sought when there is likely to be a lengthy delay or where the whole of the hearing cannot be heard at one time. However, as matters are heard by Magistrates and not Judge and Jury, it is unlikely to be useful in the Magistrates Court jurisdiction, as the special hearing has to be recorded. The magistrate is more likely to just proceed with that part of the hearing without the need to conduct a special hearing.

**EXAMPLE APPLICATION:**

Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001

Application

Section 5 Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001

To: Dinesh Loganathan

Crotty Legal

174 Macquarie Street

Hobart Tasmania 7000

Senior Constable Scott Wilkie v John DOE (A Pseudonym)

1. Application is made pursuant to Section 5(1) of the Evidence (Children and Special Witnesses) Act 2001 that:

(i) The video statement made by AAAAA, an affected child, on 30 May 2019 be admitted into evidence; and

(ii) The video statement made by AAAAA, an affected child, on 9 April 2020 be admitted into evidence; and

(iii) The video statement made by BBBBB, an affected child, on 9 April 2020 be admitted into evidence.

DATED this 7th day of January 2021

Signed: .................................................

Tamila Smith

Crown Counsel

Office of the Director of Public Prosecutions

### Chunk 5
#### Explanation of power of arrest under the Criminal Code.
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code] [SECTION: 301] [CITATION: Criminal Code s 301 - Power of Arrest Explanation] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Police power to arrest] [CONCEPTS: arrest, duty, reason, Criminal Code, law enforcement]

The *Criminal Code, *s301(2), provides as follows:

"**(2)** It is the duty of a person arresting another, whether with or without warrant, to give notice, if practicable, of the process or warrant under which he is acting, or of the cause of the arrest."

If it is pointless (i.e., they would not be able to hear the cops) to inform or attempt to inform an arrested person of the cause of the arrest, it is not "practicable" to do so within the meaning of s 301(2) **Johnson v Reeves [2004] TASSC 110 (24 September 2004)**

# Definition and Understanding of Harassment under the Family Violence Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: test is mental element for "harassing" requires intent to act] [CONCEPTS: harassment, Family Violence Act, mental element, conduct, statutory context] [CITATION: hoc-b026-m001-harassment-definition] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55]

Section 4 covers PFVO and FVO definitions of harras-/harassing. It is an exhaustive definition **Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55 [para52]** 

The only mental element is that the def intend the ACT that by virtue of the definition in s 4 constitutes harassing, not the act had any particular effect on the person to be protected.

60. "Harass" in its ordinary sense involves conduct committed with the purpose of causing an effect on the person harassed, eg worry, fear or mental anguish: see *Johnson v Collier* [(1997) 142 FLR 409]. However, in my view, the provision of an exhaustive definition of "harassing", by reference to the actions of the alleged harasser, excludes the need to establish that the actions in question have had any particular effect on the person protected by the order, unless expressly so provided in the definition. It must logically follow that there is no requirement that the defendant intend or otherwise foresee the causation of such an effect. These conclusions are confirmed by the inclusion in some of the defined actions of an express requirement that the action occur in circumstances "where it is likely" that there will be some consequence relevant to the protected person. For example, in par (f), it is necessary that the offensive material be left "where it is likely to be found by, given to or brought to the attention of" the protected person. There are similar express requirements contained in pars (g) and (h). There would, of course, be no need to include these specific provisions if an intention to cause or the actual causation of apprehension or fear was already implied in the definition.

61. This construction is consistent with the subject matter and social purpose of the relevant legislation. The provisions in question are concerned with the establishment of firm and viable processes to ensure the protection of persons from persons who probably have committed, and will again commit, family violence [s 16(1)] of the Act. Family violence is recognised as a significant social problem and the Act is directly and expressly directed at dealing with this problem. A family violence order is the primary protective mechanism employed by the Act. [Section 3] provides:

"In the administration of this Act, the safety, psychological wellbeing and interests of people affected by family violence are the paramount considerations."

62. The construction of the Act in respect of the mens rea required for the offence of contravention of an order will be heavily influenced by this statutory context. However, while these considerations weigh against the presumption in favour of proof of intention to commit the offence, they do not completely displace it. Rather, they fashion and define the required mental element. Although the offence created by [s 35] is summary, the section prescribes heavy penalties. This is also a relevant consideration, one which weighs in favour of the application of the presumption. In my view, the proper balance leads to the conclusion that the requisite mental element is that the defendant must intend to do the act, which by virtue of the definition in [s 4], constitutes "harassing". In this case, the relevant act is that defined by par (a), following the complainant. If this mental element is present, then the legislation operates to deem that the person has harassed the person protected by the order and, if the condition is in place, deems that there has been a contravention of the order. A requirement of this nature is consistent with the statutory scheme, is appropriate having regard to the subject matter of the provision, and enables and supports the viable enforcement of a family violence order. It also provides the person subject to the order with a clear understanding of the conduct proscribed by it.

# Interpretation of "Following" and "Keeping Under Surveillance" in Harassment Context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: court considers evidence for "keeping under surveillance" includes ongoing observation] [CONCEPTS: following, surveillance, Family Violence Act, case law, mens rea] [CITATION: hoc-b026-m002-following-surveillance-evidence] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18]

**Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18 (3 August 2020)**

[154] Obviously the words 'following' or 'keeping under surveillance' are not actually used in the particulars of count 4 (unlike counts 5 and 7 which specifically allege that Mr Bearman followed Ms Paul). Grammatically, at least, it is difficult to see how the particulars of count 4 could possibly satisfy the term 'following'. Further, the term 'keeping the person under surveillance' requires proof (at least) of close observation of the protected person by the defendant; and to be guilty of 'keeping' another person under surveillance requires that the surveillance be conducted on a continued basis (as distinct from, for example, just looking at someone only once - and only for a short period). Such an interpretation gives meaning to the word 'keeping' and promotes the purpose and objects of the *Family Violence Act 2004* generally. Further, it avoids giving the word 'harassing' too broad a meaning in the broader context within which this particular penal provision of the *Family Violence Act 2004* sits.

# The Nature of Harassment as a Single Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers single act sufficient for harassment under Family Violence Act] [CONCEPTS: harassment, single act, statutory interpretation, case law, communication] [CITATION: hoc-b026-m003-single-act-harassment] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33]

**Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33 (29 July 2013)**

{para 13} Harassing means doing any one or more of the following actions in respect of a particular person. 

*(i)* Contacting the person by post or telephonic, electronic or any other means of communication.

The reference in the definition to "any one or more of the following actions" suggests that a single act might be sufficient. Furthermore, it is appropriate, in my view, to interpret the term having regard to the context in which it is used in the order. The reference to "threaten, harass, abuse or assault" suggests that the order is to be understood as providing protection to a person from contact with the respondent which is unwelcome, and might be in various forms or have variable effect.

{para 14} This interpretation is consistent with case authority (see *Daly v Medwell* [(1986) 40 SASR 281], where it was stated that the offence of using a telecommunications service for the purpose of harassing a person could be constituted by a single call or a course of conduct consisting of several calls, depending on the nature and intention of the relevant conduct, per White J at 284, King CJ at 296 and Prior J at 297; see also *Devlin v Cooper* [1991] TASSC 115).

# Financial Abuse: Understanding the Threshold
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: financial abuse requires actual intent and course of conduct] [CONCEPTS: financial abuse, intent, course of conduct, legislation, legal standards] [CITATION: hoc-b026-m004-financial-abuse-threshold] [ACT: None] [CASE: None]

Unlike emotional abuse there is no objective test -- there must be evidence of actual intent.

Must be a course of conduct. Whilst the type of abuse can be only one of the types provided, there must be a course of conduct -- implying more than one act.

<!-- block_026 procedure -->

### Heading: Definition and Commentary on Harassing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas)] [SECTION: 4] [CITATION: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s 4 - Definition and Commentary on Harassing] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definition and commentary on harassing] [CONCEPTS: harassing, definition, Cannell v G, mens rea, family violence]
Section 4 covers PFVO and FVO definitions of harass-/harassing. It is an exhaustive definition **Cannell v G; G v Cannell \[2018\] TASSC 55 \[para 52\]**. 

The only mental element is that the def intend the ACT that by virtue of the definition in s 4 constitutes harassing, not that the act had any particular effect on the person to be protected. 

60. "Harass" in its ordinary sense involves conduct committed with the purpose of causing an effect on the person harassed, eg worry, fear or mental anguish: see *Johnson v Collier* [(1997) 142 FLR 409](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281997%29%20142%20FLR%20409). However, in my view, the provision of an exhaustive definition of "harassing", by reference to the actions of the alleged harasser, excludes the need to establish that the actions in question have had any particular effect on the person protected by the order, unless expressly so provided in the definition. It must logically follow that there is no requirement that the defendant intend or otherwise foresee the causation of such an effect. These conclusions are confirmed by the inclusion in some of the defined actions of an express requirement that the action occur in circumstances "where it is likely" that there will be some consequence relevant to the protected person. For example, in par (f), it is necessary that the offensive material be left "where it is likely to be found by, given to or brought to the attention of" the protected person. There are similar express requirements contained in pars (g) and (h). There would, of course, be no need to include these specific provisions, if an intention to cause or the actual causation of apprehension or fear was already implied in the definition.

61. This construction is consistent with the subject matter and social purpose of the relevant legislation. The provisions in question are concerned with the establishment of firm and viable processes to ensure the protection of persons, from persons who probably have committed, and will again commit, family violence. Family violence is recognised as a significant social problem and the Act is directly and expressly directed at dealing with this problem. A family violence order is the primary protective mechanism employed by the Act. *Section 3* provides: "In the administration of this Act, the safety, psychological wellbeing and interests of people affected by family violence are the paramount considerations."

62. The construction of the Act in respect of the mens rea required for the offence of contravention of an order will be heavily influenced by this statutory context. However, while these considerations weigh against the presumption in favour of proof of intention to commit the offence, they do not completely displace it. Rather, they fashion and define the required mental element. Although the offence created by *s 35* is summary, the section prescribes heavy penalties. This is also a relevant consideration, one which weighs in favour of the application of the presumption. In my view, the proper balance leads to the conclusion that the requisite mental element is that the defendant must intend to do the act, which by virtue of the definition in *s 4*, constitutes "harassing". In this case, the relevant act is that defined by par (a), following the complainant. If this mental element is present, then the legislation operates to deem that the person has harassed the person protected by the order and, if the condition is in place, deems that there has been a contravention of the order. A requirement of this nature is consistent with the statutory scheme, is appropriate having regard to the subject matter of the provision, and enables and supports the viable enforcement of a family violence order. It also provides the person subject to the order with a clear understanding of the conduct proscribed by it.

### Heading: Commentary on Following/Keeping Under Surveillance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas)] [SECTION: 4] [CITATION: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s 4 - Commentary on Following/Keeping Under Surveillance] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Commentary on Interpretation of Following and Keeping Under Surveillance] [CONCEPTS: following, keeping under surveillance, Sundram v Bearman, family violence, legal interpretation]
**Sundram v Bearman \[2020\] TASMC 18 (3 August 2020)**

\[154\] Obviously the words *'following'* or *'keeping under surveillance'* are not actually used in the particulars of count 4 (unlike counts 5 and 7 which specifically allege that Mr Bearman followed Ms Paul). Grammatically, at least, it is difficult to see how the particulars of count 4 could possibly satisfy the term '*following*'. Further, the term *'keeping the person under surveillance'* requires proof (at least) of close observation of the protected person by the defendant; and to be guilty of '*keeping*' another person under surveillance requires that the surveillance be conducted on a continued basis (as distinct from, for example, just looking at someone only once - and only for a short period). Such an interpretation gives meaning to the word *'keeping'* and promotes the purpose and objects of the [*Family Violence Act 2004*](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/fva2004158/) generally. Further, it avoids giving the word *'harassing'* too broad a meaning in the broader context within which this particular penal provision of the *[Family Violence Act 2004](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/fva2004158/)* sits.

{para 13} Harassing means doing any one or more of the following actions in respect of a particular person. *(i)* Contacting the person by post or telephonic, electronic or any other means of communication. 

The reference in the definition to *"any one or more of the following actions"* suggests that a single act might be sufficient.
