### Heading: Commentary on Family Law Act Amendments
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act 2018] [SECTION: 68T; 68R; 68P; 45A] [CITATION: Family Law Amendment Act 2018 - Commentary on Family Law Act Amendments] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Commentary on Family Law Act Amendments] [CONCEPTS: Family Law Act, family violence orders, judicial discretion, interim orders, child protection]
The *Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act 2018* commenced on 31 August 2018.

A copy of the Bill and Explanatory Memorandum can be obtained at https://www.aph.gov.au/Parliamentary_Business/Bills_Legislation/Bills_Search_Results/Result?bId=s1109.

**Amendment to Section 68T -- Special provisions relating to proceedings to make an interim (or interim variation of a) family violence order**

Section 68R of the *Family Law Act 1975* provides that a court of a State or Territory which holds jurisdiction under Part VII of the Family Law Act may, when making a family violence order, revive, vary, discharge, or suspend a range of family law orders to the extent to which they provide for a child to spend time with a person, to ensure they are consistent with the State or Territory family violence order. Subsection 68R(4) restricts the court's power when making an *interim* family violence order to revival, variation or suspension of a family law order.

Section 68T previously provided that where the court, when making an *interim* family violence order or an *interim variation* of a family violence order, revives, varies or suspends an order, injunction or arrangement under section 68R, that revival, variation or suspension ceases to have effect at the earlier of:

(a) the time the interim order stops being in force; and 

(b) the end of the period of 21 days starting when the interim order was made.

The Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act has removed the 21-day time limit in paragraph (b) of section 68T and replaced it with:

(b) the time specified in the interim order as the time at which the revival, variation or suspension ceases to have effect; and

(c) the time the order, injunction or arrangement is affected by an order (however described) made by a court, under section 68R or otherwise, after the revival, variation or suspension.

The amendments require judicial officers to consider whether the revival, variation or suspension should expire on a specific date (and if so, when), or whether it should expire when the interim family violence order expires, or when a court makes an alternative order.

These new provisions strengthen the court's ability to protect children and other vulnerable family members by allowing judicial officers to set timeframes according to the particular circumstances of the case. Relevant considerations when making such a decision may include balancing the safety risks to the child arising from having contact with a parent with the maintenance of the child/parent relationship. These changes are intended to better protect children and other vulnerable family members from violence by reducing the risk that can arise from inconsistent orders applying to a child's contact with an allegedly violent parent.

Judicial officers may only change family law orders under section 68R when they have evidence that was not before the court that made the original order (subsection 68R(3)). Evidence must support a finding that changing the family law order is in the best interests of the child concerned including because the family law order is inconsistent with a family violence order in force, or a person, including the child, has been or is likely to be exposed to family violence as a result of the operation of that order (subsection 68R(5)).

### Heading: Commentary on Court Obligations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act 2018] [SECTION: 68P] [CITATION: Family Law Amendment Act 2018 - Commentary on Court Obligations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Court Obligations under Family Law Act] [CONCEPTS: court obligations, Family Law Act, family violence order, child protection, legal responsibilities]
**Amendment to Section 68P -- Obligation of court making order or granting an injunction under this Act that is inconsistent with an existing family violence order**

Section 68P of the Family Law Act sets out the obligations of a court when making an order or granting an injunction under the Family Law Act that is *inconsistent with an existing family violence order*. Subparagraph 68P(2)(c)(iii) requires the court, to the extent to which the order or injunction provides for a child to spend time with a person, or expressly or impliedly requires or authorises a person to spend time with a child, to explain the order or injunction to the person protected by the family violence order (if that person is not the applicant or respondent). In some circumstances, the person protected by the family violence order may be a child. Paragraph 68P(2)(d) sets out the matters that the court must include in the explanation, and notes that the explanation must be in language the person is likely to readily understand.

The Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act has inserted three new subsections:

- subsection 68P(2A) provides that the court is not required to provide the explanation mandated by subparagraph 68P(2)(c)(iii) to a child if the court is satisfied that it is in the child's best interests not to receive an explanation of the order or injunction;

- subsection 68P(2B) provides that the court is not required to include a particular matter otherwise required to be explained by paragraph 68P(2)(d) if the court is satisfied that it is in the child's best interests for the matter not to be included in the explanation; and 

- subsection 68P(2C) provides that when determining what is in a child's best interests for the purposes of new subsections 68P(2A) and 68P(2B), the court is required to consider the matters in existing subsection 60CC(2), but is not required (but may choose) to consider the matters listed in existing subsection 60CC(3), despite the requirement in subsection 60CC(1).

In most cases, it will be in a child's best interests to understand how court orders apply to them and their family. However, this may not always be the case. In particular, it may not be in the child's best interests to be exposed to the conflict between their parents to the extent necessary for courts to comply with this requirement. In some cases, such as where the child is an infant, it will not be practical to give an explanation.

The provision is designed to ensure that the information children receive from a court is appropriate and does not expose them to further details of family violence which may result in re-traumatisation.

### Heading: Commentary on Summary Decrees
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act 2018] [SECTION: 45A] [CITATION: Family Law Amendment Act 2018 - Commentary on Summary Decrees] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Summary Decrees in Family Law Act] [CONCEPTS: summary decrees, family law, judicial discretion, abuse of process, family violence]
**Insertion of a new Section 45A - Summary decrees**

The Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act has inserted a new section 45A into the Family Law Act, which strengthens and codifies family law judges' power to dismiss unmeritorious cases and proceedings that are frivolous, vexatious or an abuse of process. While this does not significantly change the existing powers of the courts exercising family law jurisdiction to dismiss proceedings if they have no reasonable prospect of success, the provision is intended to improve court efficiency by providing greater clarity about when applications can be dismissed by the court.

Subsections 45A(1) and (2) allow the court to make a summary decree in favour of one party, in relation to the whole or part of a proceeding, if satisfied that a party has no reasonable prospect of successfully:

- prosecuting the proceedings or part of the proceedings, or 

- defending the proceedings or part of the proceedings.

Subsection 45A(3) provides that, for the purposes of the section, in determining whether a defence or proceeding has no reasonable prospect of success, proceedings need not be hopeless or bound to fail.

Subsection 45A(4) empowers the court to dismiss all or part of a proceeding if it is frivolous, vexatious or an abuse of process.

Subsection 45A(5) provides that proceedings or a part of proceedings are not to be considered frivolous, vexatious or an abuse of process just because a related application is made and later withdrawn. This is intended to operate as a safeguard against the court misinterpreting the actions of victims of family violence who are litigants in person, who might make an application and then withdraw it for reasons other than the merits of their case, for example, as a result of the power and control dynamics of family violence.

Subsections 45A(6)-(8) provide that:

- the court may make costs orders as it sees fit

- the court may take such action of its own volition, or on the application of a party to the proceedings, and 

- the new section does not limit any powers that the court has apart from this section.

Section 45A has general application. Its use is not limited to matters involving allegations of family violence or other particular categories of disadvantage.

Section 118 of the Family Law Act, which deals with frivolous or vexatious proceedings, has been repealed.

### Heading: Commentary on Other Amendments
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act 2018] [SECTION: relevant section if applicable] [CITATION: Family Law Amendment Act 2018 - Commentary on Other Amendments] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Other Amendments to Family Law Act] [CONCEPTS: family law, amendments, jurisdiction, property matters, family violence]
**Other amendments**

The Family Law Amendment (Family Violence and Other Measures) Act has also amended the Family Law Act such that:

- state and territory courts can be prescribed by regulations to have the same jurisdiction in family law parenting matters as that held by state and territory courts of summary jurisdiction under Part VII of the Family Law Act. The intent is to enable children's courts to make appropriate orders under the Family Law Act where there are existing proceedings before the court, particularly in child protection matters (see section 69GA).

The new provisions allow for the prescription of classes of proceedings in regulations, in which the prescribed court will have Part VII jurisdiction. It also allows for the prescription of rules of court in regulations, to apply when the prescribed court is exercising family jurisdiction.

It is a requirement that the Attorney-General consult with the relevant state or territory Minister before a court or any other matter is prescribed.

- Courts of summary jurisdiction can hear contested family law property matters without both parties' consent, where the value of the property is up to a new limit to be prescribed in regulations. The new limit will exceed the current value of \$20,000, and different limits can be prescribed for different states and territories (see section 46A).

It is a requirement that the Attorney-General consult with the relevant state or territory Minister before a property value is prescribed.

Unless or until a new value is prescribed, the current limit of \$20,000 will continue to apply.

- Subsection 114(2) of the Family Law Act is repealed. Consequently, it is no longer open to the court to make an order relieving a party to a marriage from an obligation to perform marital services or render conjugal rights. The High Court held in *R v L (1991)* 174 CLR 379 that there is no such obligation under Australian law. Section 114(2) has therefore been effectively redundant since that time.

# Emotional Abuse and Relevant Legal Provisions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Types of emotional abuse in legal context] [CONCEPTS: emotional abuse, victim statements, Family Violence Act, coercion, threats] [CITATION: hoc-b027-m001-emotional-abuse-legal-provisions] [ACT: Justices Act 1959, Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

There is no need to specify the type of effect "i.e. unreasonable controlling etc.) it might have on the victim. Can simply state them all in the alternative.

Victim has provided reports that she lied in the making of the statements that gave rise to the charges. There is no clear corroborating evidence as both parties alleged the other assaulted them and both had injuries. Insufficient evidence to proceed against vic for false statement but concession from victim that she will agree to an order protecting Hanlon. Def will PG to other assault on basis of TNE on this. ### Course of Conduct
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Course of conduct in family violence cases] [CONCEPTS: course of conduct, threats, emotional abuse, Family Violence Act, legal requirements] [CITATION: hoc-b027-m002-course-of-conduct] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Howe v S, Thomas v Stewart]

The test is that it needs more than one action; however, if they use threats like "I'll kill myself if you leave," this should satisfy the requirement of ss 2. Howe v S — applied by MCKEE in Thomas v Stewart [2017] TASMC, Mr Doyle again submits that the difficulty with this charge is that a single conversation cannot amount to a breach of s.9 of the Family Violence Act in that it is an element of the offence created by that section that the defendant has pursued a course of conduct, and that this necessarily involves more than one conversation.

I agree with the general proposition that a course of conduct would normally be interpreted as "conduct which is protracted or conduct which is engaged in on more than one separate occasion". (See Gunes v Pearson [1996] 89A Crim R 297 at 306). I note also that the definition of "family violence" in s.7 of the Family Violence Act includes "stalking" within the meaning of s.192 of the Criminal Code. That section defines "stalking" as a course of conduct. S.192(2) provides that for the purposes of that definition:

(a) A person pursues a course of conduct if the conduct is sustained or the conduct occurs on more than one occasion.

On the other hand, s.9 itself defines "a course of conduct" to include "limiting the freedom of movement of a person, spouse or partner by means of threats or intimidation". It seems to me that if a threat or act of intimidation had the effect of limiting the freedom of movement of a person, spouse or partner, a single act of threat or intimidation could, in those circumstances, amount to "a course of conduct" and hence amount to an offence contrary to that section. Because of what follows, however, I do not intend to decide this question.

# Powers of Police in Family Violence Circumstances
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Police powers in family violence situations] [CONCEPTS: police powers, family violence, forensic examination, entry powers, seizure] [CITATION: hoc-b027-m003-police-powers-fv] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

A police officer can do almost anything on the basis of "reasonably suspects". Section 10(b) of the Family Violence Act appears to give police the power to subject parties to forensic examination. The term 'forensic examination' is not defined, but the Forensic Procedure Act 2000 specifies that it includes non-invasive procedures, which are defined to include photographs. Section 5 of that Act states that the FP Act does not apply to victims. Although likely to be charged as a defendant as well on the reciprocal charge, photos were taken for the purpose of investigation as her as a victim.

### Justices Act 1959 - Section 30 Annotated
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959] [SECTION: 30] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 s 30 - Complaint Details] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Requirements for complaints in justices proceedings] [CONCEPTS: complaint details, ordinary language, essential elements, particulars, justices proceedings]
Any complaint, summons, warrant, or other document that is laid, issued, or made for the purpose of, or in connection with, proceedings before justices shall be sufficient if it --

1. describes the matter of complaint with which the defendant is charged or of which he is convicted in ordinary language, avoiding as far as possible the use of technical terms and without necessarily stating all the essential elements of the matter of complaint; and
2. contains such particulars as will give reasonable information of the nature of the matter complained of.

### Powers of Police in Family Violence Circumstances

- Forensic examination is not defined. Forensic Procedure, defined in the Forensic Procedure Act, includes non-invasive forensic procedures. Non-invasive procedures defined to include photos.

### Arrest and Detention Protocols
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 12] [CITATION: Family Violence Act 2004 s 12 - Arrest and Detention] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Protocols for arrest and detention in family violence cases] [CONCEPTS: arrest, detention, police bail, Family Violence Orders, breach]
Section 12 of the FV Act says that section 34 of the Justices Act does NOT apply where charged for a breach of PFVO or FVO. Meaning they must be taken to court. However, where there is NO breach of a PFVO or FVO -- defendant can be released unconditionally or under police bail. 

### Intent Considerations in Breaches
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 35] [CITATION: Sundram v Bearman - Intent Considerations] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Intent required for breach prosecution] [CONCEPTS: intent, breach of order, criminal prosecution, evidence burden, family violence]
In Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18, it is noted that the Family Violence Act 2004 does not create an offence of strict liability, regardless of fault. The evidence must prove that Mr Bearman "knew all the facts constituting the ingredients necessary to make his physical acts criminal were involved in what he was doing."

### Collateral Challenge Rule
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 35] [CITATION: McKenna v Smith - Collateral Challenge Rule] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Restrictions on challenging underlying orders] [CONCEPTS: collateral challenge, validity of orders, family violence, court orders, evidence]
In hearings for charges under Section 35 of the FV Act, the defense cannot seek to challenge the validity of the underlying orders that contain the conditions. This is illustrated in McKenna v Smith [2014] TASMC 11.

### Subjective Recklessness in Threats
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: R v Meek - Subjective Recklessness] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Legal interpretation of threats and recklessness] [CONCEPTS: subjective recklessness, threats, family violence, fear arousal, intent]
Subjective recklessness is referenced in R v Meek [1981] 1 NZLR 499 (cited in GARY JOHN CARTER v R No. SCCRM 94/72 Judgment No. 4498) regarding whether fear is aroused in the victim.

### Principles for Construing Family Violence Orders
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 7] [CITATION: Kerr v Brown - Principles for Constructing Orders] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Interpretation principles for family violence orders] [CONCEPTS: family violence order, construal principles, intent, compliance, court obligations]
The general principles for construing the terms of a Family Violence Order were discussed by Magistrate Brett in Kerr v Brown [2013] TASMC 30.

### Matters Arising from Relationships
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Police v Martin - Communication in Relationships] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Analysis of communication within relationship context] [CONCEPTS: communication, relationship dynamics, discussion, apology, family violence]
In Police v Martin, Magistrate Jago noted the distinction between an apology and a discussion surrounding matters arising from a relationship.

### Attempt under Acts Interpretation Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Acts Interpretation Act 1931] [SECTION: 34] [CITATION: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 s 34 - Attempt Definition] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of attempt to commit an offense] [CONCEPTS: attempt, offense, Acts Interpretation, punishable offense]
Where by any Act an offence is constituted, any attempt to commit such offence shall be an offence and be punishable in the same manner as the offence constituted by such Act.

### Breach Evidentiary Considerations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 35] [CITATION: Bray v F Hoffman - Breach Evidence] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Evidentiary burdens in breach charges] [CONCEPTS: breach charges, evidentiary considerations, second-hand hearsay, protection to respondent, Family Violence Orders]
Where there is a charge under section 35, the allegation in the complaint is evidence that there was an order in place (Bray v F Hoffman -- La Roche Ltd [2002] FCA 243).

### Sentencing Considerations for Family Violence Orders
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Family Violence Sentencing Considerations - General] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Considerations for sentencing breaches of family violence orders] [CONCEPTS: sentencing considerations, family violence order, victim protection, general deterrence, aggravated conduct]
Her Honour (Magi Edwards) noted that sentencing aims relevant to family violence offences will likely include general deterrence, specific deterrence (in the case of repeat offenders), and denunciation and vindication of the victims.

### Discussion of Property Offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Property Offences - Joint Ownership] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal implications of damaging jointly owned property] [CONCEPTS: property offences, joint ownership, family violence, legal implications, co-owned property]
To allow one impunity for damaging property jointly owned would allow a person to destroy the entire house with impunity. Tenants in common -- the decision of one party to unilaterally destroy property owned in common destroys the propriety right of the other owner.

### Firearms Conditions Interpretation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Firearms Conditions - Meaning of Surrender] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Interpretation of firearms surrender conditions] [CONCEPTS: firearms conditions, surrender interpretation, legal obligations, family violence, compliance]
In the absence of a statutory definition, it is necessary to give the word its ordinary meaning, which means "the ordinary meaning conveyed by a provision having regard to its context in the Act and to the purpose or object of the Act."

### Seriousness of Family Violence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Brown v Jones - Serious Nature of Family Violence] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Judicial recognition of family violence severity] [CONCEPTS: family violence, seriousness, deterrence, judicial recognition, court system]
The nature of family or domestic violence needs to be noted and steadfastly borne in mind. "Family violence must always be regarded as a serious matter": Bonde v Ellery [2016] TASSC 43 per Brett J at [27]. 

### Physical vs Psychological Harm Consideration
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Cooper v Goodwin - Harm Evaluation] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Distinction between physical and psychological harm] [CONCEPTS: psychological harm, long-term effects, domestic violence, victim impact, legal evaluations]
The submission that the throwing of the telephone was reckless and did not cause long-term harm focuses entirely on physical and not long-term psychological harm, underscoring the nuances in domestic violence evaluations.

<!-- block_028 master -->

# Sentencing Considerations in Family Violence Cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers attitude of complainant] [CONCEPTS: family violence, sentencing, complainant attitude, rehabilitation, statutory provisions] [CITATION: hoc-b028-m001-sentencing-considerations] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Brown v Jones [2021] TASSC 58]

As to the attitude of the complainant, to the extent it was suggested it was one of forgiveness, it carries little weight: *Director of Public Prosecutions v Chatters* [2011] TASCCA 8, [21 Tas R 26] at [60], *Menichelli v Tasmania* [2009] TASSC 111, [19 Tas R 299] at [16]. In both cases reference was made to *R v Palu* [2002] NSWCCA 381, [134 A Crim R 174] in which the New South Wales Court of Appeal said the attitude of the victim cannot be allowed to interfere with the proper exercise of the sentencing discretion. The Court went on to say that sentencing proceedings are not a private matter between the victim and the offender, and that a serious crime is a wrong committed against the community at large, and the community itself is entitled to retribution. See also *R v Burton* [2008] NSWCCA 128 at [102]–[106].

That matter of established principle has particular application in cases of family violence. I would respectfully agree with the following comments of Pearce J (with whom Blow CJ and Tennent J agreed) in *Director of Public Prosecutions (Acting) v JCN* [2015] TASFC 13 at [20]:

"The response to family violence is often complex. Family violence offences are not uncommonly accompanied by support of a perpetrator by a victim and reluctance on the part of the victim to assist a criminal prosecution. That is so for a range of possible factors including fear and a wish to preserve relationships, even dysfunctional and violent ones, for the sake of loyalty, affection, companionship, economic and domestic support. Sometimes those motivations are misguided but persist nevertheless. As a result, victims sometimes act in a way that seems to an objective observer to be incongruent and difficult to understand."

---

# Threshold for Family Violence Orders under Section 29A
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: statutory threshold for declarations under s29A] [CONCEPTS: FVO, statutory threshold, risk factors, sentencing, domestic violence] [CITATION: hoc-b028-m002-threshold-s29a] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

In relation to the declarations under s29A and the associated provisions, Her Honour noted that the statutory threshold is *not a high one*. Both sides should identify when this is triggered and be in a position to address the Court as to whether a declaration is warranted. Section 29A(3) identifies the matters to which the Court is to have regard, but gives a wide scope to that enquiry, noting that s29A(3)(d) allows the Court to look at any other matter which it considers to be relevant.

Her Honour provided a (non-exhaustive) list of matters that she will look at, noting that other Magistrates may consider different factors to be relevant, as follows:
- Gravity and seriousness of the matters on which the defendant is currently being sentenced.
- Risk factors (prior matters, any reports available relating to the defendant).
- Antecedents -- in particular, the gravity of any previous, like offending.
- Whether there is, or has been, more than a single complainant -- a propensity to behave violently in a relationship elevates the risk.
- Escalation in offending.
- Persistence in offending, especially if previous sentences have failed to deter the defendant.
- Personal circumstances.
- Any recognition or insight by the defendant into their behaviour.
- Any attempt to hide the offending conduct.
- A lack of remorse, e.g. if the matter proceeded to hearing.

Her Honour clarified that, from her perspective, she considers the threshold can be met by three (or more) offences being dealt with at court on the same day, even in the absence of prior matters (provided the offences themselves have occurred on different dates). Her Honour also indicated she would be receptive to submissions regarding the length of a declaration, or a further declaration by way of an effective extension (if an existing serial family violence perpetrator is being sentenced for new offending); also to deferring the question of whether to make the declaration or not, if defence are seeking the opportunity to demonstrate rehabilitation.

---

# Recent Case Law Impacting Sentencing in Family Violence Context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Miller v McCullogh factors in family violence sentencing] [CONCEPTS: rehabilitation, sentencing, appeal, family violence, physical violence] [CITATION: hoc-b028-m003-miller-mccullogh] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Miller v McCullogh [2024] TASSC 61]

**Miller v McCullogh [2024] TASSC 61 (11 November 2024)** - Held: Def had taken appreciable rehabilitation steps between sentencing and offending, but given severity of physical violence in a FV context, appeal against term of imprisonment was rejected.

Facts: Def charged with multiple assaults -- non-strangulation holding of neck, and emotional abuse. Def had priors for assault against the victim, assault generally and breaching orders. Had pleaded guilty before the pre-set hearing date and had voluntarily engaged in counselling to address issues before sentencing.

As highlighted by counsel for the respondent, the maximum penalty for the Family Violence Act offence is two years' imprisonment. The other three all attract a maximum of 12 months. The applicant had prior convictions for violence, the most recent in April 2020 which led to a suspended prison term. When considering his earlier offending, in particular the family violence offences in 2019, it might be thought that the applicant may have been motivated to seek assistance with his mental health and alcohol abuse issues before being charged in August 2023 with the present matters, and his relationship ending as a result of his conduct.

In any event, the fact remains that he did seek assistance both in relation to his mental health and specifically his alcohol abuse. Those rehabilitation attempts were pursued until he was remanded in custody on 22 April 2024. That fact and the potential continued rehabilitation efforts were not matters that had no significance in the sentencing process. Additionally, the applicant was entitled to some, but in the circumstances no great, credit for his pleas of guilty. The magistrate accepted an expression of remorse but treated it as a "late development". No doubt that was prompted by the initial pleas of not guilty and the very late change. Remorse seems first to have been expressed to Dr Little and given the developing insight in the sessions with him, it is reasonable to accept that it was then genuine.

---

# Considerations of Children in Family Violence Sentencing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: children as aggravating factors in family violence cases] [CONCEPTS: children, aggravating factors, domestic violence, psychological impact, family violence orders] [CITATION: hoc-b028-m004-children-considerations] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: Allen v Kerr [2009] TASSC 10]

The presence and involvement of the children was "obviously" an aggravating factor. It caused great upset and no doubt ongoing anguish. Living arrangements were altered and the children's routine no doubt disrupted. As to this factor, I venture to repeat what I said in *Allen v Kerr* [2009] TASSC 10 at [13]: Porter AJ

"The reasons that the presence of children is an aggravating factor of some significance are largely self-evident. Violence witnessed by children in the domestic environment not only is distressing (usually the victim is a parent or someone in the place of a parent), but it also serves to desensitise impressionable minds to violence, and to encourage the notion that resort to violence is acceptable."

---

# Jurisdiction of Justices under Family Violence Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Jurisdiction of justices under the Family Violence Act] [CONCEPTS: Justices, jurisdiction, Family Violence Act, bail, interim FVO] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m001-jurisdiction-justices-fva] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

The court, when constituted by one or more justices (one of whom is not a magistrate), may exercise only the following jurisdiction under the Family Violence Act 2004:

(a) the granting or refusal of bail under section 12 of that Act;

(b) the making, variation, extension or revocation of an interim FVO under section 23 of that Act;

(c) the revocation (but not the making, variation or extension) of an FVO under section 20 of that Act.

# Standing in Private Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Standing of police in family violence proceedings] [CONCEPTS: standing, family violence, McLean v Kemp, police authority, application] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m002-standing-police-family-violence] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: McLean v Kemp [2005] TASSC 100]

Generally none. However, there is authority that had deemed that police were not a party to family violence proceedings and that the Act was amended thereafter to permit it. This was the decision of the Supreme Court in *McLean v Kemp* [2005] TASSC 100. Parliament responded by introducing s31 (2A) to remedy the situation.

An ambiguity arises if similarly, to *Lacroix v Lacroix* [2015] TASSC 42 and *Lacroix v Lacroix* [2018] TASFC 8, a fresh application, rather than extension/variation, is filed.

It may be that given that under s14(8) the PFVO is revoked, making of an IFVO or FVO, an application under s15 is to be construed in these situations to also be made pursuant to Part 3. This would mean that s31(2A) would engage the Police as a party.

Otherwise, the situation would be that a PFVO could be weakened significantly by the making of a basic FVO without further input from the Commissioner for Police, despite a police officer being satisfied that a PFVO on particular terms was required to protect the safety, wellbeing, and interests of the protected party and/or any affected children.

# Revocation of PFVO vs FVO
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Court considerations for revocation of PFVO compared to FVO] [CONCEPTS: PFVO, FVO, revocation, family violence, court's considerations] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m003-revocation-pfvo-vs-fvo] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

Not the same as the making of an FVO -- an FVO needs to be satisfied that family violence (not merely an offence) has occurred and that family violence (not merely an offence) will happen again. The revocation of a PFVO has no such indicia. The court may make an FVO at the hearing of revocation of a FVO -- then bound by those considerations -- but if not being asked to make one, or electing its own jurisdiction, the question is only whether to revoke a PFVO.

I accept that this is not necessarily an unreasonable basis for the question to be determined, but that has not been provided for under the ACT. Section 14 should be considered in light of the objects of the Act -- in the administration of the FV Act, the safety, psychological wellbeing, and interests of people affected by family violence are the paramount considerations.

This is consistent with the indicia an Inspector of Tas police must consider, not the convenience of the Respondent. The order was validly made. There can be no further challenge to its validity. Where there is recent physical violence only, in my submission, strong psychological evidence supporting the contention that the protected party's mental wellbeing would be adversely affected by the continuation of the PFVO would justify its revocation -- and none has been submitted.

# Authority and Delegation of PFVO
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Authority to issue PFVO and delegation] [CONCEPTS: PFVO, issuance, authority, delegation, Commissioner of Police] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m004-authority-delegation-pfvo] [ACT: Police Service Act] [CASE: None]

PFVO can only be issued by an officer of the rank of Sergeant or above, or authorised by the Commissioner of Police. There is a power vested in the Commissioner of Police, under a section of the *Police Service Act*, to appoint an officer to temporarily act in a higher rank, and such officer, so appointed, is vested with all the powers inherent in that role/rank (per s 34 Police Service Act 2003).

There is yet another section in the *Act* that allows the Commissioner to delegate any vested power (section 8). Finally, there is an evidentiary provision in the *Act* that allows a certificate declaring that such a delegation had occurred to be tendered as evidence of such (s 95 Police Service Act). There is a power of delegation document on record. This document is to Inspectors or higher (on file). *23AA Acts interpretation Act* TAS states that reference to a rank is sufficient -- need not be a person.

# Conditions of PFVO
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Court considers conditions of PFVO] [CONCEPTS: PFVO, conditions, family violence, restrictions, legal enforcement] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m005-conditions-pfvo] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

Limited to the confines of the permitted restrictions under s 14. A PFVO may require the person to whom it is issued to do any or all of the following:

(a) vacate any premises, whether or not that person has a legal or equitable interest in the premises;

(b) not enter any premises or only enter premises on certain conditions, whether or not that person has a legal or equitable interest in the premises;

(c) surrender any firearm or other weapon;

(d) refrain from harassing, threatening, verbally abusing or assaulting an affected person, affected child or other person named in the order;

(e) not approach, within a specified distance, an affected person, an affected child, other person named in the order, or certain premises;

(f) refrain from contacting an affected person, affected child, or other person named in the order directly or indirectly or otherwise than under specified conditions.

# Hearing of PFVO Revocation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Court's consideration when hearing PFVO revocation] [CONCEPTS: PFVO, revocation, hearing, family violence, evidence standards] [CITATION: hoc-b029-m006-hearing-pfvo-revocation] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [CASE: None]

Revocation of a PFVO is determined differently than the making of an FVO. The FVO needs to be satisfied that family violence (not merely an offence) has occurred and will happen again. The implications of this distinction are significant in how the court approaches the question of revocation. The court must consider whether any new evidence or substantial change in circumstances justifies the revocation of the PFVO that had initially been established for protective purposes.

The court may make an FVO at the hearing of the revocation of a FVO, thus binding to those considerations. However, when it is solely determining whether to revoke a PFVO, the parameters are less stringent, as the court focus does not require establishing the potential for future violence as a basis for revocation. It highlights the distinction in evaluative criteria that govern whether a PFVO can be rescinded as opposed to when an FVO is sought or considered.

<!-- block_029 procedure -->

### Jurisdiction of Justices
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 12, 20, 23] [CITATION: Justices Rules 2003 Reg 54T - Jurisdiction of Justices] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Jurisdiction of justices under Family Violence Act] [CONCEPTS: jurisdiction, justices, Family Violence Act, bail, interim FVO]
The jurisdiction of justices when constituted by one or more justices (one of whom is not a magistrate) is limited to the following under the Family Violence Act 2004:

1. The granting or refusal of bail under section 12 of that Act.
2. The making, variation, extension or revocation of an interim FVO under section 23 of that Act.
3. The revocation (but not the making, variation or extension) of an FVO under section 20 of that Act.

### Authority in Private Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 31(2A)] [CITATION: Justices Rules 2003 - Authority in Private Applications] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Authority of justices in private applications] [CONCEPTS: authority, private applications, Family Violence Act, police involvement, McLean v Kemp]
Generally none, however, there is authority deeming police were not a party to family violence proceedings, leading to amendments. Reference is made to McLean v Kemp [2005] TASSC 100, where the Supreme Court introduced s31 (2A) to allow police participation. Ambiguity arises if a fresh application rather than extension/variation is filed.

### Legal Commentary on McLean v Kemp
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: McLean v Kemp Commentary] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal commentary on McLean v Kemp] [CONCEPTS: McLean v Kemp, significant change, leave to appeal, relevant circumstances]
Decision made in the context of granting leave to hear the application. The court determined there must be substantial change in circumstances to render a leave application permissible. The terms "substantial change" and "relevant circumstances" suggest that a significant change must be established.

### Family Court Orders
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: 68] [CITATION: Family Court Orders - Definition] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Family Court Orders under Family Violence Act] [CONCEPTS: family court orders, Family Violence Act, parenting orders, powers of inconsistency]
Family court order defined in the FV Act as one made under Part 7 (children) of the Family Law Act. Other orders can be made, so always check. Family law act powers of inconsistency (e.g., 68 onwards) pertain only to Parenting orders under part 7.

### Forms of FVO, PFVO and Applications

An FVO, a PFVO or an application under this Act is to be in a form approved by the Chief Magistrate.

### Requirements for Hearings and Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Justices Rules 2003 - Hearing Applications] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Hearing applications under Family Violence Act] [CONCEPTS: hearings, applications, service, affidavit, Justices Rules]
At the hearing of an application under this Part, evidence may be given by affidavit. A person making an affidavit is not required to attend unless required by a party or the justice.

### Affidavit Requirements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Magistrates Court (Civil Division) Rules 1998] [SECTION: 55, 56] [CITATION: Affidavit Requirements - Magistrates Court Rules] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Contents and form of affidavits] [CONCEPTS: affidavits, Magistrates Court, contents, form, sworn evidence]
An affidavit must:
1. Specify the title of the action.
2. Be drawn up in the first person.
3. Contain descriptive elements about the affiant including name, address, and occupation.
4. Be divided into numbered paragraphs relating to the subject matter.
5. Contain a statement signed by the person before whom it was sworn.

The act provides a rebuttable presumption of validity for PFVO issuance. This presumption confirms that an order is presumed made and valid and arises from section 35 (3) of the Family Violence Act.

6. Refrain from contacting affected parties under conditions.

Revocation of a PFVO is not the same as making an FVO. The court does not need to satisfy indicators applicable to FVO unless asked to issue one during PFVO revocation.

# Victim Impact Statements: Legislation and Presentation Guidelines
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: Victim impact statements under the Sentencing Act 1997] [CONCEPTS: victim impact statement, sentencing, emotional damage, evidentiary limitations, presentation guidelines] [CITATION: hoc-b030-m001-victim-impact-statements] [ACT: Sentencing Act 1997] [CASE: Belbin v Bennett 2011 TASSC]

After a person has been found guilty of, or has pleaded guilty to, an indictable offence the law provides that the victim of the offence can make a victim impact statement (s81A of the Sentencing Act 1997). In respect of a summary offence, a VIS can be provided where death or serious injury has occurred as a result of the offence, or if it is a family violence offence. This is often the only opportunity for the victim to have their say. A VIS is entirely voluntary and if the victim feels it would be more traumatic to go through the process of preparing one then there is no obligation for them to do so. On the other hand, some people find the process of preparing and presenting a VIS therapeutic and want to have a role in the process. The decision whether or not to prepare a VIS can be discussed with a WAS officer who can also assist with the preparation of the document. Victim Support Services can also provide assistance with the preparation of a VIS.

Prosecutors should be aware that a VIS is not evidence to show that a victim is suffering from a psychiatric illness or disorder as a result of the crime. Where necessary, a report should be obtained from a relevant expert.

There are some limitations as to what can be included in a VIS. The statement must be limited to details relating only to the crime of which the offender is convicted. Following are some pointers to assist in the preparation of a VIS. It should include:

• details of any physical injuries caused by the crime and the impact of these injuries

• details of emotional and psychological damage caused by the crime

• details of any changes in behaviour, thoughts, attitudes, coping skills, social life or relationships with others (i.e. how life was before the crime and how it has changed)

• details of the impact of the crime on employment, education or loss of future prospects

• details of the financial impact of the crime

• relevant medical or psychological reports that support the VIS

It should not include:

• any abusive or derogatory comments about the accused

• any impact that is not the result of the crime concerned

• any conduct of which the accused has not been convicted

• any suggestion as to what the sentence should be

Presenting a VIS to the court entails three options. The first option is for the written document to be handed up to the judge for private reading. Some people prefer this because no-one else in the courtroom will hear it. However, it must be borne in mind that a VIS is not an automatically confidential document. A copy must be given to the accused's lawyer so the accused will also see it. Also, judges regularly use extracts from a VIS in their sentencing comments.

The second option is for the victim to read their VIS to the court.

The third option is for the victim to nominate another person to read the VIS on their behalf.

When reading out a VIS, it should be addressed to the judge or magistrate, not directly to the accused. If defence counsel challenge any material in the VIS, it will be necessary to discuss this with the victim. It is possible (though very unusual) that the victim may have to go into the witness box in order to prove the material.

<!-- block_030 procedure -->

### Victim Impact Statements - Overview
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Sentencing Act 1997] [SECTION: 81A] [CITATION: Sentencing Act 1997 s 81A - Overview] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Overview of Victim Impact Statements] [CONCEPTS: victim impact statement, sentencing, voluntary, psychological impact, legal process]  
Victim impact statements are a crucial part of the legal process after a person has been found guilty of, or has pleaded guilty to, an indictable offence. The law provides that the victim of the offence can make a victim impact statement (s 81A of the Sentencing Act 1997). For summary offences, a VIS can be provided in cases of death, serious injury, or in family violence offences. This often is the only opportunity for the victim to express their perspective. A VIS is entirely voluntary; if the victim feels it would be more traumatic to prepare one, there's no obligation to do so. Conversely, some may find preparing and presenting a VIS therapeutic and prefer to participate.

### Limitations on Content of Victim Impact Statements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Sentencing Act 1997] [SECTION: 81A] [CITATION: Sentencing Act 1997 s 81A - Content Limitations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Limitations on what can be included in a VIS] [CONCEPTS: limitations, content, legal requirements, details, inappropriate content]  
There are limitations on what can be included in a VIS. The statement must be confined to details relating only to the crime of which the offender is convicted. The following points should assist in preparation:  
- Include details of any physical injuries caused by the crime and the impact of these injuries.  
- Include details of emotional and psychological damage caused by the crime.  
- Include changes in behavior, thoughts, attitudes, coping skills, and relationships (before and after the crime).  
- Include impact on employment, education, or loss of future prospects.  
- Include financial impact details.  
- Attach relevant medical or psychological reports supporting the VIS.

### Presentation Options for Victim Impact Statements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Sentencing Act 1997] [SECTION: 81A] [CITATION: Sentencing Act 1997 s 81A - Presentation Options] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: How to present a VIS to the court] [CONCEPTS: presentation, options, confidentiality, procedural rules, courtroom dynamics]  
There are three options for presenting a VIS to the court:  
1. The victim may provide the written document for the judge to read privately. Note: A VIS is not automatically confidential; a copy must be provided to the accused's lawyer, so the accused sees it, and judges may reference it in their sentencing comments.  
2. The victim can read their VIS directly to the court.  
3. Another person can be nominated by the victim to read the VIS on their behalf.  
When reading a VIS, it should be addressed to the judge or magistrate, not directly to the accused. If defence counsel challenges any material in the VIS, this should be discussed with the victim. There’s a rare possibility that the victim may need to go into the witness box to prove the content. 

### Legislative References for Victim Impact Statements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Sentencing Act 1997] [SECTION: 81A] [CITATION: Sentencing Act 1997 s 81A - Legislative References] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Statutory context for Victim Impact Statements] [CONCEPTS: legislation, victim rights, judicial procedures, statutory authority, rules of court]  
**81A. Court may receive victim impact statement**  
1. In this section --  
   - immediate family, in respect of a deceased victim, includes --  
       a. the spouse or partner of the deceased victim;  
       b. a parent, guardian, or step-parent of the deceased victim;  
       c. a child or stepchild of the deceased victim;  
       d. a brother, sister, stepbrother, or stepsister of the deceased victim;  
   - indictable offence means --  
       a. an offence punishable on indictment even if it may be dealt with summarily;  
       b. any other offence prescribed for the purposes of this section;  
   - victim, in regards to an offence, means --  
       a. a person who has suffered injury, loss, or damage as a direct consequence of the offence;  
       b. a member of the immediate family of a deceased victim of the offence.  

2. If a court finds a person guilty of an indictable offence, a summary offence resulting in death or serious injury, or a family violence offence, the victim may furnish a written statement that details injury, loss, or damage and describes the effects of the offence.

### Excerpt from DPP Guidelines on Strangulation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Strangulation] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: DPP guidelines on strangulation and related charges] [CONCEPTS: strangulation, DPP guidelines, family violence, legal considerations, prosecutorial discretion]  
The following is an excerpt from the DPP guidelines. This is crucial when reviewing charges, especially in out-of-hours courts. Assault cases involving choking, smothering, or strangulation, particularly in a family violence context, should be regarded as grave criminal conduct. Even without injury, a charge of assault under s 184 of the Criminal Code should be considered. Refer to Mayne v Tasmania [2017] TASSC 38, which provides guidance on recognizing the seriousness of smothering as a violent act that warrants an indictable charge.

### Considerations for Charging Strangulation or Suffocation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code] [SECTION: 184] [CITATION: Criminal Code s 184 - Charging Considerations] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Factors in charging for strangulation or suffocation] [CONCEPTS: charging decisions, strangulation, suffocation, legal factors, prosecutorial guidelines]  
When determining whether to charge an accused with summary or indictable assault in relation to strangulation or suffocation, consider:  
- The duration of the strangulation or suffocation.  
- Whether the victim lost consciousness or sustained injuries.  
- Any additional violence.  
- Accompanying threats to kill.  
- The relationship history.  
- The presence of children during the incident.  
If a person chokes, suffocates, or strangles another to incapacitate them with intent to commit or flee an offence, a charge under s 168 of the Criminal Code is preferred. The choice of charge category is a matter of judgment, and the Office welcomes discussions about charging decisions before they are made.

### Case Authority Referencing Strangulation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code] [SECTION: 184] [CITATION: Mayne v Tasmania [2017] TASSC 38 - Case Authority] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Judicial remarks on strangulation sentencing] [CONCEPTS: case law, sentencing remarks, strangulation, judicial considerations, violence]  
In Mayne v Tasmania [2017] TASSC 38, the court highlighted the implications of a single act of smothering:  
> "A relevant consideration is whether the sentence impinges upon the sentencing range for far more serious acts of smothering. I do not consider that that is the case. Conduct involving persistent smothering and loss of consciousness should properly be regarded as grave acts of violence. It would warrant an indictable charge of assault contrary to the Criminal Code. Without being prescriptive, such cases may attract a very lengthy term of imprisonment, more than the maximum penalty of two years' imprisonment as an aggravated assault under the Police Offences Act."

### Definition of Breach of the Peace
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Common Law] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Breach of the Peace Definition] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definition and legal basis of breach of peace] [CONCEPTS: breach of peace, definition, public order, disorder, R v Howell, legal context]
Breach of the peace is not an offence, but instead it is the basis for the execution of the common law power which prevents public disorder. Furthermore, breach of the peace is one element of other public order offences such as unlawful assembly or riot. In attempting to establish what breach of the peace entails, we can turn to the leading authority of the concept which can be found in *R v Howell*, where Watkins LJ in the English Court of Appeal, defined breach of the peace in the following way: 
***"There is breach of the peace whenever harm is actually done or is likely to be done to a person or in his presence to his property or a person is in fear of being so harmed through an assault, an affray, a riot, unlawfully assembly or other disturbance."*** *R v Howell [1981] 3 All ER 383* Cited in *Cintana v Burgoyne A Crim R 296 at para 13-19*. 

---

### Procedure in Hearing Breach Applications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act] [SECTION: 106E] [CITATION: Justices Act s 106E - Procedure for Hearing Breach Applications] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Procedure for hearing and determining applications for restraint orders] [CONCEPTS: hearing applications, Justices Act, applications, open court, complainant treatment]
**106E. Procedure in relation to hearing and determining applications under this Part**
(1) Except as otherwise provided by this Act or ordered by justices -- 
> \(a\) an application under this Part is to be heard and determined -- 
> \(i\) in open court; and 
> \(ii\) in the same manner, as nearly as practicable, as a complaint for a simple offence; and 
> \(b\) a person who makes an application under this Part is to be treated, as nearly as practicable, as a complainant. 
(1A) At the hearing of an application under this Part, the following persons may conduct the applicant's or respondent's case and examine and cross-examine witnesses: 
> \(a\) the applicant or respondent; 
> \(b\) a lawyer who represents the applicant or respondent; 
> \(c\) a police officer who represents the applicant or respondent. 
(1B) If a police officer could, if available as a witness, give direct oral evidence of a fact in his or her professional capacity in the hearing of an application under this Part, the statutory declaration of the police officer in relation to that fact and made in support of the application is admissible as evidence notwithstanding that the police officer may be available as a witness. 
(1C) A statutory declaration referred to in subsection (1B) is not admissible as evidence if the justices are of the opinion that the representation ought not to be admitted without being tested by cross-examination. 
(1D) The representation of a medical practitioner in a document tending to establish a fact is admissible as evidence, provided certain conditions are met.

### Driving Definition in Case Law
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Traffic Act 1925] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Driving Definition - Cooley v Lowe Analysis] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Definition and criteria for 'driving' under Tasmanian law] [CONCEPTS: driving definition, vehicle operation, Cooley v Lowe, case law, Traffic Act]
***Cooley v Lowe***
For it to be proven that a person was 'driving' a motor vehicle, the following matters are to be considered: 
- The degree to which the 'driver' was operating the controls of the motor vehicle (steering, brakes etc.)
- The distance travelled.
- The road conditions, (undulations, corners etc.)
- The speed of the vehicle.
- If the vehicle was being towed (as in this case), the length of the tow rope, communication (if any) between the vehicles, the manner in which the towed vehicle was steered and stopped. 
*... "driving"*, requires proof that the person charged *"...had the capacity to do so, and that there was at least a reasonable possibility, if not likelihood, that he would attempt to drive the vehicle and so endanger the public..."* in accordance with the Full Court decision in *Cooley v Lowe [1984] Tas R 107.*

---

### Analysis of Public Street Definition
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Traffic Act 1925] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Traffic Act 1925 - Definition of Public Street] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of public street in legal context] [CONCEPTS: public street, definition, accessibility, legal interpretation, Traffic Act]
***Public street*** means any street, road, lane, thoroughfare, footpath, bridge, or place open to or used by the public, or to which the public have or are permitted to have access, whether on payment of a fee or otherwise.

# Interpretation of the Offence of Evading Police
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers proper interpretation of evade police provision] [CONCEPTS: evade police, statutory interpretation, apprehension, legislative intention, police powers] [CITATION: hoc-b032-m001-interpretation-evade-police] [ACT: Police Powers (Vehicle Interception) Act 2000] [CASE: A v Roughan [2019] TASSC 17]

The relevant provision of s 11A(1) is as follows:

'(1) The driver of a vehicle must not take action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer who is exercising his or her powers or performing his or her functions under any Act.'

The submissions of each counsel were based on what was contended to be the proper interpretation of this provision. The starting point of statutory interpretation must be to give legal effect according to the plain and ordinary meaning of the text, read in the context of the surrounding provisions and the legislative scheme: Project Blue Sky Inc v Australian Broadcasting Authority [1998] HCA 28; (1998) 194 CLR 355. Although the provision is inelegantly drafted, there does not seem to be any great difficulty in attributing meaning to the simple words contained in s 11A(1). Accordingly, in order to establish the offence of evade police, the prosecution must prove that:

(a) the driver of a vehicle;

(b) has taken action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer;

(c) who is exercising his or her powers or performing his or her functions under any Act.

The term 'driver' is defined by s 3 of the Act to mean 'a person having control over the steering, movement or propulsion of a vehicle and includes a person riding a vehicle'. This definition suggests a clear nexus between the driving of the vehicle and the taking of action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer. This conclusion is consistent with the long title of the Act which is as follows:

'An Act to confer additional powers on police officers to stop and search vehicles and to obtain information concerning the identity of drivers and owners of, and passengers in or on, vehicles used in connection with the commission of certain offences, and to provide for related matters.'

I am not satisfied, however, that the nexus requires the evasion be constituted solely by an act of driving, nor that the evasion and the driving must be contemporaneous and co-extensive. The requirement is that the driver of the vehicle takes action to avoid apprehension or interception. Taking action can involve a single act or a course of conduct. The use of that term without qualification is consistent with a legislative intention to prohibit any form of action which is intended to achieve evasion of a police officer who is acting within the law. The focus is not on the nature or effectiveness of the action, but rather its purpose. It is immaterial that the action is, or is not likely to be successful, in achieving the evasion of the police officer. In the second reading speech which accompanied the amending Act which inserted s 11A into the Act, the then Minister for Police and Emergency Management gave examples of the type of situation in which the section might operate:

'This provision will apply to situations where a driver becomes aware of a police presence and then takes action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer. An example of such an offence includes drivers who turn their vehicle around, or pull over and run from their vehicle, when they are approaching a random breath test site, or drives in such a manner as to attempt to evade, elude and or prevent police from speaking to the driver or occupants.'

Accordingly, provided that the Court is satisfied that the action taken by the driver of a vehicle is for the purpose of avoiding apprehension or interception by a police officer, it is not necessary that the evasion occur by the driving of the vehicle, nor that the vehicle is being driven throughout the act of evasion. It is necessary only that the purpose of the action taken be to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer. The required nexus between the driving of the vehicle and the taking of action to avoid apprehension or interception will be satisfied if the Court can conclude that the action was taken by a person in his capacity as a driver of the vehicle, but this does not necessarily mean that he or she must actually be driving the vehicle at all times, or even at any time, throughout the evasion. Generally, as a practical consequence of this nexus, some contemporaneity can be expected between the actual driving of the vehicle and the action in question. However, because the taking of action can be constituted by a course of conduct, it is not necessary that the entirety of the relevant action be constituted by the driving of the vehicle. Hence, as in the example given by the Minister in the second reading speech, it would be sufficient if the driver has stopped the vehicle and decamped from it in order to avoid apprehension or interception. It is also possible to contemplate circumstances in which the evasive action commences after the driving has come to an end. An example might be a driver who leaves the scene immediately after an accident, for the purpose of evading apprehension by police who are approaching or already present at the scene, but who prior to the driving coming to an end upon the occurrence of the accident was not evading police.

The requirement that the evasion be of a police officer performing his or her functions under any Act, imports a requirement that the taking of action must be for the purpose of avoiding apprehension or interception by an actual police officer. The prosecution must prove that there is a police officer, that he or she is exercising power or performing functions under any Act, and that it is the apprehension or interception by that police officer that the defendant has taken action to evade. It would not be sufficient, for example, that the person believes that police might be in the area when that is not actually the case. On the other hand, it is not necessary for that police officer to have formed an intention to, nor be actually attempting to apprehend or intercept the driver. The power or function does not necessarily need to relate to the apprehension or interception of the driver. The requirement that the driver take action to avoid apprehension or interception by the police officer refers to the subjective purpose of the driver and does not rely upon or circumscribe the intentions of the police officer. It must be independently shown that the police officer is exercising powers or functions under an Act, and provided that is shown, it is irrelevant whether the driver has correctly determined that the police officer intends to apprehend or intercept him or her. The offence is established when the driver of the vehicle takes action for the purpose of avoiding apprehension or interception, whether or not that prospect is, in fact, about to occur.

# Aggravated Evade Police: Driver's Perspective
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers actions constituting aggravated evade police] [CONCEPTS: aggravated evade, police interception, course of conduct, driving actions, apprehension] [CITATION: hoc-b032-m002-aggravated-evade-police] [ACT: Police Powers (Vehicle Interception) Act 2000] [CASE: Police v Quinn [2019] TASMC 4]

Most circumstances that lead to a 2A charge must exist when driver is taking action to avoid whilst driving. So, let's say he is driving, Cop lights go on. He pulls over and gets out of the car THEN takes action to evade by running away, it's not an Agg evade for most of the 2A circumstances, as "the ACT" needs to occur whilst driving. Unless you can support the contention that the pulling over of the car was part of a course of conduct to evade. 

The above was just comments by the chief magistrate.

# Sentencing Framework for Aggravated Evade Police
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers sentencing principles for aggravated evade police] [CONCEPTS: sentencing, public safety, police pursuit, deterrent, legislative intent] [CITATION: hoc-b032-m003-sentencing-aggravated-evade] [ACT: Police Powers (Vehicle Interception) Act 2000] [CASE: Murray v Tasmania Police [2022] TASSC 1]

The magistrate correctly identified the purpose of the law as one to protect the public against the risk involved in a police pursuit and to facilitate the enforcement of the law. When subs (2A) was inserted into s 11A of the Act, the penalties for a breach of s 11A(1) -- evading police -- were increased. In the second reading speech, the Minister noted that evading police had emerged as a significant safety problem for Tasmania Police and the whole Tasmanian community, and referred to the need for there "to be a more significant deterrent, as drivers should not see any advantage in evading police, and repeat offenders should be discouraged from undertaking a cost-benefit analysis of not stopping."

The penalties prescribed by [s 11A(2A)](https://www6.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ppia2000358/s11a.html) of the *Police Powers (Vehicle Interception) Act 2000* are indicative of the Parliamentary view of this type of offending. For a first offence, the penalty is a fine of not less than 20 penalty units and not more than 100 units, or a term of imprisonment not exceeding three years or both. For a second or subsequent offence, the penalty is a fine within the same range or a term of imprisonment for not more than five years, or both. Such legislative changes and the maximum penalties set for a particular offence provide "legislative guideposts" to a court, and are matters to which a court should have regard: *Muldrock v The Queen* [2011] HCA 39; [244 CLR 120], *Banks v Tasmania* [2019] TASCCA 1; [31 Tas R 342].

### Case Authority Discussion on Evade Police
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Powers (Vehicle Interception) Act 2000] [SECTION: 11A] [CITATION: A v Roughan [2019] TASSC 17 - Meaning of the Offence] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Interpretation of evade police offence] [CONCEPTS: evade police, statutory interpretation, legal principles, court decisions, police powers]

**A v Roughan \[2019\] TASSC 17**, particularly at \[11\]-\[16\], Brett J dealt with the meaning of the provision creating this offence, and he set out the applicable principles which are binding on my determination of this case:

\[11\] The relevant provision of s 11A(1) is as follows:

\'(1) The driver of a vehicle must not take action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer who is exercising his or her powers or performing his or her functions under any Act.\'

\[12\] The submissions of each counsel were based on what was contended to be the proper interpretation of this provision. The starting point of statutory interpretation must be to give legal effect according to the plain and ordinary meaning of the text, read in the context of the surrounding provisions and the legislative scheme: Project Blue Sky Inc v Australian Broadcasting Authority \[1998\] HCA 28; (1998) 194 CLR 355. Although the provision is inelegantly drafted, there does not seem to be any great difficulty in attributing meaning to the simple words contained in s 11A(1). Accordingly, in order to establish the offence of evade police, the prosecution must prove that:

\(a\) the driver of a vehicle;

\(b\) has taken action to avoid apprehension or interception by a police officer;

**(c) who is exercising his or her powers or performing his or her functions under any Act.**

...

\[16\] The requirement that the evasion be of a police officer performing his or her functions under any Act, imports a requirement that the taking of action must be for the purpose of avoiding apprehension or interception by an actual police officer[. **The prosecution must prove that there is a police officer, that he or she is exercising power or performing functions under any Act**]{.mark}, and that it is the apprehension or interception by that police officer that the defendant has taken action to evade...

---

So, lets say he is driving, Cop lights go on. He pulls over and gets out of the car THEN takes action to evade by running away, it's not an Agg evade for most of the 2A circumstances, as "the ACT" needs to occur WHILST driving.
