<!-- block_033 master -->

# Passive Ingestion of Methylamphetamine
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: passive inhalation of methylamphetamine vapour] [CONCEPTS: methylamphetamine, vapour, passive inhalation, scientific evidence, physical characteristics] [CITATION: hoc-b033-m001-passive-inhalation-meth] [ACT: None] [CASE: None]

Methylamphetamine is heated to become a vapour which is then actively breathed into the lungs. It does not exist in the air, like smoke from cigarettes or cannabis -- therefore it cannot be inhaled passively. There is no scientific literature or research into passive methylamphetamine exposure, as it is just not possible to passively inhale methylamphetamine vapour, from our understanding. It will be difficult to write a proof for this, given there is no scientific evidence, but the physical characteristics of methylamphetamine mean that it does not exist in a gas form, it has to be heated to form a vapour.

Further to this, the only other information that I believe could be of help to you is that methylamphetamine hydrochloride (commonly referred to as crystal meth) the form typically "smoked" has a melting point of between 170-175 degrees. Obviously, it will vaporise above this temperature but this gives an indication that methamphetamine is not just floating around in the air of a room at ambient temperature when people are using it. It will, in fact, easily condense on the cooler parts of the glass smoking devices used before it has any chance of "escaping" into a room -- at any significant/detectable level. Meth and THC differ considerably in how they are "smoked". Cannabis is typically literally burnt as per a cigarette so that the smoke is inhaled/exhaled. Meth is heated until it changes from a solid to a liquid to a gas and this is what is inhaled. Smoke from a cannabis joint can be long lasting (given the right conditions e.g. lack of ventilation), meth vapour is not the same.

---

# Drug Pattern Defence and Timing of Drug Use
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: timing of drug use and evidence standard] [CONCEPTS: THC, drug use timing, mistake of fact, De Wit v Ali, expert evidence] [CITATION: hoc-b033-m002-timing-drug-use] [ACT: None] [CASE: De Wit v Ali [2021] TASMC 10]

THC positive usually indicates use within 6 hours. After that usually undetectable regardless of subjective characteristics. This raises issues around a mistake of fact for timing of drug use, as observed in the case of **De Wit v Ali [2021] TASMC 10** where the defendant argued that 4 days had passed since use. Expert evidence indicated that anything beyond 6 hours would likely meet the cutoff for analysis, thereby potentially affecting the admissibility of evidence regarding the defendant's drug use.

---

# FSST Analyst Evidence Questions in EIC
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: examination-in-chief questions for FSST analyst] [CONCEPTS: FSST analyst, evidence, blood sample, Road Safety Act, obligations] [CITATION: hoc-b033-m003-fsst-eic-questions] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: None]

Q -- Can you please tell me your name and occupation please?  
A -- My name is ........... and I am a Forensic Scientist employed by Forensic Science Service Tasmania.  

Q -- Can you please tell me what your duties at FSST are?  
A -- My duties include but are not limited to receiving, preparing and analysing Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 blood samples for alcohol and/or illicit drugs as well as the preparation and review of reports for Police and the Coroner.  

Q -- Please tell me what qualifications you have to carry out those duties.  
A -- Detail education and experience etc. and the fact that you are an 'approved analyst' pursuant to the Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970, such appointment being declared by the Governor in the Government Gazette on .../.../... (date).  

**Just the month and year will be fine if you don't recall the exact date.**  

Q -- You are aware that you have been summoned to give evidence in relation to ..... .... (this defendant) and the analysis of his/her blood sample?  
A -- Yes  

Q -- Do you have an independent recollection of what you did in relation to the defendant's blood sample?  
A -- No I do not, as I am involved in the analysis of (....) samples of blood each year.  

Q -- Do you make notes or keep records?  
A -- Yes, we have instrument print-outs for each sample and keep a case file including all relevant paperwork including submitted forms and reports etc. which are subsequently used to create the 'Report of Analysis'.  

Q -- Have you reviewed your notes in the last 24 hours?  
A -- Yes, several times.  

Q -- Has reading your notes refreshed your memory so that you can recall what you did in relation to this defendant's blood sample?  
A -- No it has not.  

**I will seek leave for you to be able to either use that document to refresh your memory, or read from it pursuant to section 32(3) of the Evidence Act.**  

**At this point, the court will allow you to read directly from your notes or the report of analysis to give your evidence about the analysis of the blood sample.**  

---

# Requirement to Communicate "Requirement" for Breath Analysis
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: communication requirement under Road Safety Act] [CONCEPTS: communicate requirement, unlawful arrest, McLean v Wilkie, breath analysis] [CITATION: hoc-b033-m004-communicate-requirement] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: McLean v Wilkie [2022] TASSC 13]

Need to communicate requirement. Failure to do so makes it an unlawful arrest. But evidence may still be admissible under s138, as stated in **McLean v Wilkie [2022] TASSC 13**. The defendant objected to the admissibility of the result of the breath analysis on the basis that Constable Prettyman had unlawfully arrested him and failed to communicate to the defendant that he was required "to proceed to such place or into such vehicle as the officer may indicate and there submit himself to that analysis" as contemplated by the Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970, s 10(1) (the Act). The officer was also obliged to communicate the reason for the obligation. I adopted the reasoning of Wood J in **Arnesto v Hickman** as to the meaning of the word 'require' in the Police Offences Act 1935, s55A as logically applicable to the same expression in the Act, s 10(1).

---

# Refusal to Submit to Blood Test: Evidence Requirements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: refusal to submit impact on analysis admissibility] [CONCEPTS: breath analysis, blood test, needle phobia, Lyons v Dyson, driving under influence] [CITATION: hoc-b033-m005-refusal-blood-test] [ACT: None] [CASE: Lyons v Dyson [2018] TASMC]

Needle phobia no excuse. In summary, the magistrate found that satisfying the court that the defendant had a needle phobia is insufficient; he needed to satisfy the court that he had the needle phobia and that the taking of a sample of blood would have been detrimental to his physical or mental health and that appropriate interventions (such as being given a sedative to calm him) could not be used to enable the sample to be taken.

The question of whether the defendant was driving under the influence of a drug to such an extent as to be incapable of having proper control of the motor vehicle is a question of fact which must be proved beyond reasonable doubt: (see Richardson v Shipp; Pulleine v Button). If the drug in question was alcohol, then it is well established that inferences can be drawn from indicia, "certain observable abnormalities of behaviour and certain physical signs which evidence when a person who has ingested alcohol has become 'influenced' by the alcohol."

### Chunk 5: FSST Analyst Evidence on Blood Sample Analysis
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [SECTION: 13B] [CITATION: FSST Analyst Report Service] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: Evidence of FSST Analyst on report service obligations] [CONCEPTS: blood sample analysis, report service, legal obligations, peer review process, forensic procedures]  
Continuing examination:
9. Are you aware of your obligation pursuant to the Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act in relation to service of the 'Report of Analysis'?  
   A -- Yes, my obligations are that I must cause it to be served on that person within 21 days, pursuant to section 13B of the Act.  
10. Do you recall what you did with the report relating to the report in this matter?  
    A -- I do not have an independent recollection of what I did with the report as it was some time ago and I complete many such reports as part of my duties.  
11. What is your usual practice when you complete a report?  
    A -- I am one of (\...\....) scientists in FSST responsible for analysing about (\...\....) blood samples every year.  As such I do not have a direct recollection of what I did with this report. What I can say is, it is standard practice that a colleague, in this instance that was (.... ....), checks the report (peer review). The purpose for this is checking for errors and peer review.  
    I know that the peer review/checking was done because the records reflect that (.... ....) did this. I then made a copy for internal filing and placed it into the 'out' tray in the administration section so it will be posted. I am certain that I would have done this in this instance as I never deviate from this procedure after completing a Report of Analysis.

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: FSST State Servant Mailing Reports] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: Evidence of FSST State Servant on the mailing of reports] [CONCEPTS: blood analysis, report shipping, contemporaneous records, court documents, process validation]

   - Response needed.

   - Response needed.

   - Response needed.

   - Response needed.

   - Response needed.  
10. Can you look at page 4 of the document and tell me if you recognize the handwriting and signature on that page?  
    - Response needed.  
11. Does seeing the document refresh your memory enough so that you recall mailing that particular report?  
    - Response needed.  
    **If you still do not recall mailing that report (and there is no issue if you do not) I will seek leave for you to be able to either use that document to refresh your memory, or read from it pursuant to section 32 of the Evidence Act.**

### Chunk 7: McLean v Wilkie Summary
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [SECTION: 10] [CITATION: McLean v Wilkie Summary] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Legal requirement to communicate arrest conditions] [CONCEPTS: unlawful arrest, breath analysis, police communication, case authority, legal requirements]  
**McLean v Wilkie \[2022\] TASSC 13 (24 February 2022)**. Need to communicate requirement. Failure to do so makes it an unlawful arrest. But evidence may still get in under s138. \[11\] "The defendant objected to the admissibility of the result of the breath analysis on the basis that Constable Prettyman (sic), had unlawfully arrested him and failed to communicate to the defendant that he was required '...to proceed to such place or into such vehicle... as the officer may indicate and there submit himself ... to that analysis...' as contemplated by the *Road Safety Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970*, s 10(1) (the Act). The officer was also obliged to communicate the reason for the obligation. 

### Chunk 8: Refuse to Submit to Breath Analysis
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [SECTION: 7A] [CITATION: Refusal to Submit to Breath Analysis] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Requirements for breath analysis submission] [CONCEPTS: breath analysis, police requirements, statutory obligations, driver's liabilities, refusal consequences]  
1. **Refuse Breath Analysis**  
   Section 7A(1): Driver liable to submit to a breath test to a Police Officer  
   Section 7A(4): Whether a positive response or not to the breath test, if the Police officer reasonably believes alcohol may be present, they become liable to submit to a breath analysis.  
   Section 10(1): Police can require someone to go to a police station for a breath analysis.  
   Section 10(2): Can take a person into "custody" for the necessary time period to effect sub-s 1.  
   Section 10(3): Power under sub-s 2 restricted to where Police believe can be done within 3 hours after "relevant time" (defined in Act).  
   Section 10(4): Power to "direct" person to submit to breath analysis.  
   Section 10(4A): Where person fails or refuses direction in 10(4), the "approved operator" is to inform them that they may elect to take blood instead if it can be done within 3 hours of "relevant time".  
   Section 14(2): Fail or refuse to provide breath test in accordance with directions of "approved operator' is an offence.  
   Section 25(2): Evidentiary certificate as prima facie evidence contained within.  
   Section 26: Evidentiary certificate that device was working.  
   Section 29: Caveat -- certs must be served 14 days prior (was given by cops to him at the time.  
   Regulation 6: Device must have been tested within 12 months before using.  
   Regulation 7: Requirements that the approved operator must comply with when undertaking a breath analysis.  

### Chunk 9: Refusal to Submit to Blood Test
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Refusal to Submit to Blood Test - Case Summary] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Legal implications of refusing blood test] [CONCEPTS: blood test refusal, health implications, legal standards, needle phobia, case law]  
**Needle phobia no excuse.** See para 23-33 ***Lyons v Dyson*** \[2018\] TASMC. In summary, the magistrate found that satisfying the court that the defendant had a needle phobia is insufficient; he needed to satisfy the court that he had the needle phobia, and that taking a sample of blood would have been detrimental to his physical or mental health and that appropriate interventions (such as being given a sedative to calm him) could not be used to enable the sample to be taken.

### Chunk 10: Driving Under the Influence Considerations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Driving Under the Influence - Legal Considerations] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Proving driving under the influence] [CONCEPTS: driving under influence, burden of proof, observable behavior, case citations, alcohol influence]  
The question of whether the defendant was driving under the influence of a drug to such an extent as to be incapable of having proper control of the motor vehicle is a question of fact which must be proved beyond reasonable doubt: (see *Richardson v Shipp* \[1970\] TASStRp 6; *Pulleine v Button* \[1948\] SAStRp 5). If the drug in question was alcohol, then it is well established that inferences can be drawn from indicia, "certain observable abnormalities of behaviour and certain physical signs which evidence when a person who has ingested alcohol has become 'influenced' by the alcohol" (*O'Connor v Shaw* \[1958\] Qd R 384).

### Chunk 11: Requirement to Communicate Under the Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [SECTION: 10] [CITATION: Requirement to Communicate [Road Safety Act]] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Obligation to communicate requirements for analysis submission] [CONCEPTS: police obligations, communication requirement, breath analysis law, legal standards, case law]  
"... the effective communication of the requirement is a precondition to the exercise of police power to enforce the obligation on the defendant to submit to breath analysis." *[Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970, s10(1)]*. However, can argue under s 138 EA (under some circumstances) that results should be allowed in even when not communicated. **Wilkie v McLean \[2021\] TASMC 5 (4 October 2021)**.

### Chunk 14: Presumptions and Certificate Regulations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [SECTION: 29] [CITATION: Presumptions and Certificate Overview] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Certificate requirements and presumptions] [CONCEPTS: presumptions, legal certificates, service requirements, evidence handling, regulatory compliance]  
**Presumptions:**  
23A: Positive result at any time within 6 hours of relevant time of driving, is taken to have had drugs in system.  
**Certificates:**  
All subject to section 29 requiring service of certificates 14 days beforehand. Note: "Cert of service" is proof under s 29(1A). Section 29(2): Certificates can be tendered without the author being called. Note that the defendant can call the analyst if they want, and even if called by defence can be cross-examined by the defendant.

# Evidence Sequence in OFA Cases: Witness and Certificate Requirements
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: court considers the necessity of certificates for evidence] [CONCEPTS: oral evidence, certificate of service, approved operator, breath analysis, storage of samples] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m001-evidence-sequence] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: Tasmania v Billinghurst (No 1) [2018] TASSC 3]

Evidence collection in driving offences requires specific procedures and adherence to statutory requirements. Starting with the witnesses, the evidence presented must include aspects such as the intercept, driving of the motor vehicle, belief supported by positive Operating Field Test (OFT) results, and the oral testimony of an approved operator. It is also essential to note that a Form 5 document must be read over and handed to the defendant. This practice is not merely procedural but serves to ensure that the defendant is fully aware of the evidentiary process. 

Moreover, certificates play a crucial role in these cases. For example, subsection 29(1A) mandates a "certificate of service," which serves to prove that the defendant has been duly served with these certificates prior to reliance on them in court. Mere disclosure is insufficient; the delivery must be demonstrable, typically established through procedures acknowledged in the *Acts Interpretation Act*. 

In section 27A(1), a certification of taking and dealing is required. This certificate must specify the approved Bas operator's details, the sample taken, and the time of the offence. Section 27A(2) introduces a "certificate of delivery," which is critical because it reflects upon the analysis provided to the analyst, needing detailed descriptions of the marks and labels on the samples received.

Sections 28 and 28A elaborate on the "certificate of analysis" that corroborates information concerning the continuity of delivery. This is inclusive of coverage for the provisions outlined in section 19C of the Act. Each layer of evidence, from witness testimony to various certificates, underlines the importance of meticulous adherence to procedures intended to uphold the integrity of the judicial process.

# Liability Regardless of Intended Purpose in OFA Cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: liability in sham breath test situations] [CONCEPTS: sham breath tests, police powers, liability, evidentiary processes, judicial reasoning] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m002-liability-intent] [ACT: None] [CASE: Tasmania v Billinghurst (No 1) [2018] TASSC 3]

The liability in driving offences remains regardless of the intended purpose behind the actions taken. In *Tasmania v Billinghurst (No 1) [2018] TASSC 3*, the court provided a pertinent observation regarding this principle at paragraph [15]: *"... Even a sham random breath test site would not, to my mind, have involved impropriety, much less the symbiotic use of three sets of police powers via the conduit of a working random breath test site."* This statement signifies that actions taken under the guise of procedural compliance cannot absolve individuals from liability.

# Defences to Operating a Vehicle under the Influence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: onus of proof in drug or alcohol offenses] [CONCEPTS: defence, onus of proof, evidence act, Chidiac v R, Bass v Holbrow] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m003-defences-oft] [ACT: Evidence Act] [CASE: Chidiac v R (1991) 171 CLR 432; Bass v Holbrow [2011] TASMC 4 (21 April 2011)]

In defending against offences related to operating a vehicle while under the influence of drugs or alcohol, it is important to note that the onus is on the defendant to establish their case on the balance of probabilities. Under section 142 of the Evidence Act, the burden lies with individuals like those in *Chidiac v R (1991) 171 CLR 432* and *Bass v Holbrow [2011] TASMC 4 (21 April 2011)* to substantiate their defence claims effectively.

# Breath Analysis: Legal Responsibilities and Presumptions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: legal ramifications of breath analysis and presumptions] [CONCEPTS: breath analysis, liability, presumptions, testing time, relevant act] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m004-breath-analysis-law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: None]

Liability under section 8(1) of the Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act illustrates the officer's authority to pursue breath analysis once they harbor suspicion, which arguably represents a lower threshold than positive belief. The officer may conduct a roadside breath test to validate the presence of alcohol. Should the reading be negative, however, this liability dissipates as per section 8(8). Following breath analysis, the officer is obligated to complete a Form 2 and read it to the defendant.

Further, section 23(2) presumes the results of breath analysis are accurate at the time of testing unless proven otherwise on a balance of probabilities, positioning the defendant in the position of proving their argument. Section 23(4) delineates the timeframe wherein breath level is considered relevant and indicates that the "relevant act of driving" pertains explicitly to the conduct constituting the alleged offence.

# Case Commentary on Presumed Outcomes in Drink Driving Offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: commentary on presumptions in breath analysis results] [CONCEPTS: presumptions, alcohol concentration, burden of proof, Ausburn v Tasmania Police, Peterson v Mitchell] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m005-presumptions] [ACT: None] [CASE: Ausburn v Tasmania Police [2009] TASSC 21; Peterson v Mitchell [1972] Tas SR 202]

In *Ausburn v Tasmania Police [2009] TASSC 21*, the magistrate accepted that the applicant consumed alcohol post-driving but failed to establish, on the balance of probabilities, that their alcohol concentration at the time of driving was .05 or less. The primary intention of section 23(4) is to ease the proof burden in drink driving cases—without it, demonstrating an offence could necessitate presenting extensive evidence on what was consumed and expert opinions based on blood or breath analysis.

Additionally, in *Peterson v Mitchell [1972] Tas SR 202*, the Full Court emphasized that section 23(4) imposes a true burden on the defendant to prove that their alcohol concentration did not exceed limits. The language encompassed in section 23(4) clearly indicates that breath analysis results are presumed to represent the alcohol concentration at the specific moment of driving, thereby shaping the evidentiary landscape in prosecution cases concerning drink driving.

# Drink Driving Sentencing Submissions: Factors Considered
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: sentencing factors in drink driving offences] [CONCEPTS: sentencing, factors, prior offences, appreciation of risk, specific instructions] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m006-sentencing] [ACT: None] [CASE: McDonald v Nilsson [2009] TASSC 83]

In the context of sentencing for drink driving offences, prior convictions can significantly influence the court's decision. The case of *McDonald v Nilsson [2009] TASSC 83* illustrates that having seven prior offenses often leads to severe penalties, potentially including imprisonment. Courts may consider if an offender was aware they exceeded the prescribed limit; while some leniency may be applied in lower range offences, courts rarely accept claims of ignorance in high-range instances.

Factors the court considers include the manner of driving, whether there were passengers, connection to any accidents, distance travelled, and reasons for failing to adhere to prescribed limits. It is crucial to gather comprehensive instructions concerning the client's alcohol consumption and use prior to operation, as police will usually question defendants about their drinking habits post-positive breath tests.

# Special Circumstances in Drink Driving Offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: special circumstances in the context of sentencing] [CONCEPTS: special circumstances, principles, cases, principles applied, sentencing context] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m007-special-circumstances] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970] [CASE: Barratt v Thorpe [2020] TASMC 15; Strickland v Whitehead [1995] TASSC 125]

In considering the presence of special circumstances during sentencing in drink driving cases, the court refers to the principles outlined in various precedent-setting cases. In *Strickland v Whitehead [1995] TASSC 125*, it was elucidated that special circumstances must be extraordinary or unusual and not merely common to numerous breaches of the Act. In *Barratt v Thorpe [2020] TASMC 15*, relevant legal tenets on the nature of special circumstances were specifically highlighted.

Concepts that aid in determining whether circumstances can be classified as special include how the specifics relate collectively or concern the nature of the offence and offender alike. The court encourages a holistic approach rather than assessing each element in isolation, highlighting that frequent situations do not constitute special circumstances.

# Section 6(3A) Defence: Administrative Errors in Licensing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: administrative error defence in licensing issues] [CONCEPTS: administrative error, burden of proof, magistrate's discretion, court's finding, respondent's obligations] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m008-admin-error] [ACT: None] [CASE: Richardson, Roger v Pullen, Darren Arthur [1998] TASSC 167]

In the case of *Richardson, Roger v Pullen, Darren Arthur [1998] TASSC 167*, the necessity for a defendant to clearly satisfy the court regarding an administrative error emerged. The learned magistrate must carefully evaluate all evidence pertinent to whether the respondent would have retained their right to drive but for an unintentional failure to comply. It was noted that the magistrate did not thoroughly reference the evidentiary burden on the respondent or scrutinize facts sufficiently, revealing shortcomings that required attention.

# Challenges Associated with Home Breathalyser Equipment
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: efficacy and reliability of home breathalysers] [CONCEPTS: home breathalysers, reliability, evidentiary challenges, case precedent, professional judgment] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m009-home-breathalysers] [ACT: None] [CASE: F v Ling [1985] TASRp 10; [1985] Tas R 112]

The effectiveness of home breathalysers in establishing valid blood alcohol levels is called into significant question. In *F v Ling [1985] TASRp 10; [1985] Tas R 112*, the court determined that it is unreasonable to rely on such devices, thus challenging their admissibility as evidence in court.

# THC Cannabis Driving: Legal Considerations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: considerations for cannabis driving offences] [CONCEPTS: THC, cannabis driving, legal consequences, intoxication, prescribed limits] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m010-cannabis-driving] [ACT: None] [CASE: None]

In understanding the legal ramifications associated with THC and cannabis driving, it is imperative to evaluate intoxication levels and adherence to prescribed limits. Jurisprudential references highlight that the legal framework was designed with these concerns in mind, ensuring individuals are adequately prosecuted for violations.

# Costs of Analysis in Drink Driving Cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: analytical costs recoverable in drink driving offences] [CONCEPTS: analysis costs, legal provisions, recovery processes, evidential context, financial implications] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m011-costs-analysis] [ACT: None] [CASE: Tasmania Police v Kelly Jane Suitters [2022] TASMC 1 (28 January 2022)]

Costs incurred during the analysis processes in drink driving cases can be claimed against defendants, as outlined in *Tasmania Police v Kelly Jane Suitters [2022] TASMC 1 (28 January 2022)*. This establishes an important financial principle within the prosecutorial context, which highlights the implications of evidence evaluated during trials.

# Drink Pattern Defence Basics in Drink Driving Cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: requirements for establishing a drink pattern defence] [CONCEPTS: drink pattern defence, burden of proof, consumption replication, case law, evidential support] [CITATION: hoc-b034-m012-drink-pattern-defence] [ACT: None] [CASE: Payne v Crawford Unreported Judgement A/54 of 1992]

To establish a credible drink pattern defence that sufficiently displaces the statutory presumption under section 23, there needs to be careful replication of drinking behaviour in alignment with a defined timeline, as highlighted in the judgement of *Payne v Crawford Unreported Judgement A/54 of 1992*. Many defences falter due to inadequate proof regarding numbers of drinks consumed concerning the established timetable, leading to a lack of corroborative evidence compelling to the court.

### Hearing sequence 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Hearing Sequence - Witness Statements] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Witness evidence requirements] [CONCEPTS: witness statements, evidence requirements, intercept evidence, driving, motor vehicle, approved operator]

This chunk outlines the sequence of witness statements required during a hearing. 
1. Evidence of intercept/ driving a motor vehicle
2. Evidence of belief (positive OFT)
3. Evidence of an approved operator (oral)
4. Evidence OFA conducted (oral)
5. Form 5 -- doc read over and handed to defendant (oral and tender form 5)
6. Evidence of storage of samples (oral plus the storage register doc)

### Certificates 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: 29(1A), 27A(1), 27A(2), 28/28A] [CITATION: Certificate Requirements] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Requirements for certificates] [CONCEPTS: certificate of service, evidence, delivery, analysis]

This chunk discusses the requirements for various certificates necessary for the prosecution. 
1. Start with sub-section 29(1A) "certificate of service." Must prove that the defendant was served with certificates before they can be relied upon (disclosure not sufficient, but postage is -- per *Acts Interpretation Act*).
2. Section 27A(1): Certificate (taking and dealing) stating the person stated (approved Bas operator) took the sample at a day and time of the offender named, also that they were an approved operator.
3. Section 27A(2): "Certificate of delivery," by the person named, to the analyst with details regarding marks and labels.
4. Section 28/28A: "A certificate of analysis" that shows corresponding continuity information as to “delivery receipt.”

### Defences to OFA 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: 142] [CITATION: Defences to OFA - Onus on Defence] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Onus of proof in defences to OFA] [CONCEPTS: defence, balance of probabilities, case law, Chidiac, Bass v Holbrow]

This chunk outlines the onus on the defense in relation to offences of being under the influence of alcohol. 
The onus on the defence is on the balance of probabilities under section 142 of the Evidence Act. See case law: (Chidiac v R (1991) 171 CLR 432) and **Bass v Holbrow [2011] TASMC 4 (21 April 2011)**.

### Breath Analysis law 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: 8(1), 8(8), 11, 23(2), 23(4)] [CITATION: Breath Analysis Law Overview] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Breath analysis legal provisions] [CONCEPTS: breath analysis, law, responsibility, breath testing, alcohol level, liability]

This chunk provides clarity on breath analysis obligations and responsibilities under the law. 
Liability under section 8(1) states that if there is suspicion (arguably lower threshold than belief), a person is liable to breath analysis. An officer can choose to conduct a roadside breath test to confirm alcohol presence, but if a negative reading occurs, liability dissipates (s. 8(8)).
Section 11 states that after the breath analysis, the officer must hand a form 2 to the defendant and read it, informing them of the test's implications.
The presumption under section 23(2) is that the result of breath analysis is accurate at the time of testing unless proved otherwise on a balance of probabilities. 
Section 23(4) notes that the breath level of the defendant was present at any time within 6 hours following the "relevant time," defined under section 2A(2)(a) as the specific time of driving. 

### Presumptions 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: 23(4)] [CITATION: Presumption of Alcohol Concentration] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Presumptions and proof of drink driving] [CONCEPTS: presumptions, breath analysis, accuracy, time of testing, burden of proof]

This chunk discusses the presumptions regarding alcohol concentration in drink driving cases. 
**Ausburn v Tasmania Police [2009] TASSC 21 (6 April 2009)** 
14. The primary purpose of section 23(4) is to facilitate proof of drink driving offences. Proof of a motorist's alcohol concentration at the time of taking the breath or blood sample is often insufficient to prove the commission of an offence. Section 23(4) assists in proving that a driver's alcohol concentration exceeded the limit when driving, relieving magistrates from needing detailed findings in cases where defendants can't establish their innocence.

### Drink driving sentencing submissions 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Drink Driving Sentencing Considerations] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Sentencing factors for drink driving] [CONCEPTS: drink driving, sentencing, prior offences, awareness, court considerations]

This chunk outlines considerations for sentencing in drink driving cases. 
7 priors means prison! ***McDonald v Nilsson [2009] TASSC 83***
For lower-range offences, a court may accept that a person was unaware they were over the limit. In contrast, at high ranges, it is rare that a lack of awareness can be credibly asserted by someone who made the decision to drive under intoxication.
**The Manner and Reasons for Driving:**
- Whether there were any passengers. 
- Whether the incident resulted in an accident. 
- The distance and period of driving. 
- Location of driving. 
- Reasons for driving while over the prescribed limit. 
- Previous arrangements made regarding driving and outcomes of those plans. 
- Circumstances leading to apprehension by police.

### Special circumstances 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: 17(5)] [CITATION: Special Circumstances Criteria] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definition of special circumstances in sentencing] [CONCEPTS: special circumstances, sentencing criteria, case law, circumstances of the offence]

This chunk defines special circumstances in relation to sentencing for drink driving offences. 
In *Barratt v Thorpe [2020] TASMC 15 (14 May 2020)*.
23. In *Strickland v Whitehead [1995] TASSC 125,* Zeeman J discussed principles relating to special circumstances as follows:
1. It’s not possible to exhaustively enumerate circumstances that may constitute special.
2. The collective view is required for determining the existence of special circumstances.
3. These circumstances may relate both to the commission of the offence and the offender.
4. Common situations in drink driving offences generally do not constitute special circumstances.

### Section 6(3A) Defence 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: 6(3A)] [CITATION: Section 6(3A) Defence Ruling] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Ruling on administrative defence under section 6(3A)] [CONCEPTS: section 6(3A), administrative error, burden of proof, defence case]

This chunk discusses the onus under section 6(3A) of the Act. 
**Richardson, Roger v Pullen, Darren Arthur [1998] TASSC 167 (23 December 1998)**
*For the purposes of a defence under [s6(3A)](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/rsada1970264/s6.html), it was necessary for the magistrate to consider any relevant evidence and to determine whether the respondent had satisfied the court that, but for an unintentional failure to comply with an administrative requirement, he would have been licensed to drive. The magistrate did not do so. In his reasons, there was no expression of the evidence needed for establishing the respondent's defence.*

### Drink Pattern defence 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Drink Pattern Defence Overview] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Requirements for Drink Pattern Defence] [CONCEPTS: drink pattern defence, alcohol consumption, defence strategy]

This chunk discusses the establishment of a 'drink pattern' defence. 
It is generally accepted post-***Payne v Crawford*** that a defendant must replicate their drinking pattern according to a set timeline to effectively challenge the statutory presumption (S.23). Many 'drink pattern' defences fail due to defendants' inability to satisfy the court regarding the number of drinks consumed versus the established timeline.

### Traffic Act 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Traffic Act 1925 (Tas)] [SECTION: 32(2B)] [CITATION: Traffic Act GBH Definition] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Summary matters under traffic offences] [CONCEPTS: traffic act, GBH, summary matters, public safety]

This chunk summarizes aspects of the Traffic Act regarding grievous bodily harm related to driving offences. 
Section 32(2B) GBH from driving is a summary matter. In the High Court case *Schubert v Lee* [1946] HCA 28; [(1946) 71 CLR 589], it was stated that:
*"... The words 'open to or used by the public' are apt to describe a factual condition consisting of any real use of the place by the public as the public..."*

### Vehicle and Traffic Act 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Vehicle and Traffic Act 2013 (Tas)] [SECTION: 67] [CITATION: Evidentiary Certificates Overview] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Delegation of functions under s67] [CONCEPTS: evidentiary certificates, delegation, traffic act]

This chunk outlines provisions regarding the signing of certificates and delegation under the Vehicle and Traffic Act. 
Under section 67, a certificate can be signed by a delegate. The Registrar has the power under section 7 of the Act to delegate its functions.

### Questions to ask re suspended licence papers being sent 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Suspended Licence Inquiry Questions] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Questions regarding suspended licences] [CONCEPTS: suspended license, inquiry, evidence]

This chunk outlines essential questions to consider when addressing suspended license inquiries.
- You were driving?
- ID issues?
- Letterbox security?
- Issues with mail delivery to others?
- Location at time of delivery?
- Circumstances when notified?
- Comments made during notification?
- Actions taken after being intercepted? Delay in contact to MPES suggests prior knowledge of suspension.
- Nature of payment plan (if under one)?
- Compliance with the payment plan (if non-compliant, then not reasonable that they didn’t know).

### Requirement to get Tas licence after 6 months 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Vehicle and Traffic Act 2013 (Tas)] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Tas Licence Requirement] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Licensing requirement after 6 months] [CONCEPTS: Tasmanian licence, regulations, residency, exemption]

This chunk discusses licensing requirements for residents in Tasmania. 
Section 8(1) of the Vehicle and Traffic Act states that there is a requirement to hold a Tas licence. Section 8(2) states that they are exempt from subsection (1) if they hold a licence from elsewhere. Recourse then to Regulation 49 of the *Vehicle and Traffic (Driver Licensing and Vehicle Registration) Regulations 2021* which states that the exemption lapses after 3 months of residency in Tasmania.

### Requirement to get Tas Registration after 3 months 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Vehicle and Traffic Act 2013 (Tas)] [SECTION: 28] [CITATION: Tas Registration Requirement] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Registration requirement after 3 months] [CONCEPTS: vehicle registration, exemptions, laws, residency]

This chunk discusses requirements for vehicle registration in Tasmania. 
Section 28 of the Vehicle and Traffic Act states that there is an exception from registration when registered under another law. Regulation 129 of the *Vehicle and Traffic (Driver Licensing and Vehicle Registration) Regulations 2021* states that the exemption lapses once in Tasmania for 3 months unless a person has a residential address or ordinarily resides interstate.

### Drive unlicensed 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas)] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Drive Unlicensed Case Reference] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal implications of driving without a license] [CONCEPTS: unlicensed driving, legal implications, case law]

This chunk outlines the legal implications surrounding the issue of unlicensed driving. 
Interstate licences are only valid for 6 months before a Tasmanian licence must be obtained. 
Strict liability offence **Richardson, Roger v Pullen, Darren Arthur [1998] TASSC 167 (23 December 1998)**. 
**Sentencing:** 
Not an insignificant offence. The requirement to possess a licence is not just for revenue purposes but is aimed at public safety, as noted in ***Lusted v Saunders [2012] TASSC 6***.

### Unregistered 
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Vehicle and Traffic Act 2013 (Tas)] [SECTION: 69] [CITATION: Unregistered Vehicle Regulations] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Legal definition of unregistered vehicles] [CONCEPTS: unregistered vehicles, legal definitions, traffic regulations]

This chunk discusses the legal implications of driving an unregistered vehicle. 
If no plate is displayed, then the vehicle is deemed unregistered (s. 69 Vehicle and Traffic Act).

# Speeding – Officer Evidence in Speeding Cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Officer evidence requirements in speeding cases] [CONCEPTS: speeding, evidence, traffic signs, approved device, police training] [CITATION: hoc-b035-m001-speeding-officer-evidence] [ACT: Traffic Act 1925] [CASE: Wheaton v Wilson [2017] TASSC 40]

In any speeding case, the police officer's evidence must establish certain elements to support the charge effectively. The following points should be addressed:

1. Identification of the driver.

2. The speed zone -- was it a built-up area or were there speed signs?

3. Do the speed signs comply with the legislation in size, and how were they displayed? 

**See section 51 of the Traffic Act -- averment in a complaint is evidence.**

4. Was the device used approved? (see regulation 6 of the *Vehicle and Traffic (Offence Detection Device) Regulations* 2012)

5. Was the device tested within the last 12 months? (see regulation 8 ibid.)

6. Has it been repaired, altered, or adjusted? (see regulation 8 ibid.)

7. Was the device sealed so its speed computing circuitry cannot be interfered with? (see regulation 8 ibid.)

8. Do we have proof of the device having been tested in the last 12 months? (see regulation 9 ibid.) Tender the regulation 10 certificate.

9. Was the police officer suitably trained and/or experienced in the use of the device? See *Wheaton v Wilson* \[2017\] TASSC 40.

10. The police officer must provide evidence of the device's serial number, ensuring it matches the serial number/s on the certificate.

Additionally, obtaining evidence of the morning testing activities the police undertake when they set up can be crucial. This includes obtaining information such as:

- Name, rank, and current posting of the officer.
- Duration of service with Tasmania Police and specifics of roles on the relevant day.
- Identification and the designated speed limit of the area.
- The recorded speed from the device and the driver's identity confirmation.

<!-- block_035 procedure -->

### Speeding Evidence Collection Workflow
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Traffic Act 1925] [SECTION: s 51] [CITATION: Traffic Act 1925 s 51 - Evidence Collection Workflow] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Step-by-step workflow for determining evidence of speeding by an officer] [CONCEPTS: speeding, evidence gathering, police procedures, traffic laws, enforcement]

Tactical workflow for determining evidence of speeding by an officer.

1. Identification of the driver.
2. The speed zone -- was it a built-up area or were there speed signs?
3. Do the speed signs comply with the legislation in size, and how displayed?
4. Was the device used approved? (see regulation 6 of the *Vehicle and Traffic (Offence Detection Device) Regulations* 2012)
5. Was the device tested within the last 12 months? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
6. Has it been repaired, altered or adjusted? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
7. Was the device sealed so its speed computing circuitry cannot be interfered with? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
8. Do we have proof of the device having been tested in the last 12 months? (see regulation 9 ibid.) Tender the regulation 10 certificate.
9. Was the police officer suitably trained and/or experienced in the use of the device? See *Wheaton v Wilson* [2017] TASSC 40.
10. Make sure the police officer gives evidence of the serial number of the device that matches the serial number/s on the certificate.

### Proving Speedometer Accuracy
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: Allen v Broome [2003] TASSC 38 - Speedometer Accuracy] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Case authority for speedometer accuracy as prima facie evidence] [CONCEPTS: speedometer accuracy, prima facie evidence, burden of proof, technical instruments, legal presumptions]

"There is a common law presumption as to the accuracy of readings of scientific or technical instruments. A speedometer is such an instrument."

### Prosecution Points of Proof for Speed Devices
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Vehicle and Traffic (Offence Detection Device) Regulations 2012] [SECTION: Various] [CITATION: Vehicle and Traffic (Offence Detection Device) Regulations 2012 - Prosecution Points] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Checklist for prosecution points of proof for radar and laser devices] [CONCEPTS: radar devices, laser devices, traffic enforcement, evidence checklist, prosecution strategy]

Prosecution points of proof for Radar and Laser devices:

1. Identification of the driver.
2. The speed zone -- was it a built-up area or were there speed signs?
3. Do the speed signs comply with the legislation in size? How were they displayed? Were they clearly visible? Describe the signs.
4. Was the device used the approved type? (see regulation 6 of the *Vehicle and Traffic (Offence Detection Device) Regulations* 2012).
5. Was the device tested within the last 12 months? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
6. Has it been repaired, altered or adjusted? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
7. Was the device sealed? (see regulation 8 ibid.)
8. Do we have proof of the device having been tested in the last 12 months? (see regulation 9 ibid.) Tender the regulation 10 certificate.
9. Was the police officer trained/experienced in the use of the device? See *Wheaton v Wilson* [2017] TASSC 40.

### Scripted Examination Questions for Police Officers
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: N/A] [SECTION: N/A] [CITATION: N/A - Police Evidence Questions] [CATEGORY: script] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: Scripted examination questions for police officers regarding speeding] [CONCEPTS: police interview, scripted questions, examination, traffic enforcement, legal procedures]

Get out evidence of the morning testing activities the cops do when they set up:

- Name, rank and current posting.  
- How long have you served with Tasmania Police?  
- In what role are you currently serving?  
- What were your specific duties on that day?  
- Was anyone tasked to assist you?  
- What training have you had that made you an appropriate candidate for such duties?  
- How much experience had you had in that sort of role?  
- Where were you positioned?  
- What was the designated speed limit of that area of road?  
- How did you know that was the applicable speed-limit?  
- Can you describe the vehicle?  
- Can you remember the registration number?  
- Can you describe what made it come to your attention?  
- Was an estimate made of its speed?  
- Believing it to be in excess of the applicable speed-limit, what action did you take?  
- What speed did the device record?  
- What was the devices serial number?  
- What action did you take?  
- Did you speak with the driver?  
- How would you describe the driver's demeanor?  
- Did the driver identify himself?  
- Was his identity confirmed?  
- Have you much experience using a radar speed analyzer?  
- Can you explain the process of using the device?  
- Was the functionality of the device tested?  
- Tender certificate.

# Dangerous Article Definitions and Possession
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: definition of dangerous article] [CONCEPTS: dangerous article, possession, belief, weapon, lawful excuse] [CITATION: hoc-b036-m001-dangerous-article-definitions] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [CASE: Momcilovic v The Queen; Tasmanian v Hodges; Craig v Lowrey]

*Police Offences Act 1935 - Section 15C -- Dangerous Article*  
*Police Offences Act -- Section 3 -- Interpretation*

*dangerous article* includes --

(a) any weapon other than a firearm; and

(b) any article adapted or modified so as to be capable of being used as such a weapon; and

(c) any other article that is intended to be used as such a weapon; and

(d) a knife

#### Possession:

Not defined in POA. "possession" ordinary means "physical custody or control" ***Momcilovic V the Queen 2011 HCA 34***

#### Requisite belief:

Police officer must believe that the defendant is in possession or carries a dangerous article HELD, in the circumstances, to be based on objectively reasonable grounds.

See ***Tasmanian v Hodges 2022 TASSC***. Belief is something more than mere suspicion but less than actual knowledge based on proof. There must be grounds for their belief but certainty and proof are not required.

#### 'Dangerous' and 'Dual Purpose'

***Craig v Lowrey* \[2012\] TASMC 41**

The Oxford Dictionary defines "weapon" as "a thing designed for use for inflicting bodily harm or physical damage".

Turning firstly to the three inclusive definitions set out in section 3, it is probably the case that I would find that the machete is a weapon, despite its dual purpose. Its purpose as a weapon worldwide is well known. Terrible injuries and death have flowed from machete attacks. The blade is known to be long, strong and sharp.

However authorities have held that where there is a **dual purpose**, prosecution is required to prove the intended use as a weapon. See *Chadbourne v Ansell* \[1975\] WAR 104, *Threfall v Panzera* \[1958\] VicRp 87; \[1958\] VR 547, *Coleman v Zanker* \[1991\] SASC 3121; (1992) 58 SASR 7. However these cases deal with different statutory provisions relating to "offensive weapons". This appears to be a higher test than required for part (b) of the definition in this state's Act, where an item is modified so that it is only *capable* of being a weapon. This interpretation would also seem to defeat the work of the "lawful excuse" provision. I do not need to decide this point. The definition is an inclusive one, and therefore the overriding requirement is that the court must be satisfied that it is "a dangerous article" independently of whether or not it falls into the category of a "weapon". This would at face value cover a wider array of items.

The Macquarie dictionary defines "dangerous" as "full of danger or risk; causing danger; perilous; hazardous; unsafe". The Oxford dictionary defines dangerous as "able or likely to cause harm or injury".

# Lawful Excuse in Relation to Dangerous Articles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: doctrine] [TYPE: doctrine] [TOPIC: lawful excuse for possession of dangerous articles] [CONCEPTS: lawful excuse, dangerous article, recreation, intent, High Court] [CITATION: hoc-b036-m002-lawful-excuse-dangerous-articles] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [CASE: Craig v Lowrey; Taikato v R; Holmes v Hatton]

#### Dangerous Article (Lawful Excuse)

***Craig v Lowrey* \[2012\] TASMC 41**

The overall effect of the defendant's explanations was that he kept the machete for recreational or farming purposes unrelated to attack or self-defence.

In *Taikato v R* \[1996\] HCA 28, the majority of the High Court in relation to a similar charge discussed the meaning of "lawful purpose". They held that as a general rule interpreting "lawful purpose" in a legislative provision to mean a purpose that is not forbidden, rather than positively authorised by law, is usually preferred. This is because statutes are interpreted in accordance with the presumption that parliament does not take away existing rights unless it does so expressly or by necessary implication. In this matter it was not argued by prosecution that using the machete for the purposes of a tool, as the defendant describes, was in itself unlawful. It is not prohibited by law, and I find that such use would be a lawful activity.

Two decisions of the South Australian Supreme Court assist with the principles to be applied in proving "lawful excuse" in the context of this type of charge. I will set out relevant passages from those decisions.

In *Holmes v Hatton* \[18 SASR 412\], Wells J stated at p418:

It seems to me that the expression "without lawful excuse" obliges a Court to examine all the circumstances that characterize the purpose and reasons for which the weapon is carried, and to determine, as a matter of fact and degree whether, reasonably appraised, those purposes and reasons constitute lawful excuse or not.

His Honour further stated as p419:

> *Where an offensive weapon is carried knowingly, lawful excuse or its absence is ordinarily to be judged by a consideration of the purpose or purposes, or the reason or reasons, for which the weapon is being carried. (If it should be -- and I leave this point for consideration when it directly arises -- that a man may "carry" a weapon within the meaning of the section even where he is unaware that he is carrying it -- for example, where he is helping to carry another's luggage which, unknown to him contains a gun -- absence of purpose or reason and an inadvertent carrying may amount to lawful excuse).*
>
> *The purpose or reasons for which an alleged offender was carrying a weapon are to be examined and assessed having regard to the united force of all the circumstances put together. If the sole purpose or reason for which the weapon is carried is plainly unlawful or is otherwise contrary to the section, the carrier cannot claim lawful excuse.*
>
> *Where the sole purpose or reason is lawful, there will be lawful excuse. Where there is a duality of purpose or reason, no hard and fast rule can provide a solution. The court, in determining the issue, must, in my opinion, decide a question of fact and degree, and must, in particular, ask whether that which, ex facie and taken in isolation, may be regarded as an unlawful purpose or reason, was, in the circumstances, so transient, so remotely related to the defendant's conduct, so speculative, so unlikely to become translated into action, so subsidiary -- in short, so unimportant or insignificant, as to leave the alleged offender with lawful excuse.*
>
> *The foregoing discussion, it will be observed, has omitted any reference to onus of proof. The application of the provision as to onus of proof should cause little difficulty in practice, but occasional cases, in which dual purpose is under examination, may arise where it will be necessary to keep steadily in mind what the defendant is obliged by [s. 15](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/s15.html) to prove.*
>
> *Plainly, given that all other elements are proved, if only a single purpose or reason is in issue, and if the defendant proves on the balance of probabilities primary facts that, in law, amount to lawful excuse, he will be acquitted. If proof falls short of that, he must be convicted. If the evidence as a whole raises a question of dual purpose or reason, the matter will, to my mind, stand thus. Where the evidence as a whole includes evidence suggesting an unlawful purpose or reason for carrying as well as of a lawful purpose or reason, in order to acquit the defendant the court must find itself persuaded that, having regard to the cogency with which the relevant facts and circumstance are proved by the evidence as a whole, the unlawful purpose or reason was so unimportant and insignificant that there probably was lawful excuse.*

### Dangerous Article – Definition and Interpretation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 15C] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Dangerous Article Definition] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of Dangerous Article] [CONCEPTS: dangerous article, definition, weapon, adaptation, possession]

*dangerous article* includes --

(a) any weapon other than a firearm; and

(b) any article adapted or modified so as to be capable of being used as such a weapon; and

(c) any other article that is intended to be used as such a weapon; and

(d) a knife.

### Possession – Legal Meaning
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not defined] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Definition of Possession] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of Possession] [CONCEPTS: possession, legal meaning, definition, custody, control]

### Requisite Belief – Objective Reasonable Grounds
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Requisite Belief] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Requisite Belief for Dangerous Articles] [CONCEPTS: police belief, possession, reasonable grounds, objective, suspicion]

Police officer must believe that the defendant is in possession or carries a dangerous article HELD, in the circumstances, to be based on objectively reasonable grounds.

See ***Tasmanian v Hodges 2022 TASSC***. Belief is something more than mere suspicion but less than actual knowledge based on proof. There must be grounds for their belief but certainty and proof are not required.

### Definitions of 'Dangerous' and 'Dual Purpose'
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specifically cited] [CITATION: Craig v Lowrey [2012] TASMC 41 - Definition of Dangerous and Dual Purpose] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Interpretation of 'Dangerous' and 'Dual Purpose'] [CONCEPTS: dangerous, dual purpose, weapon, machete, interpretation]

12. The Oxford Dictionary defines "weapon" as "a thing designed for use for inflicting bodily harm or physical damage".

13. Turning firstly to the three inclusive definitions set out in section 3, it is probably the case that I would find that the machete is a weapon, despite its dual purpose. Its purpose as a weapon worldwide is well known. Terrible injuries and death have flowed from machete attacks. The blade is known to be long, strong and sharp.

14. However authorities have held that where there is a **dual purpose**, prosecution is required to prove the intended use as a weapon. See *Chadbourne v Ansell [[1975] WAR 104]*, *Threfall v Panzera [[1958] VicRp 87]*; [[1958] VR 547], *Coleman v Zanker [[1991] SASC 3121]*; [(1992) 58 SASR 7]. However, these cases deal with different statutory provisions relating to "offensive weapons". This appears to be a higher test than required for part (b) of the definition in this state's Act, where an item is modified so that it is only *capable* of being a weapon. This interpretation would also seem to defeat the work of the "lawful excuse" provision. The definition is an inclusive one, and therefore the overriding requirement is that the court must be satisfied that it is "a dangerous article" independently of whether or not it falls into the category of a "weapon". This would at face value cover a wider array of items.

15. The Macquarie dictionary defines "dangerous" as "full of danger or risk; causing danger; perilous; hazardous; unsafe". The Oxford dictionary defines dangerous as "able or likely to cause harm or injury".

### Legal Context of Dangerous Article (Lawful Excuse)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Craig v Lowrey [2012] TASMC 41 - Dangerous Article (Lawful Excuse)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Context of Lawful Excuse for Dangerous Article] [CONCEPTS: lawful excuse, context, machete, recreational use, legal principle]

20. The overall effect of the defendant's explanations was that he kept the machete for recreational or farming purposes unrelated to attack or self-defence.

21. In *Taikato v R* [[1996] HCA 28], the majority of the High Court in relation to a similar charge discussed the meaning of "lawful purpose". They held that as a general rule interpreting "lawful purpose" in a legislative provision to mean a purpose that is not forbidden, rather than positively authorised by law, is usually preferred. This is because statutes are interpreted in accordance with the presumption that parliament does not take away existing rights, unless it does so expressly or by necessary implication. In this matter it was not argued by prosecution that using the machete for the purposes of a tool, as the defendant describes, was in itself unlawful. It is not prohibited by law and I find that such use would be a lawful activity.

22. Two decisions of the South Australian Supreme Court assist with the principles to be applied in proving "lawful excuse" in the context of this type of charge. I will set out relevant passages from those decisions.

### Prescribed Offences and Regulations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37K] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Prescribed Offences] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Prescribed Offences Defined] [CONCEPTS: prescribed offences, regulations, vehicle and traffic act, speeding offences, police powers]

Prescribed offences are prescribed and can be found in Regulation 6 of the *Police Offences Regulations* 2014:

[Section 13](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/tas/consol_act/vata1999150/s13.html) of the [Vehicle and Traffic Act 1999](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/tas/consol_act/vata1999150/) (drive disqualified) or [section 19A](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/tas/consol_act/rsada1970264/s19a.html) of the [Road Safety (Alcohol and Drugs) Act 1970](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/tas/consol_act/rsada1970264/) (drive was disqualified) so long as it is a subsequent offence for either. And a speeding offence above 45kmh.

Further prescribed offences in s 37K of the ***POA*** include: 

**14B. Unlawful entry on land involving use of vehicle.** 

**15B. Dispersal of persons involving use of vehicle.** 

**37J. Excessive noise, smoke, &c., from vehicles.**

**48. No motor-vehicle race to be held without a permit.**

**32. Reckless driving TRAFFIC ACT 1925.**

**11A. Evading police POLICE POWERS (VEHICLE INTERCEPTION) ACT 2000.**

### Power to Clamp and Confiscate Vehicles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37N] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Power to Clamp Vehicles] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Power to Clamp and Confiscate Vehicles] [CONCEPTS: clamping, confiscating, prescribed offences, vehicles, legal authority]

Power to do so where there is a prescribed offence or UP TO 14 days after the offending (POLICE OFFENCES ACT 1935 - SECT 37N).

### Mandatory Penalty for Prescribed Offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37MA] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Mandatory Penalty for Offences] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Mandatory Penalty Related to Offences] [CONCEPTS: mandatory penalty, special penalty, clamped vehicles, regulation, sentencing act]

Mandatory penalty for prescribed offences including a vehicle that are "clamped or confiscated" are subject to a "Special Penalty" (s 37MA POA). There is no discretion and it is above any other penalty found in the ***Sentencing Act***.

The amount of the special penalty is found in Regulation 7 of the *Police Offences Regulations* 2014 **[OR]{.underline}** 2024 Subject to the date.

Offending post 12 December 2024 (when the new regs came in) is charged as 4 penalty units.

Offending pre that is \$600.

### Motor Forfeiture Application Process
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37Y] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Motor Forfeiture Application] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Process for Motor Forfeiture Applications] [CONCEPTS: motor forfeiture, third offence, police offences act, relevant considerations, court process]

Where third offence – application can be made (POLICE OFFENCES ACT 1935 - SECT 37Y).

Look at *Perkins v Douglas* \[2010\] TASMC 8 for relevant considerations of the court.

When preparing for a hearing, get certs of convictions for relevant prescribed offending. Get FFP for this so def can be cross examined on previous driving history in support of the position that the vehicle ought not be returned. Confirm the penalty that was imposed on the conviction that led to the conviction. Not the circumstances of the relevant offending that led to confiscation. The more basic, the less likely that forfeiture will be granted.

If application made by the owner (not offender) consider their knowledge and acquiescence to letting the offender use the car. Did they know that the defendant was unlicensed/unregistered etc.? Is there a history or pattern of the owner allowing the defendant to use the car? Did they know they were offending/likely to offend?

### Legal Reference for Excessive Noise from Vehicles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37J] [CITATION: Lane v Kelly [2010] TASMC 20 - Excessive Noise] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Legal Reference for Excessive Noise from Vehicles] [CONCEPTS: excessive noise, vehicles, case authority, legal reference, police offences]

### Motor Vehicle Stealing – Legal Specifications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: 37B] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 - Motor Vehicle Stealing Definition] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of Motor Vehicle Stealing] [CONCEPTS: motor vehicle stealing, legal specifications, consent, mental element, police offences act]

If serious can be "made a code offence" 37(4)(b).

12 month limitation:

- drives or [uses]{.mark}

- a motor vehicle

- without the consent of the owner or registered operator of the vehicle or of some person who is lawfully in charge of the vehicle and has authority to give that consent

- mental element: def must have subjective knowledge that consent has not been given.

***Willox v Warren \[1987\] TASSC 74***:

*S. 37B of the Police Offences Act 1935 provides:--*

*"37B--(1) Subject to subsection (2), a person who drives or uses a motor vehicle without the consent of the owner of the vehicle or of some person who is lawfully in charge of the vehicle and has authority to give that consent is guilty of the offence of motor vehicle stealing."*

*(2) Subsection (1) does not apply to or in relation to the driving or use of a motor vehicle by a police officer or an authorized officer acting in the execution of his duty."*

*The essential external elements of this offence are, first, that the defendant drove or used a motor vehicle and, second, that he did so without the consent of the owner or person lawfully in charge of it. To these should be added a mental element, namely that at the time he did so he knew that the owner or person lawfully in charge of it had not given his consent to such use (R. v. Murphy \[1957\] VicRp 75; \[1957\] V.R. 545; Wray v. Robertson \[1970\] Tas.S.R. 253). Stealing, on the other hand, requires a dishonest taking or conversion with intent to permanently deprive the owner of the thing stolen. [Although the summary offence is described as "stealing", it has that clear generic difference to stealing as defined under the Criminal Code and at common law. It has frequently been said that the offence was introduced to prevent "joy riding"]{.mark} (see Wray v. Robertson (supra) at p.255 and cases there cited).*

### False Statement Requirements

### Willful Obstruction of Police – Legal Precedent
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Liley v Lowe [1987] TASSC 76 - Willful Obstruction of Police] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Willful Obstruction of Police Defined] [CONCEPTS: willful obstruction, police officers, legal precedent, lawful direction, obstruction]

Must be an intent to frustrate as opposed to help. Refusing a direction given amounts to obstruct where the police officer is trying to arrest or carry out other lawful duty: **Liley v Lowe \[1987\] TASSC 76 (20 March 1987)**.

### Distinctions Between Stealing, Robbery, and Burglary
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Distinctions Between Offences - Stealing, Robbery, Burglary] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Differences Between Stealing and Other Offences] [CONCEPTS: stealing, robbery, burglary, definitions, distinctions]

**Quick differences**:

**Stealing** is when you take something, robbery is when you take with a threat or actual violence, burglary is trespass (need not take) in any place with intent to commit crime (need not be stealing), aggravated burglary is when you trespass with weapon OR in a place ordinarily used for human habitation OR uses violence or assaults while burglarizing.

**Stealing:**

Take anything capable of being taken with the intent to permanently deprive.

***Take*** includes trick, intimidation, mistake of the owner (if known by the offender), or by finding (if the owner can reasonably be discovered).

### Aggravated Burglary Description
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Burglary with Aggravation - Definition] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of Aggravated Burglary] [CONCEPTS: aggravated burglary, definition, weapon, unlawful entry, violence]

**Aggravated Burglary:** 

Burglary, committed with a weapon; committed with the use or threat of violence **Or** where the trespass is into a place used normally for human habitation.

### Stealing by Finding Definition
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: Not specified] [CITATION: Stealing by Finding - Definition] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of Stealing by Finding] [CONCEPTS: stealing by finding, definition, legal understanding, possession, ownership]

Stealing by finding.
